THE RED PAGE.
THE RT OF PHIL MAY
“ I'\KA)V firm ! and be jolly ! ” said W. M.
| 1 Hunt to a'class. His beatitudes were
-J J' May’s aptitudes. Decisive pen-line,
sunny temperament : May owed them
to inherited nervous organisation. The essentials
of his career were assured before he was born.
Yet of his parentage we know scarcely anything.
For one who talked so freely, May was singularly
reticent about himself. That the household was
straitened, that the father died early, that the boy
was thrown on the streets for a livelihood ; we have
heard little more than this. Of his mother, May
seems never to have spoken. Yet her influence
must have been great, possibly in the end para-
mount.
Gifted by birth, May was doubly gifted by
circumstance. “What was the chief factor of
your success ? ” J. E. Eraser asked an American
millionaire. “My poverty.” Poverty, mother
of heroes ! May became a fine draughtsman
simply because he had to. It was “Draw, Phil,
or die !”, In those early days at Leeds, and later
in London, May lived by his. art, not for it.
Often his dinner must have depended upon his
talent for catching a likeness. He was in the
position of a dog that is compelled to beg for a
biscuit. May got the biscuit, and later found that
he had trained nerve and muscle rarely.
Hunger sharpened his eye for form. Hunger
Eerfected the co-ordination of brain and
and. One may ponder what May would have
become had his natural capacity been fostered in
a comfortable home, through the routine of the
art schools. Ponder—and conclude that, though
his work might have covered a broader field, it
could never have risen to the height it holds in
his own particular field. His temperament would
have undone him. He had to be spurred to draw :
he needed no spur to jollity. “If I had a son,’’
he said, “ I would not like him to go through the
struggle that I have had.” But it was to that
struggle he owed his success; he was forced to
succeed, half despite himself. His bitter boyhood
achieved him.
It is not necessary to believe all that May told
interviewers about his methods and his work.
Remember that he had posed before the foot-
lights, was acquainted with all the shifts and
devices of the stage. Hardship had made him
shrewd : he knew the value of a mask in public.
Often he worked laboriously, studied life care-
fully. Often, toward the end, he trusted memory
and photographs, and with his own skill and an
actor’s passed tinsel for profound.
The “leaving-out method” was doubtless based
on a genuine principle, deliberately adopted. In
Gutter-Snipes this method is manifested admirably.
But once the public had been taught to believe
that the fewer the lines, the greater the talent,
one suspects that May, with tongue in cheek,
would minimise his labour to little more than
those few lines. Some of the drawings in the
Annual warrant the suspicion. When- he worked
in a hurry (and he was in a hurry often) May was
not too scrupulous.
May looked old when he was young, and he •
remained young when he was old. He was boy
for ever, in spite of wrinkles, in spite of experi-
ence and fame. In Sydney, in London, his heart
and brain changed little from the heart and brain
of the youngster in Leeds. The pictures he saw
most clearly and set down most effectively were
the pictures that recalled his childish days—pic-
tures of street character, street children. His art
was a tool to express anything ; but his brain lived
and died with eager, cheerful, irresponsible
urchins in a city slum.
May wished to go on the stage. He wished to
be a painter. Capacity "to paint, to act, he had
possibly; but the time to train his capacity had
gone by. The technic of pen-drawing he had
mastered almost to perfection : for the technic of
acting, of painting, he needed a new youth, new
energies, a fresh apprenticeship to Necessity.
With another bent, May might have become a
great painter. But his bent had been taken
decisively: he remains a brilliant draughtsman,
an illustrator with unsurpassed talent for depict-
ing certain people in certain scenes.
d*
May’s art was essentially athletic. Herkomer
meant this when he said that May’s line was like
the stroke of Joachim’s bow. A good drawing by
May is a tour de force. What his eye clearly saw,
his hand accurately expressed, in so far as a black-
on-white line can express objects. The mechanics
of the feat, the correspondence between aim and
realisation, are so absolute that we receive a
pleasure akin with that given by a wonderful
stroke at billiards, or a clean somersault in the
circus. There is in May’s drawings an intellectual
beauty of precision and harmony that is the basis
of their artistic merit. Many inferior draughts-
men have become memorable artists. But May,
lacking his draughtsmanship, would have counted
for little. We prize him chiefly because he could
give to a single line individual power, par-
ticular charm.
In this quality of athletic skill, May never sur-
passed some of the drawings contributed to The
Bulletin. His eye, his hand, and his brain in
so far as it controlled eye and hand, were
never better adjusted than when he lived in
Australia. Here he exhibited all his wonderful dex-
terity at its height. Later he gained in suavity
and humanity at some expense of brilliancy. Gain
and loss were the natural consequence of age: as
May’s brain grew fuller in understanding, its
means of expression grew less sure. Possibly
gain and loss were to some extent deliberate;
for all art is a compromise. The poet resigns the
just word for the sake of a poem; the artist
resigns the just line for the sake of a picture.
The absolute expression of such a skill as May’s
seems always a little hard, a little cold. One
gains softness and emotion by conceding.
To a certain extent May’s vision was superficial,
his range limited. His art is neither very broad
nor very deep. Artist he was, yet his athletic
faculty was not enforced by the highest artistic
consciousness. Loosely to phrase it, if eye
and hand were an artist’s, the brain was a
gamin’s. Life gave May a series of little emotional
fillips. These he transfers to his page with a bare
suggestion of the character behind the image, the
meaning beneath the scene. He never generalised;
it is an accident if some of his figures are types.
It was less humanity than the appearances of
humanity that he portrayed. One could almost
say that his impression of objects went no deeper
than his eye. But his hand was miraculous.
d*
He drew firm ; he was jolly.
RECENT READING
Our N.S.W. Birth-rate Commission (some of
the members have no answer to “Physician, heal
thyself ! ”) can read Margaret Bisland’s article,
“ The Curse of Eve,” in July North Amer. Review.
The lady discusses the decline of the U.S.A.
birth-rate—
This failure in natural and wholesome increase among
our white natives is due to nothing more or less than the
over-education and abnormal public activities so ardently
encouraged among our women since the close of the
Civil War. We take too slight an account of this when
we wonder uneasily at our inability to plant an individual,
self-sustaining, indigenous people in the United States.
We forget, or refuse to realise, what is inevitably to
follow as the result of a false equality permitted between
the sexes ; and we have not rightly interpreted or sus-
tained that masculine instinct, old as the race itself,
which regards as an unmixed evil any emancipation of
the woman that excites her to effort and attainment
beyond the bounds of domesticity.
The denial to woman of an equal share in man’s intel-
lectual and physical career is not, as the near-sighted
advocates of feminine enfranchisement would have us
believe, a useless relic of barbarism and savagery. It is
not an indication of mere male covetousness, selfishness,
and blind prejudice, upheld and exercised through ages
by force of sheer physical superiority, and serving now
as a stumbling-block in the path of beneficent progress.
True enough, perhaps, it is a tradition inherited from
our barbaric and Asiatic ancestors ; nevertheless, it flows'
from an ancient and profound realisation of and respect
for an inexorable law of nature—a law that never fails to
deprive intellectually developed woman of her fecundity.
It flows also from a knowledge, gained through the
tragedy of experience, that only in the domestic shelter
does civilised humanity find the environment congenial
to reproductivity and proper development.
Margaret adds: “ WithouHier motherhood woman
has nothing of importance to contribute to the
work of human elevation. Indeed, after a certain
point, the non-domestic and childless woman is a
menace to social purity and to national stability.”
That is so. Only by fecuridity woman becomes
useful to the race. That she may be racially use-
ful, she must be secluded in a harem or its modern
equivalent of the home, and kept off the grass
of cerebral excitement.
d*
M. B. Synge tells ‘ ‘ the story of the world for
the children of the British empire.” Sis books
are conceived as a series of dramatic passages
taken from the legend of tradition and of history,
simply and effectively related. The three volumes
issued — On the Shores of the Great Sea ; The Dis-
covery of New Worlds ) The Awakening of Europe
—rank well upon the list of available school-,
books. Asa universal history for children their
scope is limited, their method conventional.
World-history to-day should not begin with,
Hebrew myths, given disproportionate importanoe
And, without insisting on the doubtful point
whether a history for children should contain
ethical valuations of deeds and epochs in relation
to human progress, one thinks that M. B. Synge
should have devoted less space to war and more
to social conditions. The type of sis volumes is
good, but the illustrations are scanty. History
for children should be pictured on every page.
d*
The King business is like a poker game. It’s
been goin’ on f’r a long time, an’ whiniver it puts its
money in we lay down, thinkin’ we was up again’ a hand-
ful iv kings an’ queens. But th’ minyit they’se a show-
down th’bluff is over. Thin we see that th’hand that
we were afraid iv is composed intirely iv sivins, sixes an’
dooces, with maybe wan jack that looks like a king on’y
to near-sighted people. A show-down is death to rilety.
Whin I was a boy, if a king fell out with his folks, no
wan knew iv it but th’ earls an’ markesses an’ jooks that
overheerd th’ row while they were waitin’on th’table.
They didn’t say anything, but wrote it down in a note-
book an’ published it afther they was dead. Whin th
king passed th’ butther plate so high to his wife that it
caught her in th’ eye, it was a rile secret. When his rile
spouse pulled his majesty around th’ room be th’ hair iv
th’ head, th’ tale remained in th’ fam’ly till it got into
history. Whin wan iv th’ princesses threatened to skip
with a jook, th’ king touched th’ spring iv th’ thrapdure,
an’ her rile highness, Augoostina Climintina Sofia Maria
Mary Ann, wint down among th’ coals an’ th’ potatoes
an’ niver was heard iv again.
But nowadays ’tis diff’rent. Th’ window shades is up
at th’ king’s house as well as ivrywhere else. Th’ gas is
lighted, an’ we see his majesty stormin’ around because
th’ dinner is late, kickin th’ rile dog, whalin’ th’ princes
iv th’ blood with a lath, brushin’ his crown befure goin’
out, shavin’ his chin, sneakin’ a dlirink at bed-time,
jawin’ his wife, an’ makin’ faces at his daughter.—F. P.
Dunne.
•
John Masefield, writing of “The Sea and
Sailors ” in Lond. Academy of 25th July, Says that “ The
only poet who has seen the sailor in the dingy intimacy
of his fo’c’s’le, and sung him in verse which is more
clarion than doggerel, is Mr. E. J. Brady, an Australian,
whose book of poems, The Ways of Many Waters, was
published in Sydney a few years ago. Mr. Brady writes
in ballad-metres, much as Adam Gordon wrote, and
Kingsley and Macaulay before him. He writes movingly
and convincingly without putting an undue accent upon
that ‘ plea for escape,’ or submission to a certain idea,
which mars most modern English ballads, and without
subordinating his talent to realistic effect. He has a
certain manly frankness about certain masculine short-
comings, which one admires, because man is a frank
creature naturally and not a whit the nicer for being
hypocritical in print. He knows the vagabond and the
steward, and the stevedore, and that pitiful hero the
sailorman. He knows them perfectly. . . . But
the curious thing is that he knows the blue-jacket
quite as well as his poor relation, and ‘The Passing of
Parker,’ a long ballad of the death of a blue-jacket in a
China Squadron battleship, is perhaps the best narratival
ballad in dialect that we have. We like Mr. Brady less as
a serious poet, because his assertion in that metier is
mere violent rhetoric, and comes from a very windy tra-
dition. Though he can be suggestive and moving in some
of the images he uses, as in—
The penguin, standing lonely
’Neath weird, snow-darkened skies—
which conjures up a vision of the no-man’s-land some-
where south of the Magellan Straits.”

A group of miners were discussing the well-
known Dutch law relating to royalty, which sets forth
that any queen who takes to herself a consort, as the
present Queen Wilhelmina has done, must bear a baby
before the expiration of five years, failing which she
must divorce her husband. One of the party here inter-
rupted. “ That’s a bit rough on her Prince Consort,” he
argued. “ But look here,” said a practical-minded old
miner in the party, “ before I’d let a rich claim like that
be jumped I’d see about tribute ! ” —Kalgoorlie Sun.

on Friday, 18th September, at 11 o’clock.
“A BUSH STORY”
Ttr - yPON this first-rate poem look
m I In good Hume Nisbet’s latest book—
■ J Reminding us of Captain Reece,
Commander of the “ Mantelpiece .”
THE POEM.—I.
There is no land which has not been
Of dark or noble acts the scene.
The, difficulty is to save
Their records from oblivion’s grave.
Our Saviour thus begins his song
At London by John Long :
Now turn the page to Two-Five-Three
And ivatch a foiled Oblivion flee !
IV.
“ One night my icife and I were woke
By yells which through the thick scrub broke.
We both jumped up—quick dressed, then saw
A sight ivhich filled our hearts with awe.
(Please wait in awe while we explain
That space is precious, to our pain :
If all they saw you wish to see
Remember kindly— Two-Five-Three!)
XU
And gathered here beneath the ray
Two hostile tribes in savage fray.
With bomerang, and bow, and spear,
War striped, and howls of rage or fear.
Although in unfamiliar guise
The “ bomerang ” we recognise ;
“ War striped ”is excellent—but no t
We do not recognise the ‘'bow.”
XIII.
Slowly they dragged themselves along
The clotted clay, no longer strong,
As when they came up lithe as cats
In darkness, or rapacious bats.
Rapacious bats ! ’’—the phrase is prime:
We thank thee, Phcebus! for that rhyme,
O happy blacks and bats, to be
Embalmed in such a simile 1
XVI.
And, turning, saw a girl appear
All naked, flying like a deer
Loud panting, while upon her track
Appeared a stalwart, painted black.
But ha! “I sprang, and with a blow
Soon brought the cruel savage low L”
(Corroborative details strive
For precedence at Two-Five-Five.)
XXI.
I left her with my buxom Nan,
Who with her tender tact began
To clothe and question her dark guest
Why she had been so closely prest t
Be good enough at ■ ‘ clothe ” to pause,
For clothes inspire the Saxon’s laics,
Adorn the moral of the Gael —
And her., as well, they point the tale .
XXX.
At last one day our little child
Had strayed into the bush-track wild,
When we in wildest doubt set out
With a black friendly tracker tout.
We heard a nbcent waterspout
With a black friendly tracker tout
Discuss cohere Sladen’s sting could be
And where was Purtell’s victory.
XXXI.
All day we tracked her steps, we passed
O’er many a mile, until at last
We found her underneath a tree
With something red upon her knee.
The shining red her childish eye
Attracted till the tout came by :
’Tis thus a good deed shines, we know,
Within this naughty world of woe.
XXXII.
A flannel rag the child had found
Near where she, sat, and on the ground
A scarlet flannel shawl lay there
Upon a skeleton picked bare.
You see the point ? The grateful black
Had died to save her benefac-
Tor’s child, and so, with cheerful groans,
Had hung her banner on her bones.
XXXIV.
Who had it been, we could not tell.
But this I do remember well,
That when we parted with the black
A shawl like this was on her back.
O, Mister Nisbet! we implore
Immortalise us thus no more;
Our records do not try to save,
But give us back oblivion’s grave!
MEMORANDA.
AUSTRALIAN BOOK-PLATES. —III.
Victor Daley draws our attention to the follow-
ing in The South Australian Register of Aug. 25
THE RED CONQUEROR.
POET’S END IN THE SOUTH SEAS.
A REMARKABLE' SCENE.
BANQUET, MARRIAGE, AND DEATH.
Victor James Daley, poet, of Sydney, once of Ade-
laide for a brief time, recently went to the Solomon
Islands in search of health. He died there, at the age
of 45, in most remarkable circumstances, which are
graphically told in the Bed Page of The Bulletin, by
James Harrison, who is believed to be established as
a trader on the island of Choiseul. His story, says
the editor, is substantially as follows :
Then follows Janies Harrison’s account of certain
occurrences on the island of Ohoiseul, here given on
Aug. 20. We do not know what ground The Register has
for connecting Harrison’s account with Mr. Daley, unless
it be that Mr. Daley was in the neighbourhood at about
the same time. However, Mr. Daley has called appa-
rently in propria persona, and protests that he is still
alive, and that The Register has taken a most unwarrant-
able liberty when it declares him dead. We are bound
to say that Mr. Daley looks alive, and in excellent
health. However, The Register is a most reputable
paper, with a most respectable tradition, and one cannot
conceive what motive it can have for perverting fact. On
the other hand, Mr. Daley is certainly a prejudiced wit-
ness in his own cause. It is clear that, if he really were
dead, as The Register affirms, he could not be expected to
admit it. The whole matter is shrouded in mystery.
What has The Register got to say about it ?
“ I look forward, with a thrill,” says Max Beer-
bohm in The Saturday Review, “ to the time when Aus-
el i .Maoriland, Canada and other darling colonies
shall be steadily exporting to the Mother Country all the
drama she needs to supplement her own limited produce.
. . Our kitlx and kin, so martial, so commercial, so alto-
gether remarkable and delightful, are yet undistinguished
in the arts. So much the worse for the arts. I picture
them, these poor arts, pouting, languishing, smiling,
beckoning, running the whole gamut of wistful coquetry,
while our kith and kin, with their clear colonial eyes and
their firm colonial mouths, stare stolidly back, not
seducible.”
A little bird sat on a telegraph wire,
And said to his mates, “ I declare,
If wireless telegraphy comes into tfgue.
We’ll all have to sit on the air."
— Bond. Fishing Gazette.
Richard de Bury, the quaint fourteenth-century
bookfellow wh*se Philobiblion has been re-issued recently
at 2s. 6<L, notes that “ the Saviour also has warned us by
His example against an unbecoming carelessness in the
handling of books, as we read in St. Luke. For when
He had read the Scriptural prophecy of Himself in the
book that was delivered to Him, he did not give it again
to the minister until He had closed it with His own most
sacred bands. By which students are most clearly taught
that in the case of books the merest trifles ought, not to
be neglected.” “Jesus as a Bibliophile!” would look
well on the Sunday notice-board of one of our notoriety-
hunting parsons.
The single-volume edition of The Encyclopedia
Biblica should be bought by every minister of religion in
Australasia. It costs £4, and is worth it.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Shadow Song 1 .
The words of an olden rhyme
Ring in my brain ;
Dim ghosts of a golden time
That never again
Shall we, in the years to be,
Live ever more.
The sweetness of that faint strain !
As the scent of a rose in rain,
Or wind from a perfumed shore.
The merging of sky and cloud
In sombre grey,
Far rain and mist, like a shroud
Hanging over the bay,
Call back the long-forgotten
With a rush of tears;
The passing of one sweet day,
As a dream that has fled away
And is folded in the years.
How a breath of southern wind,
Or wattle-scent,
Can waken the years behind
That are redolent
Of roses in garder s dim,
And a star or two
In the purple firmament,
And a crescent moon that is bent
To gather a glimpse of you !
The words of the song are old,
The tune is slow;
They unroll, fold by fold,
All the Long Ago ;
In the East o’er the sea
Breaks the dawning light,
And the rose of its glow,
On the sky-rim low,
Shines from the grave of night.
Q.
ELFIE V. KELLEHER.
Keepsakes.
This world of ours, all garmented in green,
The Preachers say is but a passing scene.
The things we know and love are none of ours,
But lent us for a time alone. The flowers,
The waving woods, and many-laughing sea,
Are keepsakes for the Race that is to be.
And yet I know a meadow whence the Lark
Rises, and sings. at dawn above the dark.
I know a tree that in the early spring
Blooms into rose-winged birds that soar and sing
I know the still sea in the morning wan,
Like a bright steel sword softly breathed upon.
I know the secret of the World’s Desire
That hides within the red heart of the fire.
I have some friends —none better man could own—
And must I leave them, and go forth alone ?
I saw a statue in the white moonshine —
The crowned white Mother and the Child divine;
The Mother and the Child, with calm command,
Benignly gazed upon the sleeping land.
The Preacher says this world so gay and green,
So full of glamor, is a passing scene.
I wish that, when Death closes my dim eyes,
These keepsakes I may take to Paradise.
N.B.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
In the Doldrums.
This is better than the fretting
Through the streaming, gleaming froth }
Just to dream and lie fc rgetting—
It is better for us both.
Though we tingled to the welter
Of the shredded seas, our shelter
Was to meet the storm full-lipped,
Dreaming, through the gale of passion
In a happy, heartless fashion,
Of the anchor that we slipped.
Keen the kisses through the drumming
Of the thunder of the seas ;
Oh, the lyrics we were strumming
On your banjo of heart’s ease !
Oh, your lips were hot, a-flying
Through the dark when you were lying
Jn my arms, dear, in the bows.
Now the days are slow a-going,
And a softer breeze is blowing
Very scornful on our brows.
Through the screaming dark, and hissing,
Swishing spindrift on the spars,
We were kissing, kissing, kissing,
Steering not by any stars,
With your soft lips parted, pouting
For the sting of waves that, shouting,
Whirled behind us, torn and tossed.
On the road to Hell together !
With no time or tears or tether,
And the world forlorn and lost.
But our sleep came through our straining,
And I waked from dreaming deep
To the sound of tears a-raining
For the storm we lost asleep.
And the red dawn crept from cover,
And our stormy run was over,
And our cold lips sighed apart.
Oh, the idle breezes mocking
Our stripped vessel left a-rocking
In the doldrums of the heart!
It was life, love, while it lasted,
On our wild half-ended path ;
It was wine, red wine, we tasted
In the marriage of our breath
But this quiet, dear, is better ;
Dreams are free of fear or fetter,
Full of ever-shining fire ;
So we ll end the road in dreaming.
And in ghost-ships go a-steaming
To Our island of desire.
M.L.
M. CORMAC.

JAYNE’S EXPECTORANT
CURES THE WORBTCOUGHS.


A LIVERS VAGARIES.
Nervous Weakness and Exhaustion.
A Great Change for the Better.
The Case of*
Mrs C. SHEWANJ
(Bit OCR REPORTER.)
Mrs. Elizabeth Shewan, of No. 8 Little
Eduard-streeb, Pyrmonb, was recently in-
terviewed by a press representative, and
gave her views on various matters of in-
terest.
“I am given to understand that you
created a great surprise amongst your
neighbours a little while back,” said the
newspaper man. “ Will you kindly tell me
what it was all about ? ”
“ With the utmost pleasure,” was the
hearty reply. “It was by shaking off a
long-standing complaint that I surprised
everybody, myself included, for I had been
suffering for many years from dyspepsia.
The reason why my recovery was unex-
pected was because I had been under the
care of several doctors, taking their medi-
cines till I positively loathed the sight of it,
besides being treated as an out-door patient
at a hospital for five months, and no good
came from all the physics which had been
administered to me. What else could I
think than that I was incurable ? And to
be in such a hopeless predicament was really
awful. Earth and heaven were both blotted
out of my existence, as the inefficacy of the
medical treatment I had received left me
alone in the world with nothing but physi-
cal suffering to endure. For years there
was scarcely a day passed but that I felt
intensely tired and so weary and weak that
I had to let my work go undone because I
was unable to do it, and often I could hardly
get about for the terrible pains in my chest
and between my shoulders. It seemed as if
something heavy was preventing me from
getting my breath, and if the weather was
at all warm the smothering sensations were
frightfully distressing. The whole of my
system was out of order, the consequence
being that I was hardly ever without a de-
pressing headache, and a feeling as if my
eyes were swollen also troubled me. When-
ever I made an effort to do a little work the
exertion made me feel lightheaded, and at
times I was afraid to go outside the door on
account of giddiness, which used to seize me
and make me powerless to stand on my feet.
Another reason why I deemed it prudent to
keep as still as possible was that any exer-
tion gave me palpitation of the heart. It
might be through getting up from a chair
rather too quickly, or, perhaps, through a
little agitation caused by sounds in the
street that the palpitations came on ; but in
every instance I felt greatly distressed, to
say nothing of the fears about what might
be wrong with me which entered my mind.
So many people in this country die from
various affections of the heart that anything
wrong with that organ is naturally alarm-
ing, but I now know that it was my liver
which causes those serious symptoms to
appear.”
“ Were there any other liver symptoms in
evidence 1 n
“ Well, the gall-like taste in my mouth
and the furred condition of my tongue
pointed to the fact that my internal organs
required renovating, while to digest my food
was an utter impossibility. Try what I
would in the form of light diet, it was all
the same, after eating being violent spasms
of wind in the stomach which almost drove
me frantic, and often I felt inclined to roll
on the floor in the excess of agony, which
was making my life insupportable*- The
food I had eaten was not taken from choice,
because I had no appetite for it, and it was
this continuous loss of appetite which
grieved me, as I knew that I could never get
strong so long as I was unable to take
nourishment to give me strength. And as
my vitality wasted away, so did my loins
and limbs ache. I felt as sore as if I had
been beaten from head to foot, and no won-
der that my bones were racked with aches,
for my body and brain seldom got any rest.
Sleepless nights I passed in profusion, al-
though if I slept at all I dreamed in such a
terrifying manner that wakefulness would
have been far preferable. What a nervous
wreck I became, to be sure ! My nerves
were so weak and highly strung that if any-
body knocked at the door I nearly jumped
out of my chair, and then it took them a
long time to get steady again. A dread, of
something happening tormented my mind
both day and night, but did I not have good
reason to be like that, considering that my
disease stood defiantly against my medi-
cines ? ”
“As you are so well now, Mrs. Shewan,
that statement is scarcely correct.”
“ I mean up to the time of taking
Clements Tonic; then everything changed
for the better. I made a great mistake in
not heeding the advice of many of my friends
who had repeatedly urged me to take
Clements Tonic ; but I thought if doctors
could not cure me, nothing could. When at
last I became persuaded, Clements Tonic
soon showed what a mistaken notion this
was, for after taking two or three bottles I,
felt quite different, my improved appearance
being the subject of general comment
amongst my neighbours. The appetite 1
gained through the medium of Clements
Tonic was, to say the least of it, remark-
able. I was always ready for my meals,
and how could I enjoy them ? That dull
and heavy feeling after eating passed away,
and when my food ceased turning to wind
and creating pain, I could soon go about in
comfort and do a little work, for I always
woke up feeling fresh and vigorous after the
sound sleep which came thlough using
Clements Tonic. In time my chest and
head pains disappeared, and nothing gave
me greater satisfaction than the banishment
of my heart troubles. I could walk about
quite briskly without there being any pal-
pitation or dizziness, the only inconvenience
which I experienced after three weeks’ use
of Clements Tonic being from nervousness,
for I still trembled a little if I had been ex-
erting myself very much ; but in a few days
later that trouble was entirely remedied,
and it has never come back again. My
digestive system wa3 thoroughly restored to
order, and from that day to this I must say
that the weary aches in my limbs have been
strangers. The acute pains in my head and
shoulders were also carried away by
Clements Tonic, which regulated my in-
ternal organs to a nicety. Such a catalogue
of human suffering as mine was could not
have been more severe, yet Clements Tonic
relieved me of every pain, besides pouring
into my system the invigorating and disease-
curing qualities it possessed, so that I was
left with the full vigour and health of my
younger days. The return of that alacrity
and cheerfulness of spirits that I was wont
to have made my recovery complete, and I
am not likely to forget that it is to Clements
Tonic that my gratitude is owing.”
“Will you make that admission through
the press ? ”
“ Willingly, for I recognise the fact that
many people are suffering in despair like I
was, and for their benefit you may publish
my testimony in any way you choose.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Elizabeth Shbwan, of 8 Little Edward-street,
Pyrmont, Sydney, in the State of New South
Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic ; and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements-which I give voluntarily, without re-
ceiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Pyrmont, this tenth
day of March, one thousand nine hundred and two,
before me,
JAS. McDOUGALL, J.P.

“GERRYMANDERING
»
is an American dictionary word, meaning “to alter the political map, so that the voting districts are unfairly or
abnormally arranged, for the purposes of advancing the interests of a particular party.” To illustrate—
Supposing you want a smoke, and ask for
CAMEO CIGARETTES
as you naturally would, and the tobacconist offers you another brand, saying “they are just as good” because
he makes more profit out of them—that is “gerrymandering”—for that term embraces also and is a synonym
for “fake” of all sorts, from voting to even cigarettes.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 10, 1903.
Redemption by Flag-flapping 1 .
A correspondent, with the best of intentions in
the world—one of the class which goes into politics
occasionally and becomes an Owe’Sullivan—
sends to The Bulletin an item on American
patriotism from an English paper, with the
suggestion that a “rousing article advocating
something in the same line here would do immense
good.” The extract is this:
Patriotism in America is a school subject. Even in
New York the boys and girls at the public schools are
marched off to their class-rooms every morning to the
sound of the national anthem, and then made to take a
daily vow of loyalty. Over the teacher’s desk is hung
the Stars and Stripes, and the pupils, holding out their
right hands towards it, repeat: “ I pledge my allegiance
to the flag and the country for which it stands—one
country, indivisible, with justice and liberty for all.'’
But they have gone one better even than this in Kansas.
There they take the national anthem, split it up into
sections, and make each section the basis of an “ oration ”
at the annual “ commencement exercises.” For instance,
the head pupil is allotted ‘ *My Country, ’Tis of Thee,”
and proceeds to “orate” on material America; the
second pupil holds forth on the growth of civil liberty
under the title of “ Sweet Land of Liberty ” ; the third
starts from the words, “ Of Thee i Sing,” and discusses
American poetry ; and so on through the entire hymn.
* * * *
The correspondent, with his pathetic mental
failing which makes it impossible for him to dis-
tinguish between blather and patriotic feeling, is
typical of a large class in Australia. This huge
continent, after shaking off the chains of convict-
ism and securing representative institutions,
began almost at once to fall back in national
spirit. For this there were very easily recognised
economic reasons, and there are very easily ascer-
tained economic and political remedies. But the
way to reform is hindered by the number of foolish,
well-meaning people, who see the hope for the
future in teaching school-children to yell in front
of a flag, or in persuading adults in the pupil
stage of mental development to join associations
for being very proud of being Australians. There
are certain manifestations of national feeling, in
themselves of no intrinsic worth, in many cases
somewhat ridiculous when looked at in the dry
light of reason, which are natural and excusable in
times of exultation. To celebrate by holiday the
anniversary of a great national achievement, to
get intoxicated about a flag which has led to a
great national victory, or about one which
has made a good show in the face of over-
whelming defeat, to indulge in a little “blow”
and exuberance after the heat and burden
of nation-making has ended in achieved greatness,
is natural enough—not at all essential certainly,
and to be tolerated rather than encouraged, still
not exactly ■blameworthy. But the idea seems to
be more than ordinarily prevalent in this country
that Australia is to become a great nation by
having its holiday and its whoop and its disor-
dered eloquence and its flag-worship and its
exhibition of diseased vanity without having done
any of the work. It is to become a great nation
simply by resolving that it’s a jolly good fellow
and that if anyone should tread on its tail the
consequences will be dreadful. And that’s not
the way to greatness. It’s a kind of glorying in
die mullock-heap without having won anygold—as
•■hough themud were the purpose, instead of being a
rather unpleasant by-product of the work. It is
•■rue that very rarely does a patriotic movement
progress without the accompaniment of some
turgid bluster. But the bluster isn’t the move-
ment. Many Australians make the mistake of
thinking it is.
* * * *
Flag-waving, demonstrating, processing, join-
ing mumbo-jumbo lodges and associations, becom-
ing addicted to regalia and countersigns, bleating
of children and blathering of meii, hinder, and do
not help, a national movement. They put things
in a false light of sickly sentimentalism, sap
national vitality and detract from that manly
sturdiness which is the root of all genuine
patriotism. . A people truly great is ever austere
inspirit, “ sitting in calmness and light, positive
and composed”—not in any sense dour and sombre,
gay when the occasion fits, but never vain-glorious
and sentimental. It is a sign of decadence in
the American national character that it has of late
given itself up so much to oratory and flag-waving,
and Jingoism and frenzied wars with little people
—all of which are, in a sense, expressions of the
one mental attitude. Boastfulness breeds arro-
gance, and arrogance the desire for easy mastery,
and thence cruelty, and finally cowardice. It is
at the stage when the course begins to be down
hill that a nation’s titles become most grandilo-
quent and aspiring. Every people begins to
assume adjectives as it loses attributes of real
strength, and, when its fighting arm becomes
palsied, enters upon its last campaigns of spolia-
tion against the dictionary. In so much as
American public men now encourage flag-worship
and spread-eagle oratory, by so much do
they show the weakening of their fibre
and their progress towards decay. Australia,
already much inclined that way through the evil
teachings of politicians and press-writers, should
most emphatically not be encouraged by any true
nationalists to deliberately foster a puling patriot-
ism of ribbons and rhetoric The great want in
this land is not for men, women, or children to
wave something, sing something, or orate some-
thing, but to do something—to beget children, to
establish industries, to till the fields, to pay up
the debt.
* • • •
The last might almost be put first. No country
can have any self-respect which is in pawn to
foreign money-lenders, and which must always be
considering how its actions or words will “affect
its credit.” The man who is hopelessly in debt
to a virulent Catholic can’t afford to be much of a
Protestant; the man whose furniture is liable to
be sold up by an Orangeman can’t be openly
much of a Catholic ; the man who is hopelessly in
pawn to a Jingo learns to swallow his republican
views for fear of offending his creditor and not
getting the bill renewed. No man so placed can
have a soul of his own or any decent feeling of
manliness. Nor can a nation. Australia is
nowadays asked daily to tremble because some-
body’s speech or action may affect the country’s
credit abroad. So to ask Australian children to
sing
Australia’s marching on to a future bright and grand
when, as a matter of fact, she is slinking along
beneath the eye of Uncle is bad as a lie and worse
as a folly. The continent should not have any-
thing approaching a flattering remark to say about
itself until it has at least started to redeem its
pledged property and to get some of its tools of
government out of Uncle’s claws. Nor should
there be any unnecessary waving of the Austra-
lian flag until it can wave over something like a
self-contained, self-supporting country. Aus-
tralia at present imports governors, gimcracks,
military commanders, machinery of all sorts,
officials, turkeys, cheese, clergymen, lollies, law-
yers, and a host of other articles easily capable of
local production. It exports mainly immature
promissory notes and raw products. Its lot is
that of the Gibeonites, to do the rough, coarse
work of other people, leaving higher work for
supposedly higher intelligences. Until that posi-
tion is altered, and Australia shows ability to
produce guns and governors, and ships and
engines—shows that it is as good a country as
Chili or Belgium, there is no call for vainglorious-
ness. This continent really does not own a flag
or an anthem to get excited over, not having to
its credit the achievements of which they are the
symbols. So there is no good in teaching young-
sters to bellow undeserved metrical compliments
or salute a pawned banner. We should earn our
songs first and sing them afterwards; redeem our
country before glorifying its greatness. It is one
of the most discouraging features of Australian
public life that, when so much evil awaits remedy,
and so much good work is undone, the commonly
proposed method of reform is to sing sometliing,
or wear a badge, or make a speech. That is aim-
ing at the flush of hysteria instead of the glow of
health.
Our Decaying’ Natural Industries.
The daily papers and the average platform orator
supply the loose language and the poetry about
the great natural producing industries of New
South Wales; Coghlan’s just-issued “Statistical
Register,” part X., dealing with agriculture,
supplies some facts by which the loose language
and the poetry can be audited. If the loose
language and the facts don’t agree, then the loose
language, even though it rhymes beautifully, isn’t
of much account. The Foreign-trade paper tells
us that this isn’t a manufacturing country.
Nature, it says, has given us broad acres ; our
business is to look after them. So, we are told,
while our “ lusty manhood,” pursuing a healthy’
open-air life, supplies the world with raw
materials, the finished article should come to
us from the grimy factory of some older
and more thickly-peopled land, for that is
the obvious plan of Nature. There isn’t any
-evidence that any such plan really exists; but
Coghlan’s figures allow us to test, anyhow,
whether it would be good enough to meekly follow
it, if it did exist. The first fact of any conse-
quence that sticks out is that, whereas in 1882
(the first year for which the returns are furnished)
the average value of the crop which the man on
the land in N.S. Wales got was £7 4s. sd. per
acre, this year (1902-3) it averaged only £ll7s.
per acre. The total value of the State’s agricul-
tural produce in 1882 was £4,215,268; in 1902-3 it
was £4,153,831. The former was produced from
583,868 acres; the latter from 2,245,839 acres.
Thus it took very nearly four acres in 1903
to give a. return equal to what was given by
one acre in 1882. So, for 22 years of toil, 22
years of tremendous borrowing” to develop the
“inexhaustible natural resources of this great
country,” and for the labor and heart-break
of an ever-increasing army of toilers during the
best part of a generation, there is to show—just
this:
Value of
Crop per acre

1883 .. .. 5 0 11

1885 .. .. 3 17 8
1886 .. .. 4 12 7
1887 .. .. 4 14 10
1888 .. .. 4 18 1

Thealmost continuous falling away in thereturns
is the most depressing feature. The area under
cultivation has increased fourfold ; the total value
of the crop has slightly diminished ; the return for
a given amount of labor has fallen to about one
quarter of what it used to be. So far as increasing
the return from agriculture is concerned, 22 years
of struggle have been wholly wasted. The man
on the land, engaged in what we are assured is the
great natural industry of the country, must either
work about four times as much land as he formerly
did in order to get the same return, or, if no more
land is available for him, he must starve on one-
fourth of his old income. Mostly, in order to
increase his area under cultivation, he has crowded
out someone else; the latter has either drifted
down to the city (\yhere, in the absence of indus-
tries, there is little for him to do unless he can
get a job at stone-breaking or at the Fitzroy
Dock), or he has drifted, further out West,
where there is plenty of land, of a kind, avail-
able. And the further he has drifted the further
his produce has had to be carted back for
want of an inland manufacturing population
to sell it to; and on that account, because
of his heavier expenses, the £4,153,831 of produce
which the State gathered last harvest was really
worth a great deal less to the producer than pro-
duce. of an equal value gathered 22 years ago.
Nor is this. larger bill for freight the only extra
burden which the man on the land has to bear
nowadays ; in order to get even as much as he is
now doing from the land, he has, with one-
quarter the income, to spend more money on
artificial fertilisers than was dreamed of only a
few years back. . The whole position, then, is
something like this : To get the same gross return
that he used to do, he has to work four times as
much land and provide four times as much seed ;
and out of his gross receipts he has to j ay many
expenses which were formerly unknown. From
1882 to 1889 (inclusive), the average return was
£5 os. Id. per acre per year ; from 1890 to 1895,
£4 4s. 3d.; 1895 to 1890, £2 16s. Id.; 1900 to 1903,
£2 Bs. 4d. And the new expenses have increased
in inverse ratio to the returns That, so far as
N.S. Wales is concerned, is the record of the
greatest of what are alleged to be the natural
producing industries of this country—the indus-
tries that we must look after, the Foreign-trade
paper says, to the exclusion of all else !
• * * *
As to the other States, the position would pro-
bably be found a good deal worse, because hardly
one of them is naturally so well-favored as N.S.
Wales. South Australia, for instance, which at
one time made a name for itself as a wheat pro-
ducer, cultivates absolutely less land now than it
did 20 years ago—it is giving up in many places
the weary struggle to make impoverished land pay
at the lower prices for grain which now rule. In
New South Wales the lowest average return for
all crops for any five-year period has been £2
Bs. 4d. ; while, from the wheat crops for which it
was once noted. South Australia has averaged but
13s. lid. per acre for the past 10 years, and even
that miserable yield has only been accom-
plished by a heavy outlay, amounting now
to over £100,030 a year, for artificial fertilisers
which did not figure at all in the expenses until a
few years ago. Altogether, then, the loose lan-
guage and the poetry furnished by the Foreign-
trade papers and orators don’t square at all with
the hard facts with which alone the statistician
has to deal; and if, as the former allege, Aus-
tralia can grow wheat and wool better than it can
do anything else, then, in view of these facts,
Heaven help Australia.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The East Sydney Election.
Last week there was some kind of an election in
Sydney. George Reid, who introduced to Aus-
tralia the evil he calls “gerrymandering”—
George Reid, who gerrymandered the N.S.W.
electorates, and killed the principle of one vote
one value, and rejected the scheme which his own
officers had devised for the re-adjustment of the
electorates on a population basis, and who held
the election of 1898 on the old electorates because
they suited him better—lately resigned his seat as
a protest against the Barton Government doing,
in a less virulent way, exactly what he did him-
self. It is a peculiarity of Reid’s unutterably foul
political character that every political sin he com-
plains of is the one he did himself, and the sin he
complains of most violently is generally the one
he first introduced to this country. An apparent
love of shame and exposure makes him do as pub-
licly as possible the things he denounces, and
denounce as publicly as possible the thing he did
yesterday and would do again to-morrow. Reid’s
complaint against the Federal Parliament is that,
under the old rolls which it proposes to use at the
coming Federal election, 200,000 electors in large
electorates will return seven members, while
100,000 in small electorates will do the same.
And George Reid’s own record at the 1898 elec-
tion was this:—
Electors. Electors.
Marrickville .. 3912 Inverell .. .. 1914
Grenfell ~ 3814 Tenterfield .. 1879
Darlington .. 3630 Ballina .. .. 1872
St. George’ .. 3559 Newcastle W. .. 1858
Balmain S, .. 3509 Richmond.. .. 1835
Paddington .. 3439 Uralla-Walcha .. 1832

So, as usual, the thing George Reid denounces is
exactly the thing he did himself. Even the
proportionate figures are the same. And whereas
the Federal Parliament, being dissatisfied with
the proposed arrangement, was practically driven
to fall back on the old rolls because there was
barely six months in which to re-adjust things,
George Reid had two years from the time when
he decided to ignore the recommendations of his
officers till the general election, so there is no
doubt that he gerrymandered from choice. And
he has, by his denunciations, added gross political
hypocrisy to his sin, which the Barton Govern-
ment hasn’t. And the Herald and Telegraph
and Evening News, which said gerrymandering
was quite a good thing, or at least an unimportant
thing, when, by saying so they helped to
keep an importers’ Ministry in office; and
denounced it as the unutterable sin, when by
doing so there was a chance of getting a
Ministry which encourages Australian industries
out of were lying and hypocrisy,
and dragged their alleged characters in the gutter
to which they naturally belong. It wasn’t worth
the Governments while to put up an opponent
against Reid, and it wisely left him alone in his
degradation. The election did not turn, in any
sense, on the gerrymandering question. East
Sydney is the stronghold of the German and other
foreign importers. It is a place which would
cheerfully reject an archangel if he was in favor of
encouraging Australian industries, and would
cheerfully elect a hangman if he was in favor of
repressing Australian manufactures for the benefit
of foreign industry. It has a representative
worthy of it, and it would be a pity if this beauti-
ful meeting of two kindred spirits should be inter-
rupted.
The Federal Ministry.
The fact that it is the Foreign-trade newspapers
which have revived the suggestion that Premier
Babton should become Chief Justice of Australia
provides in itself a considerable discount on the
probability of such a development. Still all
things are possible in politics ; and at the time
The Bulletin goes to press Barton may be
Chief Justice designate. It is an open secret
that some of his followers strongly desire this ;
they consider that it would be a fitting reward
for long services to the Commonwealth-—this
Eost which Barton’s abilities specially fit
im to fill. Further, they think that the
Federal cause will better progress with a
Premier of a less uncertain political mind. The
obstacles in the way of Premier Barton’s accept-
ance of the post are his optimism as to his
security of tenure as a political leader, the poor
financial provision that has been made for the
Chief Justice’s position, and the general desire
among Federalists that R. E. O’Connor —anothei
man thoroughly well equipped for it—should have
the only reward he would care to take for his ser-
vices to the Commonwealth. If Premier Barton
does retire from the Cabinet, the next Preir.ier
will be almost certainly Alfred Deakin. The
other competitor will be Lyne, and the other
“ possible ” Turner. Probably Turner will not
seek the position. Lyne’s chances are hopeless.
He was Federal Premier, technically, once, but
could not get a following, and it is not likely that
the men who would not serve under him in
1900 will have changed their minds in 1903.
An estimation of one’s own personal merit is
almost the only thing in politics not liable
to fluctuation. The Bulletin would, as a matter
of fact, prefer to see Lyne as Premier rather than
Deakin. The latter is another Barton, some-
what pliant, very affable and gentlemanly, fond of
middle courses, and likely to continue the Barton
tradition of being in all things politic. Lyne, as
Premier, would probably force Turner and
Deakin into opposition, and their union with
Kingston would give hope of a strong combination
for the next Cabinet. As it is now, Kingston is
being wooed seductively by George Reid, and the
*nightinareof a Kingston-Reidcoiii bination haunts
some minds. True, such an alliance is almost
impossible. Kingston would never sacrifice his
principles to Yes-No with Reid, and no conversion
of Reid’s mind could be accepted as genuine.
Still, The Bulletin, would like to see the men
it values by his side. The natural sequence
to Barton’s retirement would be Kingston’s
accession, the following of the man of the past by
the man of the present. But the political situation
just now forbids that. After the general election,
whatever the changes or the absence of changes
The Loafer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
We like to talk of heroes as we press our Sunday
chair,
We love to yarn of battles that would lift your
very hair ;
Our eyes they start a-flashing at our deeds of
derring-do,
And we tell our lies so often that we quite believe
them true;
But we like to take it easy
In the evening calm and breezy,
Let the Lascar sail our vessels o’er our ocean wide
and blue—
For the Lord who loves a loafer—He will see the
loafer through.
We like to tell the story how our fathers crossed
the brine,
How they battled thro’ the wilderness and forty
things malign ;
We point to much magnificence in stucco and in
stone,
And cry, “Behold the Grandeur that was lifted
from the Loan ! ”
And we never dream of Sorrow
With our Borrow, Borrow, Borrow !
We’ve a Hebrew for a pilot, anti we let him kick
the crew,
And we sing, “ The Lord who loves us—He will
see the loafer through ! ”
We’re a simple, happy nation—as we sit beside our
beer,
And we kiss the hand that fleeces us and call it
doubly dear;
We boast about our Freedom, and without a
touch of shame
We own ourselves too feeble to do battle for the
same ;
So it’s bring your Britisli seamen
To protect our languid freemen,
Let us tell the wond’ring peoples, “ We’ve as good
a chance as you,
For the Lord who loves a loafer—-He will see the
loafer through ! ”
True—the children who come after us will lift a
heavy load,
But they ’ll bear their dispensation as a bullock
bears the goad;
They ’ll be chastened for the welfare of their meek,
collective soul,
And they’ll find it reckoned Righteousness at
yonder gleaming goal.
So when troublous days importune
They must bear their evil fortune;
They must never blame their fathers if their beer’s
a bitter brew—
For the Lord who loves a loafer loves a loafer’s
children too.
And we’ve made a little anthem on our languid
little ways,
And we sing it, when we're lively, as a sort of
Marseillaise;
It’s a song of “ Leaning—Leaning,” in the blazing
summer glow;
It’s a song of “Leaning—Leaning,” when the
winter breezes blow;
It’s a kind of caterwauling,
Weary canticle of crawling,
For it tells in gentle language that is grateful as
the dew:
“Oh, the Lord who loves a loafer —He will see
the loafer through ! ”

themselves out on new lines; the Foreign-trade

wages and low national ideals.

The Dominion of the Press.
It is more than probable that, if R. E. O’Connor
should decide to continue in the Barton Ministry
and stand for re-election to the Senate in Decem-
ber, he would be defeated. In fact, such an issue
is practically certain. Compared with all the
other N.S.W. Senators, O’Connor is an intel-
lectual giant. His services to the Commonwealth
have been great, and given ungrudgingly. Yet,
providing the Sydney Morning Herald and the
Sydney Telegraph mutually agreed to run Bill
Wilks or George Perry or Eden George—and
they are both quite capable of selecting any one of
these gentlemen as a Freetrade candidate —against
O’Connor, his chance of election would be practi-
cally hopeless. In the mass, the electors of N.S.W.
cannot be canvassed by any single man ; only the
daily newspapers can reach them. And in all
essential points the D.T. and the S.M.H. are
agreed. They may differ occasionally on some
matters, but it is only by inadvertence, and then
both are uneasy until the difference is obliterated.
Each one caters for the draper’s soul and the
auctioneer’s intellect, for it is on the drapers’ and
auctioneers’ advertisements that they exist; and
each one takes, with almost invariable consist-
ency, the draper’s view of politics. With the
powers of suppression and of booming which they
possess and use unsparingly, and with the miser-
able little evening papers almost always yelping
in chorus at their rear, the morning press of
Sydney has a power over the politics of the coun-
try which is as dangerous as it is degrading. At
Senate elections it is shown most conspicuously—
as when they cast into the Senate last election five
such representatives of the political rubbish of the
State as Messrs. Millen, Walker, Gould, Neild
and Pulseord. And—The Bulletin repeats—
almost any sample of York-street warehouse sweep-
ings whom the morning newspapers agreed to boom
would probably defeat O’Connor at the polls.
This is mentioned to impress again that there is
nothing more important to the Democratic and
National party in N.S.W. than some reform of
the press position. The cause of that party is
absolutely unrepresented in the Sydney daily
press and has but very poor representation in the
country. With the exception of the Barrier
Miner there is not, to writer’s knowledge, a country
paper of any great influence on the National side
of politics. A Sydney daily to take that side is,
of course, the prime requisite ; but something
useful might be done with the country press.
Every democratic politician ought to strive to help
to de-centralise press influence and to swing his
local country paper into the right path, which is
generally the other path to that trodden by the
Sydney dailies. The courageous attitude of
Premier See in N.S.W. has done a little to clip
the wings of the Sydney dailies. Concerted
action by the political leaders of democracy could
do more. It should be the first care of every
country district member to encourage as far as he
can the growth of an independent local paper.
If there were 50 daily papers of importance in
N.S.W. the task of keeping them unanimously
on the wrong side would be impossible. When
there are but two, and both controlled by the
same class in the same cit.v. it is easy,

Macedonia and Thereabouts.
The great row in Macedonia, which promises to
set Europe in a blaze before all is over, is only the
recrudescence of a trouble that has long made that
continent very tired. The Turk isn’t wholly
responsible for the trouble, though he is largely
responsible. Turkey and a great part of
Austria are inhabited, not so much by nations as
by debris. This is the Chinese puzzle section of
the continent. In very far back times, when
invading tribes poured, one after another, out of
Asia or nowhere, and struck against, first, the
Roman, and then the Greek empire, there were
wrecks thereabouts. The early tribes came too
soon, and pounded at the back-door of the
emperors of Constantinople when those gentlemen
were still too strong to have their doors
pounded with impunity. The invading nation
which couldn’t get further south because
the battered empire was still able to pre-
sent some kind of a front, which found the
Goths too strong in the North and the Franks in
the West, sank about the spot in question, and
became a snag and a danger to navigation.
There were many of them, and the result is that
Austria and Turkey include about 25 wrecked
nations, hardly any of which is really speaking
to any of the others. Then, in addition to the
remains of old bands of brigands who came down
to pick the bones of the Greek empire and perished
on the doorstep, there are the remains of the
empire itself, and of the peoples who were before
it —in fact, this country is the Sargasso Sea of
Europe —the centre of the great whirlpool, where
the water-logged and dismasted tribes accumulate.
The Turks cr - iuered Greece and its enemies im-
partially. A ,tria, with its German element for
a backbone, Keeps about 18 of these straggling
nations together in some kind of order and pros-
perity. In this respect, Austria is the blessing of
Europe; its action in keeping the peace among
all these wild cats saves its neighbors much
trouble. Turkey inherited the rest of the cats,
and made a mess of things—it never had the
Austrian staying power, and its menagerie broke
loose. The Roumanians have some sort of claim
on Turkey in Europe as alleged descendants of
the old Latin conquerors. The Greeks have a
claim as former owners. The Bulgarians have a
claim because they also owned most of the land
during their temporary glory in Simeon’s time.
The Servians claim it because it was mostly their
empire for a few years in the days of Stephen the
‘ ‘ Suffocater. ” The Macedonians have at least the
legendary claim that it was once a Macedonian
empire. The Albanians and Montenegrins play
each a lone hand and profess a cheerful readiness
to see all the other races darned first. The Italians
reckon one strip of the country should be theirs,
as part of the old Venetian republic. The Turks
are as regards a good part of the country, the men
in possession. The Russians want to be in posses-
sion so as to have a safe outlet from the Black Sea.
Austria wouldn’t mind having part of the coun-
try ; Austria is the only country which has made
much success at taming the wild cats of S.E.
Europe. The local races could wipe the floor
with the Turk if they were speaking to each
other and if old Obrenovitoh, the Pig-dealer,
could come back from his grave to lead them, but
there seems no hope of unity amons them.lt
some strong and reasonably humane State would
conquer the whole Peninsula, s k°?., the .
back into Asia, disarm all the inhabitants, fine
everybody 2s. 6d., wipe out the local boundaries,
build roads, railways, and factories, establish
schools at which a common language would be
taught, burn about 800 monasteries, drain and
clean and to a great extent rebuild the cities,
compel the whole population to wash regularly,
and kill all the stray dogs, there might in 50
years or so, be a resurrection in the Balkan
Peninsula. A sensible kind of Europe would
offer Austria the job and pass the hat round to
help pay the expenses. Almost the only real
civilisation in the Peninsula is that which
Austria has introduced in Bosnia and Herze-
govina, and this civilisation should have a chance
to spread.
Vacillating Vandemonia.
Recently a flood of new light has been thrown
on the method of how not to do things, as from
time out of date practised and approved in Van-
demonia. When the small-pox broke out in
Launceston two months and more ago, its imme-
diate effect was to cause a very unseemly and
absurd jangle between the Launceston Board of
Health, the Government, and the central health
authority of the State; the trouble arising pri-
marily from the fact that the Oentral Board of
Health meets in the capital, and is necessarily
regarded by Launceston as a degenerate and ac-
cursed institution. In Launceston the local Bumble
desired to treat the small-pox gently, caressing it
with timorous restrictions. The Central Board, on
the other hand, ordered precautions somewhat
more drastic than the strict necessities of the case
demanded. Thereupon Launceston got delirious
and yabbered of insurrection, and threatened to
capsize the empire. Here Propsting’s timid
Government took a hand and made the muddle
muddier. Dr. M ‘Call, Chief Secretary, went
to Launceston, breathing severe measures ; but
Launceston spoke ominously about withdrawing its
support from Premier Propsting’s Government,
and M‘Call became so tame that he would feed
out of the hand. Premier Propsting meantime
sat in Hobart and received deputations of devout
Hobartians, who demanded astonishing things.
To these Propsting replied that he would do
whatever the Central Board recommended. On
that the Central Board gladly gathered itself to-
gether and demanded the moon with great vehem-
ence, and M ‘Call, sitting subdued in Launceston,
said he’d see the Central Board further—or words
to that effect. The position then was that Prop-
sting and his Chief Secretary were pulling
different ways ; the Central Board was at logger-
heads with the Government; and both the
Central Board and the Government were cordially
ignored and scouted by the Local Board of Laun-
ceston. At that stage, all the Ministers went
to Launceston to talk things over with the
Bumble of those parts; with the result that
Dr. Gresswell, of Victoria, was applied to
for advice as to what might be the
best thing to do. Dr. Gresswell, realising that
the Public Health authority in Tasmania was in a
condition of Chaos and had lost its head, advised
that a mainland doctor be appointed to take
charge, and recommended Dr. Elkington. Prop-
sting’s timid Government forthwith appointed
Dr. Elkington. That expert arrived, and super-
seded every Vandemonian authority (the Govern-
ment included) ; and the epidemic was straight-
way got under. And now the Propsting Govern-
ment is so pleased with its success that it has
decided not to charge the cost of the smallpox
outbreak to this year’s revenue lest it should spoil
the beautiful Propsting surplus, but to put the
disease down as an asset till further notice.

The N.S.W. Land Revenue.
At the present time three motions relating, or
purporting to relate, to the iniquity of using land
sale money as revenue are mouldering on the
notice paper of the N.S.W. Assembly. Gormly,
M.L.A., proposes that the proceeds of land sales
should be applied to the repurchase of large
estates, that the lands thus resumed should be
leased to small holders, and that the surplus
derived from the properties should be used as
a Public Debt Sinking Fund. J. C. L. Fitz-
patrick urges that the land sale revenue
should be used as a sinking fund for the re-
payment of the public debt. Young, M.L. A.,
desires that it should be set apart as the
nucleus of a fund from which to meet, as far
as possible, the State’s obligations in regard
to interest on the National Debt.” Young’s idea
is too silly to be worth considering. The land-sale
revenue already goes to pay interest on the public
debt. As it amounts to about £1,000,000 a year,
and the interest bill is nearly £3,000,000 a year,
it would be absorbed every year, and there would
never be any fund and not much nucleus, and
things would be exactly where they are. In fact,
Young’s idea is only worth mentioning as a sam-
ple of long-winded imbecility. As regards the
proposal to apply the land sale revenue to the
repurchase of large estates, no country should go
in for the repurchase business until it has tried,
by a really heavy graduated land-tax, to make the
big estates dissolve automatically. _ If the
graduated land-tax went up to 6d. in the £
on the largest estates,, with an extra tax on
absentees, u.ore are very few large estates
that couldn’t cut themselves up and do it, too, in
much less time than the State would spend over
the business. The most sensible proposal of the
three is that the land-sale money should be
applied straight out in reduction of the public
debt. Probably, however, none of the proposals
will get so much as a hearing. In the bogged and
congested Parliament of N.S.W. very few tilings
do get a hearing, save motions for adjournment
and the like.

The Disastrous Middle Course.
“ The man who takes the middle course in a city
street is liable to be run over by a tram, ” is an
American aphorism which N.S.W. Premier See
should bear in mind. He is always taking a
“middle course” in political difficulties. The
perplexing situation created by the Upper House
rejection of the Amending Arbitration Bill has
been solved by him in a characteristic fashion.
That Bill embodied no new principle; it merely
provided somewhat better machinery for the
Arbitration Court, and declared positively an
obvious intention of the Legislature in pass-
ing the first Act, which intention had been
misunderstood. But, to save the skin of their
bogus union (the Machine Shearers’, which a fair
interpretation of arbitration law would destroy)
the squatters and squatters’ agents, who form a
majority of the Upper House, combined to throw
out the measure. It was a flagrantly indecent act
of class spite. Premier See's obvious duty was to
join issue with the Upper House and decide
whether the representatives of the people or a
coterie of squatters ruled the State. To this
he was urged by the Labor Party—urged, too.
with a threatening insistence. But that was too
bold a course for John See. It needed decision
and courage. So he “ negotiated ” with the Labor
Party, and the result is a compromise as discredit-
able to that party as it is to him. The Upper
House is to be left with the spoils of victory, but
a roundabout device is to be tried —by the appoint-
ment of a select committee of the Assembly to
make it look as if something were being done
when nothing is being done. The select com-
mittee will inquire into “the working of the
Arbitration Act”—that is to say, it will sit
as a Court of Review over the Arbitration
Court. Necessarily its deliberations will amount
to a contempt of court, and palpably its decision
—whatever it is—will not be worth twopence. It
can discredit but not coerce the Court. Particu-
larly good care has been taken by the blunderers
responsible for this move to see that the commit-
tee’s decision shall not even have the value of an
independent opinion, for its president will be a
high official of the A.W.U.— Mr. MacDonell— an
able man certainly and an honest one, but a man
who directly represents a body which is really the
plaintiff in the suit, so that the decision of the
commission over which he presides cannot possibly
be regarded with any respect. The middle course
adopted in this matter is the worst of muddle
courses. It will save John See from the present
trouble of being decisive and taking up a definite
attitude, and that is all. The A.W.U. will get
no benefit by it and the Labor party will earn the
odium of being willing to bring judicial proceed-
ings under the review of political partizans.
Good Roads.
The newest scheme of the ’Pootra Ministry,
barracked for by all the Melbourne papers—that
of providing good roads—needs more than careful
watching. Bent and Irvine are at present “ con-
sidering ” the question, while the farmer is
enthusiastically urging them to let themselves go
and darn the expense. Where the money is to
come from doesn’t appear —possibly it may arrive
from the same source as Shiels’ surplus—but if
the “ poor farmer ” and bis Ministry can work the
swindle they will do so. Bent says he is going to
“ load ” the lands benefited by the roads (Bent is
great on loading), and extra precautions are to be
taken “ to make it exceedingly difficult for obliga-
tions to be shirked.” Which blessed statement
will deceive nobody. The irrigation trusts, after
defrauding the State of hundreds of thousands of
pounds, are to-day shirking the very moderate
obligations that remain to them, with the consent
and connivance of Irvine and Bent. In connec-
tion with this ingenious scheme for getting cheap
roads the Argus’ Warragul correspondent lately
opened his mouth too wide. In reporting
the sale of two blocks of land at Mac-
Donald’s Track (Gippsland) he said: “The
blocks brought £8 18s. and £6 17s. 6d. an acre,
but they would admittedly have brought twice or
thrice the money but for the badness of the local
roads” (Argus, 21/8/’O3). So, the State is to
make the roads and thereby treble the value of
the landlord’s land, and the landlord is to pay a
portion of the cost provided Bent sends a
constable and a bailiff and makes him. There is
no other way to do so, unless Bent goes out and
bellows at him, and that would damage the
cattle through shock. If the farmer would but
use wide tires, from three to six inches, he
wouldn’t need to worry so much about his roads,
for he would himself make his road to a con-
siderable extent, besides putting much less work
on his cattle. There is a Width of Tires Act, but
it is inadequate, and, in a.ny case, is generally
disregarded. Moreover, it is quite time for the
State to help the farmer when that person has
tried to help himself and failed. So far he has
never yet tried. He never levies more than Is.
rate on himself —often not even that much and
as his land is always grossly undervalued this
really means but a 4d. or 6d. rate; and now he
whines that he can’t make his roads, and crawls
begging the city worker and the sweated public
servant to help him—these who pay from four to
six times as heavy rates as he. Furthermore, if
the farmer would but order Irvine to put a tax on
land values there would be no need for him to go
into the Gippsland ranges or the Beech Forest
or the Back of Beyond and other roadless
regions. There is land enough and to spare,
for all present requirements, within 100 miles
.of Melbourne markets. If this land were
settled, in blocks of 200 acres or so, and if the
farmer would have the honesty and decency to
levy on himself even half the rates paid by the
city artisan, he would be able to make the best of
roads, and would not need to show himself to the
rest of Australia as an abject agriculturist. Much
contemplation of worms (Gippsland grows the
biggest worm in Australia) seems to have de-
moralised the man on the land.
The Pastoralists’ "Buttoner.”
The people (and it is alleged that there are many
of them) who can’t quite see what harm there is
in having two unions registered for the same
calling under the Arbitration Act, ought to think
hard over the meaning of the manifesto which the
A.W.U. has just issued. This manifesto is
simply an intimation to members that, though the
executive does not approve of the terms, yet
members are authorised to accept the rates fixed
by agreement between the Pastoralists’ Union and
the bogus workers’ union, and filed by the Arbi-
tration Court as an award. Though the Arbitra-
tion Court has never dealt with the shearing
matter, yet an award of the court exists;
and, though that award may be the
result of a conspiracy, yet it is an award,
and prejudices any further proceedings. The
trouble lies in the adoption of the agreement
as an award of the Court. Nobody would think
of asking a court to adopt the testimonial of a
racecourse “buttoner” that his three-card trick
employer was a perfectly honest person who
always gave a pound’s worth for a sovereign ; and
nobody would expect a court to enter up that
testimonial as part of its own proceedings. Yet
that is practically what is done in connection
with the arbitration matter. The employer and
hisbuttoner ” agree, upon a certain thing, and,
if the “buttoner” is registered, no matter how
ridiculous the agreement is, it becomes an award
of the court. Then when an honest person who
has probably been badly taken down some
time in the past comes along and says that
the three-card trick employer is a fraud and doesn t
give a fair thing, the court has to point to the fact
that there is on record in its own books an award
to the effect that he is an honest and upright
person and straight dealer who is giving all that
anybody has a right to expect. It would be a
very great pity if voluntary agreements could not
be made awards of the court, because that process
often saves time and unpleasantness; but if it is
impossible to prevent bogus unions (unions of
employers’ “buttoners”) from getting regis-
tration, then an effort will have to be
made to get the other provision struck out,
and so confine the awards of the court
to the matters which the Court itself has dealt
with. The better way certainly would be to pre-
vent the registration of bogus unions; but if the
Employers’ Unions are too strong to allow their
“buttoners” to be sacrificed, then, the other plan
must be tried; for it is absolutely certain that, if
the bogus award isn’t killed somehow, it will kill
arbitration.
