The Population Question.
This letter comes from the fertile, thinly-peopled
south-west of Victoria, where the big landlords
are
A recent Age leader is responsible for this on the
population question :
“ Our forefathers furnished examples of cheerful unsel-
fishness by bringing up families of six on an ordinary
income. We do not follow their example, and are
lapsing from their high standard. Our falling birth-rate
shows a relaxing of the moral fibre disquieting in so
young a nation. The conditions here all favor expansion ;
immigration has ceased, and a continent is literally
begging for population. Coghlan’s figures show another
serious aspect of our national life. Of the first-born for
each year one quarter were illegitimate, and another
quarter were saved by the hasty marriage of their
parents.”
There is nothing to be wondered at in all this. Man
has two powerful instincts—self-preservation and race-
preservation, which govern his life. Limit the healthy
exercise of either, and you must be prepared for abnormal
results. David Syme is amongst the few people who own
all that is worth owning of this vast begging continent
we hear so much about, and they have a hired agitator
flying in the face of the Lord of the shining universe
by stating the poor should not marry, and he is paid
to do his best to still further reduce wages, the chance of
marriage and a decent existence, and to sit on the safety
valve of our national life. The so-called closer settlement
going on in Victoria throws a strong light on its falling
birth-rate and want of chastity. Once view the miser-
able kipsies of the unfortunate rack-rented tenants on
the wind-swept plains, where fairly-educated women toil
almost the full round of the clock, and bare-footed, ill-
clothed children run about the apology for a home, and
you get a sobering view of the marriage and population
question which explains many things. The tenant-
farmers of S.W. Victoria and their wives are only sup-
posed to toil that absentee landlords may live in idle
luxury, and manure firms pay 25 per cent, dividends, and
Yankee machine firms rake in their 10 per cent, from the
fruits of the farmers’ toil. The vast begging continent’s
hypocrisy is very transparent in the light of the unholy
rush for the land made available by Taverner, though
competent authorities state the average of it will not
carry one beast to thirty acres, and the eager competition
for land where it will take half a life-time to clear enough
for a garden, and the other half to make a decent track
to the nearest cemetery. Out up the big estates in
localities where there is a sure rainfall and railway com-
munication, develop our iron deposits, and stop import-
ing commodities we can grow for ourselves, and the
solution of the marriage problem, limitation of population
problem, and even, to a great extent, of the illegitimacy
problem will be arrived at.
From Tasmania:
Premier Profsting, of Tasmania, already finds himself
in a very uncomfortable and hazardous position. The
most that the worst of the taxdodgers asked of Lewis was
that the exemption under the income-tax should he raised
to £l5O or £2OO. On the hustings, Propsting pledged
himself to the taxdodgers to an extraordinary extent.
He proposes not to collect the taxon incomes derived
from personal exertion ; and, as the taxdodger is still a
sort of power in the land, Propsting is driven to push the
absurd proposal through. One or two facts, as instancing
its absurdity. In Hobart Brownell Bros., the big
drapers, will pay income-tax, being a company ; but
J. P. Fitzgerald, an equally big draper, doing a solid
cash business, will not pay. The Cascade Brewery, being
a company, will pay income-tax; Adams’ Brewery, being
owned by George himself, will not pay under Propsting’s
policy. The theory is that George’s income is derived
from personal exertion. Of course, the chief difficulty
lies in the fact that Propsting can’t afford to lose
the income-tax of Fitzgerald, Adams and co. The small-
pox in Launceston will cost the State, in direct expendi-
ture, at least £20,000; and the indirect loss of revenue
will be very large indeed. Also, the progressive measures
introduced by Propsting —absentee-tax, death-duties and
the like-will almost certainly not pass the House of Dodery.
True, there is an understanding that this Government
is prepared to deal with the House of Dodery ; but so far
the threats of Propsting have not even made the Dead-
house turn over in its grave. As Propsting is deeply
pledged to go to the country if he doesn’t get what
he wants, that broken Prop’s position promises to be
uncomfortable.
For months past Melbourne Age leading articles
have been dotted over with insidious attacks
Victoria’s national system of education. The
very latest move is to fasten the illegitimacy and
want of chastity, set forth by Coghlan, oh to the
godless system of free education. A recent leader
makes this picturesque assertion :
Fathers and mothers used to get weekly practice in
parental prudence and self-denial when they had to lay
aside the needful sum to pay for the week’s schooling. In
these days Government manages the whole business, and
parents and children lose the moral advantage of sacri-
ficing voluntarily the lower thing for the higher.
The unfortunate thing is that these old-time
parents, instead of practising the beautiful virtue
of self-denial for the sake of their children’s edu-
cation, simply, in an immense proportion of cases,
let the children grow up like the beasts of the
field. The result was that, in 1861, 40 per cent,
of the Victorian population couldn’t even
write. Now the proportion is down to 17
per cent. —practically all young children. But
the reform was achieved after a hard struggle.
When free and compulsory education was intro-
duced, hundreds of the Age’s self-sacrificing
Earents who wanted to keep their children at
ome in blank ignorance and use them for cow-
milking, had to be dragged before the courts. As
a further and still stronger evidence—as late as
1871,18 per cent, of the adults married in Victoria
couldn’t sign their names—self-sacrificing parents
had brought them up with the liberal education
of the ox. In 1901 only 1 per cent, of the people
married were in this state of ignorance. Victoria
has no ambition to go back to the old system and
rely on the self-sacrificing parent.
The hob-nailed boot of (Johen has such a terrify-
ing effect on some electors—usually those who
claim plaintively to “ have a stake in the country”
—that a public meeting at Melbourne to discuss
the Federal capital could not be held without
those who look part having the wrath of the
British money-lender flung at them. Writing to
the Argus after the amusing upset of the ’Pootra
meeting, “A Kyabramite Reformer” moans, “I
would like to ask what the British capitalist will
think of the meeting of last Monday night?”
The utter unmanliness which makes it impossible
to pass a remark about the weather without
wondering whether Cohen will object to it, seems
to afflict a lot of citizens just at present; and the
only wonder is that a “Society for Being
Polite and Nice to Cohen ” hasn’t been started.
Nine-tenths of the “right-thinking” persons in
Melbourne now wander about, with a pained and
apprehensive expression, the result of wondering
what is going to happen with the £5,000,000 loan
that falls due in a few months. Men who haven’t
seen a £5 note for years, and who have the idea
that £lO notes are no longer printed, scan the
commercial columns of the dailies and shake their
heads dolefully over the outlook. When anybody
says anything about droughts or swine-fever,
these demented patriots say “Hush” loudly, and
explain th* Irvine sits up all night planning the
conversion of the big loan. If Cohen had. the
slightest idea of the gibbering, subservient,
palsy-stricken condition of Melbourne’s ‘‘public
men ” at present, all chance of converting the
loan would vanish. A decent-minded Cohen
would prefer to deal with Dick Swiveller than
with men of this type.

«
Matrimonially, Sydney is fairly sudden, but
if Sydney is sudden, Shakspeare’s Illyria, now on
view at Sydney Royal, was instantaneous Orsino,
its duke, is making no end of an ass of himself for
love of Olivia; no woman’s heart is big enough to
hold as much as his for her. Yet inside half an
hour he is tied to Viola, even before (having dis-
guised herself) she has time to take the trousers
on; a,nd Viola, m spite of the embarrassment of
breeches, had almost as suddenly made up her
mind that Orsino’s was the only beard for her.
iwo other cases of equal swiftness occur. You
never know what couple is going to turn up
married. The noble Olivia, who isn’t
going to even see a man for seven years,
sees Sebastian for the first time; and in-
side 10 minutes the local hasty-marriage parson
has realised another fee- —the lady had apparently
had hmi chained up on the premises, not knowing
at what moment she might want to go off. And
it is casually mentioned during the subsequent
very brief proceedings that another couple, who
couldn’t be suspected of it an hour before, has
done the same thing in the interval. In this
sultry atmosphere Shakspeare incubated his
Twelfth Night,” giving to it as a second title
What You Will, ”by way of intimation that he
was rather tired just then, and wasn’t going to
worry himself into a decline thinking up names
for his play.
* * * *
And “ Twelfth Night ” might have almost
been written by a modern for the modern stage.
Perhaps that is why it is so rarely seen there ; we
like to think of the “national bard” as one who
did things that nobody nowadays could approach.
Any way, Plain Bill Holloway was Australia’s
last Malvolio, and Essie Jenyns its last Olivia,
and that was back in the middle eighties. Cer-
tainly, staged Shakspeare is nowadays expected
to spell Spectacular Gorgeousness, and this piece
does not lend itself to that sort of thing to any
great extent. Yet the scene in Olivia’s garden is
very fine stagecraft, and stage-manager Coe, in
the heavy disguise of a coffee-colored mariner, has
to come out and bow for it; and, again, while the
orchestra plays something appropriate from Beet-
hoven, a terrific storm on the stage is wrecking
Miss Dora Rignold in a colored nightgown.
* * *
There is more laughter for the modern
audience in ‘'-Twelfth Night” than in almost
anything else that Shakspeare wrote; and in
Musgrove’s playing version, the original five
acts being condensed to three, the comedy, bor-
dering most of the time on broad farce, is not
much cut. Not Olivia, Viola, and the Duke
are the principals, but roystering Sir Toby, the
imbecile Aguecheek, pompous Malvolio, the
steward, impish Maria, and the Clown. Of these
the Clown alone is not built to appeal to the
gallery in 1903. W. H. Denny is the Malvolio.
It may be difficult to present a Malvolio that does
not appear exaggerated ; and here, as in Bottom,
Denny seems at times to be extravagant. Yet,
especially in the last scene, his performance is just
about perfect. Miss Milton’s Maria is a more
even work, excellent throughout. Athol Forde’s
Sir Toby and Croker-King’s fantastic Ague-
cheek may be bracketed as satisfactory. Ken-
ningham is introduced as the Clown ; and
though the Clown’s humor does not appeal
to the popular taste of to-day, Kenningliam’s
voice always does. As Shakspeare wrote it
the Clown’s is the one singing part, with five
songs ; in this playing version Kemiingham takes
four, and Miss Rignold the fifth. Every number
is a success ; Kenuingham’s “The Rain it Raineth
Every Day ” deserves the roar of applause it gets.
The sober people don’t, however, appeal with the
strength of the others. Norman Partridge is an
undistinguished Duke ; and except that Miss Nora
Kerin looks just as every Countess should look,
she is an indifferent-Olivia. Though Miss Rignold
gives some of her fine lines admirably, her Viola
lacks distinction. On the whole, however, besides
regarding “Twelfth Night” as better suited to
modern popular taste than “Midsummer Night’s
Dream,” the writer, at any rate, regards the acting
as better ; and, if there isn’t a Dawn in the Forest
in “Twelfth Night,” there is Kenningham’s voice,
and that is worth a good deal of dawn.
** % *
“My Lady Molly,” at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), still allows Captain Harold Romney to
lay h;s heart at her feet, and to do it to music.
That brave gallant and his rival, Bland, have
Ceased to make the tinsmith noise with their
swords as if a duel were in progress, but
Florence Young still announces that “she isn’t a
highwayman, nor any kind of a man”—to the
great joy of people who much prefer her as a
woman, For the rest, the limbs of the ballet are
as beautiful as ever, and the early Victorian
bonnets of the chorus are so appreciated that they
are being copied and worn by Sydney Society.
** * *
The two noted pianists, Florence Menk-
meyer and Hambourg, who have been appearing
simultaneously in Sydney, have not been treated
very well in the matter of audiences. Both played
to nearly empty halls. That section of Sydney-
aiders which talks with passionate admiration of
Vabgner and Motezart and Shykufsky was the very
section that stopped away. But then this is the '
section that will sit up all night playing coon and
cake-walk music. Possibly if Miss Menkmeyer
had been better business-managed she would have
been more of a financial success. And if
Lemmone and the little Batchelor girl (who
has a lark’s voice) had left Mark and Bore-us
out of the programme, they would have banked
quite a plump sum However, peace be with
their pieces—from prelude to finale.
** * *
Westminster Abbey Glee and Concert
Party opened to a hall (Sydney Town Hall)
packed as it used to be for Melba and Castles.
The six boys are so fresh and cherubic,
and have sweet voices and wear Eton
clothes. Not the football class of boy at
all, but the Carlylean boy who ought to be
brought up underground. Madame Marie
Hooton and the other grown-ups are very good—
for grown-ups, but it is the little boys that the
audience loves.
* iff * *
“A Life’s Revenge,” the new Sydney
Criterion production, is one of those cheap-and-
nasty things which consist of throwing the alleged
vices of our neighbors upon the broad white
screen of our own alleged national virtues.
It was just this sort of thing, presented, not
so much, certainly, on the stage as in the
press, that not long ago made possible a cruel
and cowardly war, which cost us a good deal of
national respect and over £200,000,000. It may
be readily admitted that the materials, such as
they are, are handled with considerable skill in
the present Criterion production. A rising
young Russian artist is in love with a Russian
princess ; and so is a wicked Russian prince.
The prince strikes the artist; the artist
whips him with the knout snatched from the
prince’s own serf-whipper. The prince wants
revenge. Orloff, the serf-spanker, is appealed to
for a plan. He is a serf himself ; he offers to tell
the prince a plan as the price of his liberty and
his son’s, lie tells it —the artist is the prince’s
own born serf; but he doesn’t know it. To save
herself from the prince, the princess marries the
artist. So, the artist being the prince’s serf, his
wife also becomes his serf; and the prince
can do what he likes with both of them. Then,
in the midst of the revenge, the prince hands
Orloff the promised release of himself and son.
The son is the artist; and the wicked prince is
foiled. English characters supply most of the
comedy or farce; though usually standing
round and making jokes, they are always
on hand to biff the villain in the eye;
and the villain looks up and damns England, and
says he would biff the Englishman back, only
Miss Beatrice Holloway is a likeable princess,
who fights a sword duel with another princess
(Miss Conroy), and gets the best of it. If some-
body would take Miss Holloway aside and, before
it is too late,_ implore her to open her mouth and
say “will” instead of “wee-eel,” she would be
even a greater success than she is. Vivian
Edwards is the handsome young artist, and does
his best work of the season ; Hodson is in a new
role as principal clown, and does his fooling
cleverly; Albert Lucas is a satisfactory Orloff;
and Godfrey Cass makes a very poor fist of the
Russian prince. The “Revenge” will be played
for a month ; and then a rising young actor named
Alfred Dampier will join the co.
** * *
On Friday of this week “The Worst
Woman in London” will give over making things
uncomfortable for Inman at the Lyceum, and
“ The Power .and The Glory ” will be revived on
Saturday. Miss Eugenie Duggan, who, so far, has
not been in the Lyceum cast this season, will
reappear.
*# * *
There is no doubt as to Augustus Thomas’
skill as a dramatist. The man, whose name is
one-half ancient Emperor and the other half
modern bootmaker, can make plays which are all
human nature. “In Mizzouri ” and “Arizona”
are a good pair on which to claim a reputation.
Production of the former by the Frawley Co., at
Sydney Palace last Saturday, necessarily chal-
lenged comparison with a former staging of the
piece here by Nat Goodwin and co. On that com-
parison it shows poorly in places, some of the actors
rather spoiling, with comedy business, characters
which were quite funny enough without it. The
Dave of J. R. Amory was a notable example of
this. But the principal roles were well sustained.
Miss Van Buren added to her reputation as a
skilled and versatile actress, as Kate Vernon, and
Daniel Frawley gave a forcible and natural ren-
dering of Jim Radburn. In picturesque character
parts Miss Christine Hill and Jeffrey Williams
were as good as they were in “Arizona”—which
is saying a great deal. The Frawley season closes
to-morrow (Friday) with a revival of “ Arizona ”
—taking them all in all, its best piece. The co.
has given to the public, always carefully and
sometimes brilliantly, four interesting American
plays, and a return visit with more of the same
sort would be welcome.
* * * *
For the forthcoming week “ the Players ”
hold Sydney Palace and will produce “Dandy
Dick” and “Sowing the Wind” by leave of
Robert Brough, and, being a more than ordinarily
good amateur organisation, will do it in such a
way, probably, as not to make Robert Brough
regret his leave. Then the Willoughby Geach
Co. opens a season of merriment.
** * *
At Wirths’ Circus, the heathen Chinee
who balances himself on the point of a
needle stuck into the back of a chair on the end
of a revolving ladder is wearing a slightly worried
look in his slant eyes. Last week Gov. Rawson
turned an interested eye on the gamble, but did
not show any wild desire to bet on the result,
though Pagel’s lion certainly seems a good enough
investment at even money. There were hopes
that his Excellency would have broken up the
Howard Brothers by producing one of his West
African horror curios, something so dreadful that
when the investigating Howard telegraphed to
his blindfolded brother, that party would be
seized with the idea the investigator had got
suddenly drunk. But his Excellency merely
showed a medal. Up to date there has been no
reason for adding any more sensations to the
show, but in the event of the attendance falling
off it is understood that the management will
arrange further amazing shocks.

Mr. W. J. Wilson, the well-known scenic
artist and theatrical manager, and one-time
partner with the late Signor Majeroni, is to be
tendered a testimonial matinee performance at
Sydney Palace, on the 17th. Many influential
patrons—including the Governor-General, State
Governor, Premier Barton, State Premier See,
the. Admiral, Mayor, Consuls, etc. —are lending
their support, and the pomp and fashion of Sydney
are to be there unanimously.
** * #
At Sydney Tivoli, Hill and Sylvainy, who,
as examples of strength and grace, make even that
wearisome thing, trick cycling, good entertain-
ment, are giving a short farewell season of a few
nights. Fields, the juggler, is the other big
attraction. Sydney Tivoli rather wants a nice
girl now—a girl with a good voice, and a knowledge
of dancing, and a smile like an early Spring sun-
rise—someone of the Lottie Collins type. The
only valid reason that a variety theatre has for
existence is that it can give a nice g irl a chance
to have all the stage to herself, with no “ support ”
except what Nature has provided.
** * *
The normal population of Melbourne was
swollen by a crowd of agricultural visitors last
week, and most of the contingent from the
interior crowded to see “Are You a Mason ” at
th© Princess’s. This old-fashioned farcical comedy,
adapted by an Anglicised German gentleman
from the work of two other Germans, seems to be
specially adapted for holiday audiences. It isn’t
the sort of farce that sends a cold, experienced
world into convulsions of laughter. An ordinarily
frequent theatre-goer, who has gazed at many
mothers-in-law and grown weary of their
hen-pecked relatives, asks nowadays for a
pinch of probability in a basin of thin
dramatic soup. “Are You a Mason” is
very thin soup, with no suggestion of prob-
ability in the mixture. The principal men
characters hardly pretend to be sane citizens,
except when they are conversing briskly
together, and then they talk on lines of inter-
ruption, where each speaker in turn takes
words from the other fellows mouth to save
time. If the plot were built on a human
foundation this scribe would humbly re-
tract his last week’s misstatement re Angelina,
who turns up from an imaginary grave and is
charged with being the mother of Amos Blood-
good’s unborn twins. The previous notice wrong-
fully stated that Angelina was never married to
Amos, or otherwise subjected to any risk of twins,
whereas the narrative refers to her as the divorced
wife of Amos aforesaid. After considering the
case in all its bearings this scribe prefers to cling
to his mistake. The idea that Amos had been
dreaming of indiscretions which never took place
was more humorous than the author’s idea of
divorcing Angelina without giving any particulars
of the legal proceedings. Who was the co-
respondent in the case, anyhow?
** * *
At Melbourne Royal it is refreshing to
find the Bland Holt Co. engaged upon a work that
shakes up its mental faculties and puts the mem-
bers in sympathy with their parts. “The Golden
Ladder has patches of real merit—scenes of sus-
tained interest, genuine dramatic effects, and bits
of pathos. At its worst, it is fair, average melo-
drama; at its best, it rises nearly to “Silver
King level. The second act, which has been
revised, and brought up to date, introduces the
British Fiag under claptrap conditions, and makes
Kev. Walter Baker look a foolish parson, teaching
Terewth to China whilst the Boxers howl at his
gates. Also the drama’s brief incursion into
Chowland directs unfavorable attention to Miss
Ross’s besetting sin as an actress. Miss Ross
rarely gets away from her simper, and in this hour
of imminent peril she expresses her devotion to
the parson with the same coquettish simper as
usual, although they have been married six years,
and their intelligent offspring and themselves
are likely to be disembowelled by Boxers
before the sun goes down upon the pagans’
wrath. The other three acts of “The Golden
Ladder ” have nothing to do with Britain’s sym-
bolical .bunting, or the Chinese question. The
heavy- villain spreads himself on his native
English shore, and Miss Ross is “jugged” for
shooting him by accident, and Baker sorrows to
great advantage, and Bland Holt, the benevolent
elaerly pastrycook, gently reproves his wife for
being too business-like as regards Baker’s board
and lodging.
** * *
A week of “ Sherlock Holmes ” is winding
up the season of Cuyler Hastings and co. at Her
Majesty s (Melb.). There’s a certain method
about the madness of the Sherlock story, a calm
cool air of consistency which compels respect and
strikes the sceptic dumb. The plot is a fine piece
of impudence adorned by big splashes of darkness
weird limelight eccentricities, and the ruddy glow
of an alleged cigar-end. In its purely melodramatic
way, Sherlock ”is a triumph of the preposterous,
a splendid imposition, a feast of unreason, and
a thing to be seen more than once. With
one exception, the cast of characters is what
it was a while ago. Cuyler Hastings repeats him-
self as the dreamy man of action ; Miss Chevalier
is the dreamy .young woman with the tearful voice
a,nd lunacy in the family ; Miss Lane continues to
stand and fall by the same scowling Larrabee as
before; and Atholwood is again the elderly pro-
fessor who talks into the fatal tube. But Cregan
alas !is no longer Augustus Glover. One misses
the huge voice and rolling eye of Augustus de-
ceased, and hopes that the souls of the citizens he
put away will bear no witness against him.
* * * *
This is preliminary to mentioning that the
dramatisation of Tolstoy’s novel, bearing the
creepy title of “Resurrection,” will be staged at
Her Majesty’s next Saturday. Tolstoy, as some
people don’t know, is a superior Russian demo-
crat, who once wrote a rather naughty story about
a sonata, but is an intensely moral reformer all
the same—so moral, in fact, that he “refuses to
take any fees arising from the play founded on his
book (advt.). The cause of humanity would get
along just the same if he took the fees; neverthe-
less, Tolstoy remains obdurate on the question of
cash. A play adapted from the novel of so pecu-
liar a person ought to be interesting. It will cer-
tainly be an impressive show, and the new com-
pany. selected in distant Britain for “Resurrec-
tion ’ purposes, will include two well-remembered
Australian favorites—Miss Maud Jeffries and
Julius Knight, alias Marcus Superbus.
* *• * *
Mark Hambourg is returning to Mel-
bourne for a few re-appearances at the Town
Hall, a building where Mark’s genius can be more
comfortably appreciated in September than in
mid-winter. The first of the Hambourg concerts
will have Marshall-Hall’s orchestra in the enter-
tainment. The famous concerto by the composer
whose name begins with Tschai and ends with
kowsky is a hysterical delight in store for the
musical Elect, likewise a pleasure for the common
or garden Goth. This orchestral concert is to
happen on Saturday afternoon (12th). Subse-
quently the Hambourg brethren, Mark and Boris,
perform without an orchestra on Tuesday evening,
Wednesday afternoon, and on two later occasions.
** * *
At the Opera House the programme is
practically what it was last week and the week
before that. Dan Crawley has changed a song,
perhaps, and the first part has changed a girl, but
the Hallen-Fuller sketch still relates to the matter
of Broncho Bill, and Madame Marzella’s birds are
the principal attraction as before. The birds
drew tons of patronage to Rickards’ during Show
Week Their last nights are announced, but the
farewell seems likely to be of a lingering character,
and an extra Saturday matinee remains a feature
of the boom. Understood that the Manila
cockatoo who somersaults backwards has protested
against these extra Saturday matinees in language
quite unfit for publication.
* * * #•
Dan Barry passed away from the Adelaide
Royal with yet another speech, and with one night
of “East Lynne.” D.B. told a flattered but
openly incredulous audience that Little Willie
would ascend to heaven in the care of “real
angels,” Adelaide being the only place where he
had been able to obtain them. Most of the regu-
lar theatregoers, who had carefully stayed awaj'
from the Barry season, treated William Anderson’s
“Mariners of England” spectacle similarly, but
business improved rapidly when it was found to be
well mounted and fairly well acted. Harry Diver,
as Nelson, took most of the honors; and Miss
Helene Burdette, as the heroine, most of what
honors were left. But after a week the
co. came down to a moderate kind of detective
story about a Face at a Window. Cuyler Hastings
(who has not been seen in Adelaide yet) is
announced to produce his superior detective story
at an early date. Rudinoff is also imminent
at the Tivoli, where Hill and Sylvainy have
fallen downstairs on a single buggy-wheel for
the last time. That remarkable comedian, Grace
Emmett, left at the same time, and at present
cycle-racing on the stage is the chief attraction,
the indoor championship of S. A. being contested.
Lemare has descended upon the Town Hall, and
given a series of recitals on the big organ, which
he pronounces as easy to play, but badly in want
of up-to-date fixings.

A letter from Wellington (M.L.) :
John P. Sheridan has finished an attenuated
season at the Opera House with “Mrs. Dooley’s
Little Joke.” Nobody discovered the joke. The
season has been productive of “The Lady-
Slavey,” “Cinderella,” “Fun on the Bristol” (for the
752,956,?05th time), “ When the Lamps are Lighted,” the
aforementioned infinitesimal joke, and little cash. John
F.’s shrill Irishisms become more unintelligible with each
visit, but the ballet of the present company is like unto a
beer spring in a prohibition district. Maggie Moore
lifts up her youthful, middle-aged voice in glad
and sad Irish song in “ Killarney ” at the Opera House.
As far as Wellington is concerned, “ Killarney ” has been
considerably discounted by Premier Seddon stating that
the lakes at the place of that name are not nearly so beauti-
ful as many in Maoriland. The Irish vote totters in the
balance on this account. P. R. Dix hangs on the Theaue
Royal with a big variety company of passable calibre.
John Coleman was tried for a week, but he seemed ill
and unable to “scarecrow” worth his old hat.
Phil May’s drawings, for exhibition at 324
George-street next week, might form the nucleus
of a national portrait gallery. The politicians of
the eighties, the actors, the society people and
public functionaries, are drawn and caricatured to
the life. Here to-day’s “old men,” the veterans,
may see how they looked when in their prime—a
Dibbs erect and sprightly, a Reid almost slender
by comparison with the corpulent hero of 1903.
The “patriots” who Mafficked in Aus-
tralia when the Boers raised the siege of Barnum-
Powell will be interested to learn these facts, as
ascertained by the Pall Mall Gazette :
The town guard of Mafeking, who held the inner works
during eight months, have not been given the pay they
were promised in addition to their trench allowance; the
troopers have not been paid v/hat was due to them in lieu
of rations ; Oonolly, one of the two men who turned out
the gun, and kept Baden-Powell supplied with the shells
that went far to save Mafeking, received only £5 as an
honorarium, and Coghlan, the other man, received noth-
ing beyond trade pay.

The merit of the Wilfley Ore Concentrator is
now fully recognised throughout the Commonwealth, and
its use is extending every day. The New No. 4 pattern
is doing excellent work at many mines on which it has
been installed. Recent additions to mining plants
comprise ; 4 tables to the Great Fingall, 6 to the Oroya
Brown Hill, 4 to the Ivanhoe Company, 4 to the Sons of
Gwalia, 3 to the Queen’s Cross Co., Charters Towers, 3 to
the Long Tunnel Extended, 1 to the South Moon, Bendigo,
and many other mines too numerous to mention. Up-
wards of 600 of the Wilfley Tables are now in use in
Australia, giving excellent results on gold, silver, lead,
tin, and copper mines. Full particulars and a useful
pamphlet on concentration can be obtained from the
Sole Manufacturers in Australia, The Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne.*

* • * *
British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

The dividends of South New Moon last year
(£59,200) were equal to 400 per cent, on its paid capital.
Those of Hustlers No. 1 (£36,300) were equal to 250 per
cent. Both these are Bendigo cos., and stand at the head
of Victoria’s 1902 list. Third came the Long Tunnel
Extended (Walhalla), whose £35,040 was equal to 65 per
cent, on its capital.
* * * *
Inglewood (Vic.) is having one of its periodical
revivals. The declaration of its first dividend has been
followed by the appearance of two or three cos.—all
small things—to work old ground. The reefs are patchy ;
good finds occasionally turn up in most unexpected
places. A party went prospecting in an old cow-yard
lately, and has already got out a first crushing of loz.
14dwt. to the load. This is a bit beyond what was
thought to be the end of Maxwell’s line.

At the end of August last year Queensland had £198,705
towards paying the year’s interest bill, which amounted
to £1,509,182, and it wound up with a deficit of £191,341.
This year it has so far only £190,821 saved up ; so at the
present rate of going it will have a deficit of within a few
pounds of £200,000 at the end of the current year. Its
monthly interest bill is £125,765; and it has so far
averaged only £95,410 to meet the bill. And Queensland
still sleeps on !

* * * *
The annual report of the N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioners, now given to the Assembly, does not make
the position look any better than did the preliminary
figures compiled a month ago. There is a confession, in
fact, that “ the year under review has been, financially,
the most disastrous in the history of the railways of the
State.” The admitted deficit, reckoning interest at 3.552
per cent., was £425,879. The actual deficit was, however,
greater than that, because of the failure to charge a very
considerable sum to the capital account. The principal
disputed item is the £1,000,000 which was handed to the
Commissioners, when first they took office, to put the
railways into good order ; this amount they never have
charged to capital, on the ground that for years previously
the lines and stock had been neglected. But the public
is not interested in that quibble; what concerns it is
that the million was borrowed and spent and interest has
to be paid on it. If, then, all the proper charges are
made, the interest bill will be increased by something
over £85,000, and the year’s railway deficit will pass
£510,000. This is independent of a tramway deficit of
£19,396. The admitted capital cost of the railways was
£41,654,977, on which the interest is £1,474,473. In view
of the fact that railways, no matter how patched, go to
ruin, and rolling stock becomes obsolete, a sinking fund
of at least 1£ per cent, should be provided for. If that
were done £2,099,296 would be required for the year
above working expenses. The earnings were £3,314,893,
the expenses £2,266,299, leaving £1,048,594 to go towards
interest and sinking fund. With such a sinking fund,
then, as any honest trading firm would deem necessary,
the railway accounts for the year would show up
£1,217,705 to the bad.
* * * *
Probably the worst feature of the N.S.W. rail-
way position, next to the recent rapid addition of appar-
ently hopeless lines, is the fact that a large number of
old lines don’t seem any nearer paying now than
years ago. Here is the record of the losses made by
three or four for the last few years :
1898. 1900. 1902. 1903.
Junee to Hay .. .. £14,476 £19,057 £19,679 £16,772
Narrandera to Finley .. 12,536 15,592 13,255 12,412
Nevertire to Warren .. 865 1,034 1,148 1,547
Byrock to Brewarrina.. 2,531 5,889
Nyngan to Bourke .. 10,125 18,267
There surely ought to be some point where the advan-
tages gained by “developing the country” will
definitely show themselves. The fact that many lines
don’t seem to be getting any nearer that point sug-
gests a doubt whether they really have “developed
the country "at all in the sense of improving it Any-
how, it is an unquestionable fact that most of the Western
half of the State is worth a good deal less now than it was
before the State spent a good many millions in building
railways to develop it.

Twelve months ago the co. had, on paper, £9171 of paid

1895 .. .. £178,466 1900 .. .. £83,084

In other words, since 1895 the co. has reduced its liabili-

to do so it has lost £88,048 of its capital. And it still has
£58,301 of outside liabilities to liquidate. At the same
rate, in order to complete the liqu.dation, the securities
would have to be helped by capital to the extent of a
little over £25,000. And, on paper, there is only £9089
of capital intact to do it with. It is a tight corner to
get into.
* * * *
No doubt for good and sufficient reasons,
balance-sheets and statements of accounts don’t figure
very largely in the plan of campaign of the Independent
Order of Foresters. A request to the “ head office for
Australia ” of this weird assurance concern brings, how-
ever, certain papers relating to the business for 1901;
similar information was sought regarding 1900 and 1902,
but it is not available. That figures for only one year out
of three should be forthcoming can’t be regarded as quite
satisfactory. The Red Indian’s total net income for the
year 1901 was £524,621. The total expenses were £62,496.
This includes £37,769 “reserved for contingencies.” If
this latter amount were really reserved, it should, and no
doubt would, be included among the reserves ; but it is
not so shown and does not appear in the balance-sheet.
It is tolerably clear, therefore, that the alleged reserve
was promptly spent, and that, instead of the expenses
being £24,727 as alleged, they were really £62,496. On
that basis the expenses amounted to £lO 183 3d. .per cent,
of the total income shown in the balance-sheet. That
would be very moderate if it were all, but when the Red
Indian concern spends nearly 11 per cent, of the ordinary
income, and charges the insured heavy “ court dues ” as
well, and spends them without putting them into the
balance-sheet, it becomes, to the small insurer, a ridicu-
lously expensive affair. And in • return for its high
expense rate (much higher to the small insurer than the
rate of many Australian offices), and in consideration of
the fact that it charges more (court dues included) for a
£lOO policy than any good Australian office, how
does the security look? At last valuation the A.M.P.
Society had £17,022,045 to cover liabilities of a
present value of £16,379,150, a surplus of £642,895.
When the same sort -of investigation'of the Red
Indian’s affairs was made, the then value of its
liabilities was £7,159,342, and it had £518,724 wherewith
to meet them. In other words, the Red Indian had a
deficiency of £6,640,618. Thus, the only possible chance
of meeting liabilities lay in a vast proportion of the in-
surers dropping out and losing every penny that they had
put into the concern ; if they could be persuaded
that the concern was good enough to stay in, and
they stayed in, then the concern would become hopeless.
If the Red Indian Society were, in some legitimate way,
making very large profits now, the position, bad though
it looked at the last actuarial investigation, might be
improved. The society isn’t, however, doing anything
of the kind. On the contrary, its earnings are small. At
the beginning of 1901 it had funds amounting to £883,920.
At the end of the year they were £1,020,710. Interest and
dividends were £37,938. On the mean of the funds the
return was, therefore £3 19s. Bd. per cent. On the same
basis of calculation the A.M.P.’s earnings for the same
year were £4 9s. 6d. per cent. Comparing the Australian
Society, then, with the Red Indian concern, these things
so far show out:
(1) The Australian had a substantial surplus at its last
valuation, and the R. 1., on the same basis, had a huge
deficiency.
(2) The Red Indian expense rate is higher—apparently
the insurer has to pay a good bit for the privilege of
having “ supreme chief rangers ” and other such strange
creatures with names like Oronhyatekha.
(3) Oronhyatekha is earning far less with the R.I. in-
surer’s money than the Australian society is earning.
There are a few interesting things beside
these which the 1.0.F.’s balance-sheets and schedules
show. In Victoria (where the head - office for
Australia is) there were in force on Dec. 31, 1901,
655 policies, purporting to insure the holders for
£108,700. The balance-sheet shows the total assets of
the order to be £1,055,802, of which £7021 is stated to be
invested in Victoria—£7o2l to cover £108,700 ! There
certainly is a further item of £70,638, “ cash on deposit,”
and possibly Victoria has a few pounds of that as well ;
but even if it has its full amount in proportion to the sum
assured, the total would be only about £lOlO. In Vic-
toria, then, where the head office for Australia is, and
where presumably it has most, if not all, of its Australian
securities, it seems, as far as can be estimated, that about
£BOOO is held to secure payment of £108,700 ! Probably
this would not matter much if the concern had a sub-
stantial surplus elsewhere, but when it has a huge
deficiency elsewhere—well, it looks very much as though,
should the threatened collapse come, the Australian
policyholder’s deficiency would be rather more than
ordinary.
The Red Indian concern gives no surrender
value ; so that, when a man becomes convinced that the
security isn’t good enough, or if reverses come and he
can’t meet one payment when due, he loses everything he
has paid. And yet this has been represented as a form of
assurance specially suited to the wage and salary earner
—the person who, of all persons, being at any time liable
to temporarily lose his income, ought to see to it that! a
surrender value provision is attached to his policy to keep
it alive till he is able to pay. In 1901 the forfeited policies
numbered 12,929, and were for £2,438,608. This represents
an appalling loss of the sayings of the poor. And there is
one more point—after which the Red Indian, who hasn’t
had any attention for a long time, and was beginning to
presume upon the silence, may be left again for a while—
say, until the 1902 figures come along. The Red Indian
had total funds on Dec. 31st., 1901, amounting on its own
showing to £1,020,710. On its own showing (in fact, it
shows all these things to which allusion has been made)—
it has policies in existence purporting to assure for
£44,594,697. It has thus £1 in hand towards paying
£43 13s. 9d. The A.M.P. had, on the same date, policies
assuring £49,366,565; and its assurance fund was
£17,864,514. It had thus £1 in hand for every £2 15s. 3d.
Put in another way, if the whole of the policy-holders of
the A.M.P. fell down dead to-day, it could pay 7s. 3d. in
the £ ; in similar circumstances, and notwithstanding the
wholesale forfeitures of premiums, the Red Indian could
pay s.|d ! And this draggled concern, with intolerable
bumptiousness, professes to look down on sound assu-
rance concerns, and passes off its miserable show as a new
gospel and a great reform in life assurance.

June, 1902 2228 13 2000 38,782
Dec., 1902 4412 1 2000 41,196
June, 1903 636 59 2000 39,772
The January-June half-year is tHe “off” period in this
co.’s affairs ; the wool go°s down, and most of the station
supplies go up, in the other half. In the past six months
there was far less ordinary traffic than usual even for the
“off” period, and if it hadn’t been for the carriage of
returning live stock, the co. could hardly have escaped
a loss. Somebody was asking the other day how
it is that the privately-owned Deniliquin line can make a
profit while the State-owned lines can’t. One reason is
that the private concern isn’t run as a charity. In the
12 months ending June 30, 1901, the co.’s receipts from
the carriage of live stock were £683, equal to 4£ per cent,
of total receipts. In the last 12 months they were £3432,
or 22 per cent, of the total. The co. was very sorry, no
doubt, for the people who had stock and no feed ; but it
charged them freight all the same. The State, on the
other hand, had to show its sorrow by charging no
freight; and so the Railway Department is charged with
what the Charity Department ought to be. Bunching
the last two years of bad seasons together, the average
return has been equal to £5 13s. 9d. per cent, per annum
on the co.’s paid capital (£100,000).

J.M.O. : (1) The Bulletin never refused the
Independent Order of Foresters space to reply in its
columns. It offered the same space that this paper’s
comments on the 1.0. F. had occupied—about a column
of small type. When the 1.0. F. man insisted on filling
about two columns, and said he would have that or
nothing, the negotiation broke off. (2) The 1.0. F. never
even proposed inserting its answer as an advertisement,
therefore there was no refusal. (3) No local office
offered a single copper to get the 1.0.F.’s letters or
advertisements suppressed. Why should, it? When
each of the large offices here manages to get along despite
the competition of eleven other large offices, what differ-
ence is a concern such as the 1.0. F. likely to make to
them ? Even in its own country it amounts to very little
alongside such a concern as the Canada Life, and it isn’t
likely to shake things up very much here E.L. :
Know of no buyer of manganese ore in Australia, and at
about Is. per unit it would not pay to ship. Manganese
and many other now valueless things could be used up if
the iron industry were established P. Garlic: Why
should softgoods M'Millan be chairman of a farmers’ co-
operative concern ? Well, his firm put in about three-
fifths of the £25,000 share capital. The agreement is
that the M’Millan interest can be got rid of on a few
months’ notice ; butN.S.W. farmers don’t know enough

At the commencement of the year the paid capital was
£21,834; at the end £23,460. On the mean the profit
equalled £3 15s. per cent. ; and a co. which makes 3|
per cent, and pays 6 per cent, is rushing to destruction.
Apparently some allowance is made for depreciation,
and it would be just as well to know how much it is :
at present it is lumped with other things—the co. has
the lumping disease rather badly. Liabilities to the
public are fairly extensive for so young a concern,
and are growing : they now amount to £23,376.

At last week’s meeting of the Mutual Store
(Melb.), the chairman, in a pained voice, drew the atten-
tion of shareholders to the item of “ Income Tax, £269 ”
and urged them to protest against the iniquitous levy of
13d. in the £ upon the business profits. Practically he
asked them to believe that the dividend of 5 per cent
might have been much bigger but for that deduction of
£269, and one shareholder apparently did believe it.
Anyway a Mr. Blashki “ held that the Store had done very
well in paying 5 per cent., considering the income tax and
the condition of the city.” Whereupon several old ladies,
of neglected arithmetic, shook their heads and sighed.

The profits shown above are net profits. The reserves
have a habit of moving up or down without any explana-
tion, and in the foregoing these movements are allowed
for. Thus for the past half-year the profit and loss
account shows earnings amounting to £6392 ; but on top
of that there is an unexplained addition of £467 to the
reserves, and this is here treated also as profit. Leaving
these movements out of account the progress has been :
Net Earnings as Shown in Profit and Loss Account.
Dec., 1899 .. £8894 Dec., 1901 .. £6149
June, 1900 .. 7503 June, 1902 .. 4410
Dec., 1900 .. 8493 Dec., 1902 .. 2859
June, 1901 .. 5354 June, 1903 .. 6392
The net profits are equal to £6 17s. 2d. per cent, per
annum on the paid-up capital (£200,COO). Including re-
serves, the return is £6 os. 4d. per cent., which isn’t quite
so bloated a result as the protected manufacturer always
extorts, according to the foreign-trade person and print.
Mort’s Dock, however, shows a better result
than almost any other Australian manufacturing con-
cern, apart from breweries, noticed in this column during
the last three months. The record is, in fact, almost
ghastly. Here it is : Oastle Salt (S. Aus.), dividend 8|
per cent. ; Denton Hat Mills (Vic.), 10 per cent. ; Shale
and Oil (N.S.W.), nil; Swallow and Ariell (Vic.), nil (loss,
£4097); Sydney Meat Preserving, nil (loss, £4366);
Walker’s Engineering (Q.), nil; Ballarat Woollen and
Worsted, 15 per cent.; Australian Sheep Dip, nil;
Colonial Sugar Refining, 10 per cent. Apart from the
C.S.R , which stands by itself, there is no Sydney co. in
this list that has paid a dividend in its last term. The
only cos. that have given any return are in States (South
Australia and Victoria) which have for some years past
consistently protected their young industries.

South German G.M. (Vic.) propose to issue 24,000 contri-

snoulders of the new investors—if they can get them.
Opposition to this shifting policy has been raised by
some shareholders, who rightly reckon that if the
V*. enough for further working the men
who drew big divs. in the past should be content to stick
their speculation. South Germans were saleable at
w “en The Bulletin sounded the first note of alarm,
.the stock is now quoted at 12s. to 14s.

Bendigo’s output for August (25,5420 z.) was,
with one exception, the best for any month since 1876.
S. New Moon is still leading, with 23720 z. ; then Ulster
United, 21500 z. (from 872 tons); New Moon, 1401 oz. ;
Hustlers No. 1,11360 z. ; and Great Northern, lOOloz.
The month’s dividends were £28,728.

£ll,OOO, of which £7200 was paid by S. New Moon and New
Moon cos., the former figuring wiLh its boss div. to date—
£4Boo. Four mines on this line—the Northern continua-
tion of the Garden Gully—paid £10,050. S.N. Moon and
New Moon mines are looking better than ever. A recent
visitor expressed the opinion that there ought not to be
less than 300 stampers at work on the Clarence, S.N.
Moon and New Moon group, but the Bendigonians have
not yet got beyond the idea that there is no reason why
they should do more than “ father” did.

A NEW USE FOR THE BARMAID.
(The Sanitary Communion Service).
A strong protest is being raised against the dangerous and in-
sanitary practice whereby the communicants all drink out of the
same cup. A separate drinking vessel for each communicant is abso-
lutely necessary to prevent the spread of lip cancer and other diseases.
Broken Hill had settled itself into a dull
despair; uncaring, fondly thinking that Fate had
done its worst, and that anything else that could
happen wouldn’t interest it worth tuppence.
But, alas, the worst had yet to come—an intimation
from both the Relief Board-and the S, Australian
Government that they couldn’t supply the
breweries with water any longer. But the woe
in the faces of the inhabitants when this news
came softened the wrath of Heaven, and rain fell.
So the beer was saved.
•** * *
D.T.’s Saturday article on the “Bacillus
of Laziness” is a good example of Yes-No. It
says :
The lack of capacity for sustaining energy in the Irish-
man—of course only in Ireland —may fairly be referred to
the congenital-government combination, one of the
proofs being that when the Irishman emigrates and comes
under a new environment, his natural tendency towards
inertia is often overcome, and he becomes a power in the
land of his adoption.
And a little further on :
There are good grounds for submitting that in propor-
tion as a Government encourages its people to rely upon
the State for employment, instead of relying upon their
own efforts, that Government, pro tanto, encourages the
habit of national laziness with all its attendant evils.
Irishmen in Ireland do not rely upon the State for
employment. The Telegraph assumes they do,
and therefore are lazy. Irishmen in Australia are
prone to rely on the State. The Telegraph
assumes they are not, and are therefore energetic.
The argument of the article, by the way, is that
State Socialism makes people lazy.
A warning from. Melbourne :
A variant of the “ walking ” craze commenced in Mel-
bourne Sept. Ist, and threatens to eventuate in Sydney
on the 19th. This is the attempt of one A. H. Coppock
to cover the 600 miles between the capitals in 19 days.
Coppock seems to have undertaken the contract partly
to advertise a new patent method of infantry equipment,
designed by himself, and partly to win the usual wager.
Anyhow, he set out from Melbourne Stock Exchange at
11.30 a.m. on the Ist, carrying a rifle, a neatly-strapped
knapsack with a new pair of boots attached, and a good-
sized packet of Squashed Oats or Frayed Corn, or some
patent food of that sort. Tie is understood to be a
vegetarian, teetota gentleman, and to be in the habit of
camping out and walking long distances on little tucker.
Nobody seems to know the size of the alleged wager, or
what will happen to Coppock if he gets a lift; but, if a
footsore person in kharki, with a dilapidated billy and an
empty packet of Sprained Beans, is noticed toiling down
Pitt-street, Sydney, on the morning of Saturday, the
19th, the populace is hereby requested to stand off and
admire.
• • » *
Waverley (Sydney) cemetery trustees
acted quite rightly in refusing to allow their
field of the dead to be used as a meeting place
for people wishing to orate over the ’9B Irish
rebellion. In the first place, there is no shadow
of reason why the memory of the old rows of the
old world should be kept alive in Australia. In
the second place, anything that tends to discourage
self-advertising posturers from plastering with
their posters the bones of the dead is good.
The ’9B celebration is 10 per cent. Irish
patriotism (and in this country there should
only be room for Australian patriotism), 80 per
cent, notoriety-hunting (and for that there is no
call to make room anywhere), and 10 per cent,
damage to the cemetery premises. In the third
place, the dead deserve a rest.
* * * *
The Wesleyans and Presbyterians threaten
to unite in Australia. If they do, how will the
combined sect wear its whiskers ? The Wesleyan
monkey shave is a trade - mark. So is the
Presbyterian grizzled beard—the Presbyterian
seems mostly tough and elderly. The best hope
is that the Wesleyans will adopt the Presbyterian
style, for the Wesleyan chin is a sad, lone, de-
nuded hillside that reflects on Art, and kills
Poetry at the first glance.

* * * *
That angry attorney, E. R. Abigail, of
Sydney, was suppressed with great thoroughness
by Stipendiary MacFarlane the other day. When
the attorney refused to be a gooi little boy and
sit down when told, the magistrate had him
deposited outside by two policemen. Abigail
waved his brolly and dared the policemen to lay a
hand on him, but the officers, evidently being of
opinion that Abigail was only dangerous with
his mouth, disregarded the impending brolly and
Abigail left. If Magistrate MacFarlane would
only put his official foot down as heavily on the
dirty, insulting Police Court attorneys who try to
confuse women witnesses by asking them indecent
and irrelevant questions as to their relations with
imaginary men, he would establish a better claim
to a statue than half the statesmen who have been
immortalised in marble and other mineral sub-
stances. No man could have a better epitaph
than “ He made our Police Courts clean.”

An inquest on a patient who died, at
Sunbury (Vic.) Lunatic Asylum, of erysipelas—the
third within a month—elicited the pleasing
information that the solitary night nurse had
charge of six dormitories containing no less than
247 patients. What a hideous farce !

Governor Rawson has recommended the
Imperial Government to appoint a schoolmaster
to Norfolk Island. That is roundabout business,
but seems inevitable till the island comes under
the Commonwealth. Hampden appointed Mr,
Arnold, of N.S.W. Public Instruction Depart-
ment, and sent him to the island. The office is a
small, badly-paid one, and subject to sudden local
whirlwinds of abuse. Also, the depressing social
atmosphere makes the schoolmaster grow into an
eccentric being. One master turned Seventh Day
Adventist and refused to teach the Church of
England'-Catechism. Another went over to the
Free Methodists, and so on. Hampden fixed the
salary at £l4O and fees, latter mostly paid in fruit
and vegetables—“garden truck” as the Americans
sometimes put it.
• • • •
August 25th was the centenary of the first
church (known as “The Wattle and Dab”) in
Australia. It stood about where Tattersall’s
Chambers now are, at the corner of Hunter and
Castlereagh-streets, Sydney, and was built by Par-
son Johnson, at his own expense, cost being
£67 12s. 11 Id. The quaint bill is published in
Bon wick “First Preacher,” and shows how care-
ful the parson was to keep an aoconnt of every
halfpenny expended. The wages were paid in
SDanish dollars, rum, flour and assorted provisions.
When the parson handed his bill to the Acting
Governor —Grose —that military martinet refused
to pay it on the ground that Johnson ought to
provide his own church. The British Government
ordered.the payment of the bill, but the convicts
burned down the edifice on October 1, 1799.
* • • •
On Eight Hours’ Day in Adelaide the
unemployed paraded at the rear of the procession
of labor societies. They carried an improvised
banner on which was painted a coffin (labelled
“ Public Works ”) and a crossed pick and shovel
instead of the conventional thigh bones. Taken
altogether, the Labor display was the best for
years, but things to eat and drink were its most
prominent features. Butchers, bakers and brewers
constituted the biggest part of the turnout, and a
country has to get far ahead of beef, beer and
public works before its industries are much to
boast of.
There were recently auctioned in Sj r dney
three handsome and costly chandeliers, imported
from England, 50 years ago by Daniel Cooper
for his then residence in Macquarie-street.
They were allowed to remain in their original
cases all these years, till recently discovered by
the trustees in store. Dan Cooper’s house in ■
the fifties was that now adjoining the Colonial
Secretary’s office, opposite Government House
gates. When he took up his residence in
England the house became the home of the Civil
Service Club, of which C. A. Goodchap was
honorary secretary. Then Robert Gray, soft-
goodsman, became a tenant, and made way for
the International Exhibition Commissioners, they
in turnmaking way for the Reform Club, of which
old Jack Robertson was the tin god. When
Robertson died tlie club died also. Then Dan
Cooper’s “ town house ” degenerated fora time
to the usual semi-swell hash house, and is now an
office of the Commonwealth Government.

taken from them, and they mourned for him as
for a brother.
* • • •
A cat at-home was held recently in Hobart.
It was a very special affair, and the invitations
were sent out on embossed cards to all the first-
family cats in the capital. The Government
* fuse Thomas was brought round by a well-
oiled fiunkey, and things went smoothly enough
until Mr. Havelock started to behave rudely
to a Maltese tabby, of irreproachable ante-
cedents. A shocking scramble ensued. The
spittings and yowlings sounded like another
Mafeking revival, and hair and bristles flew
around profusely. One cat owner received a biff
on the head from a Thomas as large as a calf, and
a ferocious looking she-puss fixed her claws in her
mistress’ back hair. The cause of the trouble was
carried out, yelling profusely, and it is not con-
sidered probable that he will be invited to future
Hobart cat gatherings.

North Sydney Cemetery of St. Thomas,
now likely to be obliterated by the progress of
settlement, has been a home of the dead for nearly
60 years. The site was given by Alexander Beiry,
who owned vast areas around there.
He himself lies buried there, and his
wife was the first person to receive
a grave in the place.

WOLVES.
“That young feller there forkin’ to your sister,
burrin’ meself, is the most dangerous man in
these parts with you'se women.”
Another bit of old Sydney is about to
come down—-the house in George-street, near
King-street, where, in 1836, Thomas Ware Smart
commenced business as auctioneer, &c. He had
been employed for six years in one of the leading
commercial houses, and “thought it time to com-
inence for himself.” He became manager of the
Sydney Bank, which was a failure, but worked
himself into a fortune eventually. He built the
mansion “Mona” on the Darling Point Road,
where be bad a fine collection of pictures. Asa
politician, lie became Treasurer twice, under
Cowper, and was Secretary for Works under the
same slippery politician. In the next house, in
George-street, another Colonial Treasurer, C. E.
Weekes, carried on an ironmongery business.
That little stretch of George-street, between King
and Hunter streets, supplied N.S.W. at various
times with no less than seven Treasurers.
LADY MILLERSPOINT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M. L.
Lady of the dingy hat,
Cadging from the passer-by 1
Once at swell hotels you sab
Dining with the lords on high.
That was when the gods would cry,
Dazzled by your diamonds fat:
“ Look at Tottie, ain’t she fly 1 "
Lady of the dingy hat.
Lady of the ragged cloak
Leering at the passer-by 1
One time you were wont to soak
Heidseick Ouiv4e—extra dry.
Now you have to keep your eye
On the beer, for fear the “ bloke ”
Might get up and “ do a guy ”
With it—lady of the cloak.
Lady of the loose-lipped mouth,
Smiling at the passer-by !
Hair that pointeth north and south,
Beery wink and rheumy eye.
Yet the Johnnies used to sigh,
Ere these days of gin and drouth,
If sSme other one were nigh
Kissing of your red-lipped mouth.
R.A.A.
THE WEED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gold flowers are all a-glitter
Down the mountain side,
Showered in fragrant litter
For a poet’s bride !
The poet, nestling near it,
Importunes his love ;
And the sun-breeze and the blossoms
Bear her whisper from above.
When lo ! upon that poet
Looms a tall silk hat!
A loquacious luminary
Of the legion that is fat.
And he kicks the shimmering petals,
That the poet’s eye bewitch ;
“ But, for these, my vast possessions
Might with millions me enrich.
You perceive the noxious weed, Sir?”
And the poet answers “ Which ?"

GIRL O' MINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Where are you now, sweet girl o’ mine ?
With your witching ways and your eyes a-shine
Like stars that glimmer athwart the pine.
I miss your voice and I miss your face,
You cans no more to the trysting-place,
And, looking in \ tin for your bright milk-pail,
The cows ar .“ waiting by the old slip rail.”
“ I’m over in Sydney, boy o’ mine,
At a grand hotel where my jewels shine,
Behind the bar where the dandies pine ;
And I’ll come no more to the trysting-tree
For a bagman is going to marry me.
And nothing I care for your old, old tale,
And I loathe the cows and the old slip-rail.”

erected to someone’s memory in St. James’s-
square, Sydney. As a matter of fact, very few
of the so-called ornamental-useful fountains in
Sydney are useful. They are almost all out of

SCHNAPPS
In putting some grievances in connection
with the administration of the Old Age Pensions
Act before the N.S.W. Treasurer reoently, a
deputation gave some quaint particulars of alleged
red-tapeism arising out of the distribution of the
weekly dole. Alluding to the proof of death
required by the Board, Griffith, M.L.A., stated
that the Lands Department wrote to a woman in
the country, and referred to her husband as “the
deceased.” On the husband indignantly denying
his alleged defunctness, the Department replied
that his unsupported testimony on that point
couldn’t be accepted. Then Hollis, M.L.A., gave
a case of another woman whose husband suddenly
faded away about 1878, and hasn’t been seen or
heard of since. Before the Board will now give
the deserted woman a pension, it requires her to
give definite information of her vanished old
man’s present position, or proof of his demise 1
Regarding the clause in the Act which requires
that a person who has been in receipt of £1 per
week for the year prior to his application must
wait twelve months before the O.A.P. can
be granted, one speaker instanced a case of an
o’d man, formerly in receipt of 30s. per week as a
lift attendant, being discharged and told to apply
for the pension. On applying, however, he
learned that he had to be practically destitute for
12 months before he could get the dole, and how
to live during those 12 months of destitution is a
problem of great dimensions.
Commenting on the fact that the N.S.W.
Railway Commissioners refuse to extend the
penny section of the eastern suburbs trams, the
S.M. Herald says an enterprising omnibus pro-
prietor got over the difficulty by running a penny
’bus. The Bulletin’s opinion of the penny ’bus
is that it needs the interference of a Humane
Society. The institution, as seen in opposition to
the tram system, is a scandal to Australians, who
hitherto have been regarded as being especially
attached to the horse. But the fat dame and the
straw-hatted individual who sit on an over loaded
’bus while the driver flogs mercilessly at his under-
fed team is (thank God!) not a type of the true
Australian. The true type comes from the Snowy
River—and elsewhere.

Melbourne magistrate Panton has an
unhappy knack of advising those brought under
his notice in his beak-like capacity to go into the
country and get work. Recently he ordered a
man who was getting 30s. a week to go into the
country and increase his weekly earnings. If
Panton had a fortnight searching for work, with
liis swag up, an offer of 10s. a week and the
manner in which his first pint of flour would be
handed out to him would break up his theory
about going into the country as a panacea for all
t vils.

The Anglican clergy of Bob-Cab Smith’s
diocese propose to appeal to Bob-Cab Smith to
exclude clergymen with less than twelve months’
looal residence from all appointments worth over
£3OO a year. It is a disgrace to Australia that
such a regulation should be required, but it is
required—very badly. The usual process in the
profession with the gaiters and the amazing joke
of a hat is this: First an English bishop is
imported, it being understood that no Australian
can address the Deity with the proper British
bleat. The English bishop comes here, says
“ haw ” on landing, and tries hard to con-
ceal his pity for those of the inferior
clergy who are mere Australians. And then
he brings out his English friends and hangers-on
to fill the best available billets, and they also say
“ haw ” on landing, and the Deity is, to some
extent, addressed with the correct Oxford bleat in
these parts, which, doubtless, prevents Him feel-
ing lonely. Most of these imported Bishops are
shockingly dull old hacks with unpicturesque
whiskers, and Australia could grow quite as com-
petent prelates locally—but the bleat has to be
considered.
“F.C.” : Anyone ever notice what costly
boots the average larrikin or spieler habitually
wears ? Lately made one of a car-load travel-
ling out towards the Richmond (Melbourne)
pony races. Nearly every man of the
push possessed a face like a broken
battle-axe and a head like a sterile
cannon-ball. They were plainly of
the loafing, spieling, living-any-
how class that pervades Bourke-
street and defaces Nature. Most
wore slouch hats and so-so
clothes, but nine out of ten
had paid—or borrowed—at
least from 15s. to 30s. for
their boots, or had stolen boots
of that value. The only pair
of cheap stand overs (about
7s. 6d. worth) were on the
feet of a counter-jumper work-
ing out that way. Certainly
the hard-faced brigade could
v have found a full week’s work
for a snob —some of the boots were
in the last stages of senile decay—but
they had originally been the choicest
American importations. And the owners
wore the fierce expression of men who were going-
to steal more valuable boots immediately.

This belated letter seems to have arrived
by gradual whaleboat from Norfolk Island :
Writer of par. (8., 6/6/’O3) is off the mark. Governor
Rawson is not the first Governor to visit Norfolk Island
since Robinson in ’74. Islanders have had visits from
Loftus, Carrington, Hampden, and Beauchamp. Also,
Robinson did not reduce the fines men had to pay for
parentage without marriage. Long after his visit men
were fined the same old £lO, and labored on public works
to amount of fine. It was never paid in cash. The
new laws enforced by Colonel Spalding under Governor
Hampden did away with the fine, 2s. 6d. per week for the
mother being the new order. The new arrangement has
not changed the ways of the islanders. At a public
meeting, Chief Magistrate King in the chair, Bishop
Wilson stated that the majority of the births were of
illegitimate children, and not one of the after spiakers
objected to the statement, but all said they thought
“ something should be done.” There are no liquor shops
on the island ;no overcrowded cities; no slums ; and
parsons by the score. Norfolk Island morals should be
above reproach, but they aren't.

Bendigo, Victoria’s crack goldfield, leaves
its public institutions to drift nowadays. The
hospital shows a deficiency for the twelve months
of £1196; the benevolent asylum £786, and the
mechanics’ institute £777- The same week as
these balance-sheets were put forward three mines
alone paid £6824 in dividends.

N.S.W. Government proposes to deal
with the judicial personages who indulge on the
Bench in, foolish revilings
of the laws they are sup-
posed to respect and ad-
minister, and in other
silly verbal gambolings,
by an Act of Parlia-
ment. Surely that size
hammer is not needed to
kill the insect. A Cabi-
net minute, with pub-
licly expressed censure
of offenders, should
serve well enough. If
an individual Supreme
or District Court Judge
set at defiance the
minute, Parliament could
deal with him separately;
all other judicial persons are
amenable to Cabinet discipline.
To attempt to pass an Act means
to shelve the question. A stern
Cabinet minute could be penned and
passed in an hour, and would be just _
as efficacious, probably, in regard to a Judge s
excursions into politics as a stipendiary magis-
trate’s tolerance of smut and billingsgate.
Justice Denniston, of Maoriland, recently
defined the three stages of love as, “ Walking out,
keeping company, and engaged.” Whereupon a
scoffer wrote to a Christchurch paper to say that
his Honor didn’t know what he was _ talking
about, and defined the three as “Walking out,
lying-in, and the Destitute Persons Act. And
in a good many cases the scoffer is right.

A pen-picture by “ P.” :
Went to Sydney Protestant Hall the other night to see
G. H. Reid crowned with the laurel wreath of splendid
victory by applauding thousands of Australians, fired to
madness at the wrong done them by the Barton Govern-
ment, and intoxicated with their own avenging of that
wrong. I got there ; but didn’t find any enthusiastic thou-
sands—there might have been 300 listless persons. When
the poll was declared someone said, “ Good boy, George ! ”
someone laughed, and two straw-hatted youths on the
kerb said, “hurray” so unexpectedly that a cab-horse
was visibly startled. Then through the lighted window
protruded George—vaguely reminiscent of some enshrined
and corpulent saint seen in a picture book of
childhood—and his drawling voice somehow seemed only
a melancholy monotone, and a long, long way off. No
dash ; no triumphant note—the voice of a disappointed
man. This was the end of it all 1 After all his efforts,
his theatrical resignation, his “largest meetings ever
held,” his press booming, and general riot and fury—
only some 2000 electors had been enthused to voting pitch;
only some3oogatheredtoseetheend of thecomedy. The
situation was bathetic, and George Reid, with all his effron-
tery, felt it so. His vivacity fell from him like a discarded
mantle, leaving him naked and fat and almost ashamed.
His one desperate stroke for redemption, which might
have been finely dramatic with George at his best—his
challenge to Barton for the Maitland seat—was made
with forced enthusiasm, and aroused only a perfunctory
cheer from the audience. So tame and forceless was Reid
that in the unknown candidate who spoke after him
appeared a rugged vitality that stirred one’s pulses as
does the spirited quick-step after the dirge at a military
funeral.
* * * *
A young Sydney music-teacher who
suicided by inhaling gas left these remarks
behind her:
“ Look after mother, never mind about me. Burn me
up or nail me down, just as you please. Perhaps the
Government would do it. If they will, I will canvass for
them, also get them reserve seats, and also ask a few
cherubs to come down to this delightful place and swell
the population, which is decreasing in such a painful
manner. I will say ‘ good-bye ’ now, and make still one
less on this beautiful planet. It is a great wonder that
anyone should wish to quit it. I expect I shall haunt it
for a while, although I have not had a very pleasant time.
Again, please look after mother, and that’s all.”
The population isn’t decreasing, but all these
anxious newspaper writings about the “declining
birth-rate ” have got on the public’s nerves. Even
unmarried suicides now worry in their last
moments about the birth-rate.
• * * *
King Ned’s visit to Vienna has a Gilberbian
flavor. At the banquet the British monarch
solemnly created the Austrian Emperor a field
marshal of the British army. Cable states that
Francis Joseph received the honor in silence, but
bent forward and pressed the King’s hand.
Perhaps he was too full for words. In all the
years when the British army bore a good
reputation Francis Joseph wasn’t even made an
honorary corporal. And now, when the ‘ ‘ rag-
ging ” scandals, and the incompetence shown in
S’Africa, and the fact that it has been found
necessary to lower the height standard so as to
admit dwarfs of five feet, have made things
unpleasant, Edward decides that the army has got
down to about his friend’s level. Francis Joseph
comes in as a five-foot recruit, .so to speak. No
wonder the Austrian couldn’t think of any appro-
priate remark.
AS IT SHOULD BE.
The parsons mostly belong to the colored labor
party, and assure us that the coffee-colored person
is an equal and a brother. Yet they never show
t heir faith by picturing heaven as anything like
this.
The charge againsb Coroner Ramsay Smith,
of Adelaide, seems to be principally that he took
a number of skeletons, to which he had no just
claim, and sent them to the Royal College of
Surgeons, at Edinburgh. The taking of the
skeletons seems no great matter, but the sending
of them to Britain, when Australian students are
so much in need of bones to study, seems (if it
were done) a shameful robbery. The fact that
Britain sends us bishops is no excuse ; let Britain
keep the bishops while we retain the bones.
BE CONTENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said the preacher, “ Be content,
Pay your taxes, pay your rent;
Earthly things you must despise,
Think of mansions in the skies !
Though your back with work is bent,
Be content, man, be content ! ”
Then the preacher onward went
To the man who gets the rent.
Feasting, preacher laughed elate,
For this man has riches great ;
They that earthly things revere
Often meet the preacher here.
Be content, man, be content,
Till your race on earth is spent;
Work and strive, then go and die,
All for mansions in the sky !
Mansions here are very rare—
All may have them in the air.
N.S.W.
LUKR KAY.
PHILOSOPHY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ ’Tis better to have loved and lost
Than not have loved at all ”
An adage old as Pentecost,
The Jewish festival.
’Tis better to have loved and laughed
To drown the sob of pain,
And know that passion’s fiery draught
Has wrecked your hopes again.
Ah, well, these men who speak, perchance,
They know not how to woo !
But I have led the devil’s dance
And danced with angels too ;
Have by a hundred loves been crossed,
And proved by everyone
’Tis better to have loved and lost
Than to have loved and won.
N.S.W.
C.C.
