RAINCOATS.
“CRAVEN ETTES”
Are the Ideal Fabrics
for
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 8,1903.
My Dear Arint.—
Sydney District Nursing Association is a charity
that sends out trained nurses to attend the sick
poor in their homes, and teach them that sunlight
in a bedroom is not the noxious thing they seem
to think it, and that the baby should not be fed
on bacon, tea, and hot bread. It must be a smart
charity, to judge by the names of the men and
women on the executive committee for the fUe in
its aid, held in State Government House and its
grounds. I commend it to the notice of women
who aspire to be first over the fence in the pursuit
of the Correct Thing.
D.N.A.’s fete was opened by State Governor
Rawson on Friday, and his and Admiral Fan-
shawe s daughter, and the gilded aides and private
secs., and all the people who dine at Government
House, took a hand at keeping shops and carrying
swindling bags. Miss Rawson had worked a
table centre of white satin fenced with chiffon
ramparts, for which there was great competition.
On the Rawson-Fanshawe table was a little water-
color—a view of the Campagna by Yay de Vaya,
the titled priest without a collection-plate, who
is now stopping at State Government House. It
was modestly priced at a guinea.
Best side-show of the fete was the floral go-cart
Sarade, organised by Miss Eadith Walker and
Irs. Arthur Cox. An infant was the fare in each
floral cart, and there was an older child between
the shafts. Lovely youngsters, most of them.
Toddler Fairfax, a blue-eyed, flaxen-haired, pink-
cheeked boy, showed as one of the best articles that
can be credited to the S.M. Herald. The Lachlan
Beaton girl and boy, grandchildren of the late
W. B. Dailey, were pony and fare of a hyacinth-
covered cart. The hoods in most of the baby-carts
had been removed and skeleton-umbrellas, covered
with flowers substituted. The first prize went to
a daffodil-covered carriage. Around the hood of
one cart there was a thick fringe of the narrowest
baby-ribbon, about 6in. deep, and the end of
every ribbon was tasselled with a daffodil bloom.
Imagine the labor! It was a proud hour for the
smart woman with infants at the picturesque ages
of from two to six years, and she stood around,
conscious, holding items of baby’s kit, whilst
nurse walked by the side of the vehicle to keep
the Reginald or Ethel in the shafts on the narrow
carpet road.
A bal masque (French for playing the fool in a
Guy Fawkes visor) is a great study. When a man
gets ambushed in a domino and a mask, and
imagines that his identity is hidden, he is tempted
to get out of the straight and narrow path that
leads from his boarding house to his office, and act
as if he had gas on the brain. A masked and
fancy dress ball, in aid of the Waitara Foundling
Home, was held at Sydney Town Hall last Friday.
The galleries were packed with Superior Persons
(who paid 2s. a head) and who averred, as they
gazed down at the motley, that “they loved to
see that class of person enjoying itself, and besides
it was for a worthy charity.” Whisky must hold
an important place in this sorry scheme of things,
for all the best dresses and best-looking women
were advertisements for this or that fire-water.
Black and White ” whisky was represented
by a gazelle girl wearing a dress of black-
and-white satin decorated with all the brands
and ear-marks of that non-temperance drink.
On her head she, wore a bottle that should
have held an electric spark, but early in
the evening the illumination failed. An in-
teresting item in the passing show was “Coley”
Burke, who sponsors “ Black and White,” chasing
his advertisement to learn why she didn’t carry a
light at her mast-head.
Quite £6OO will be realised by the Cranbrook
fete in aid of the D.N. Association. Am using
the future tense because Mrs. B. R. Wise still has
a lot of pottery, cooking pots, jars, and tea-sets to
dispose of at reasonable prices. Write to her at
Darlinghurst.
Three sisters in dominoes were an admired
trinity, and the Pianola Co. advertised itself with
two negro boy employees, the real Sambo article,
smartly dressed and tied together with wide
white satin ribbon. Mr. Facing-Both-Ways was
an ingenious get-up. He wore a wig combed over
his face and a face on the back of his head, and
his frantic feet toed north and south at one and
the same time. “The .lolly Dogs,” a group of
eight persons wearing dogs’ heads and furry coats,
were most melancholy animals. They couldn’t
bark or gambol worth a dog biscuit. A lately-
returned-from-London singer and writer was an
ace of clubs with a little mask, but his smile
betrayed him, and to his disgust he was addressed
by name. Amongst the crowd in evening dress
was a lady who must have meant to represent
The Naked Truth. It was a beautiful display,
but it was very disconcerting to those who like
Truth with some bodice to her gown. Some one
ought to go to a fancy dress ball as our National
Debt. Many Sydney women could look the part
straight off; one taking the part ought to be
2001bs. in weight and wear the figures £200,000,000
above her shoulder-blades. Hughes, Mayor and
Mayoress, came along ; so did some members of
the Minister of Works family. H. J. Quinlan
and C. G. Hepburn, honorary organising secs, will
hand about £4OO to the charity.
Some cheerful Cherokee distributed hundreds of
leaflets during progress of Waitara charity dance.
These contained a poem by himself, dedicated
without permission, to Mrs. See, and a picture
illustrating same. A young lady with an old-time
Sydney jaw stands waving a “hanky” ataman
who approaches on a bike. In the background
v ictor -Daley sits on a piece of firewood, evidently
m great pain, and drawing what appears to be a
six-inch nail out of his ear. His left hand clutches
a Piece of paper, so perhaps the nail is a pencil,
ana the pain a poem. Several starved chickens
roost on adjacent) fences, and the poem invites you
to desert the madding throng in favor of the
society of gill-birds and magpies.
In Sydney, nowadays, anybody can have a ride
behind the black horses of Heath, for funeral nags
and carnages are let out on hire to the public for
occasions, and you can go to your
wedding and your grave behind the same pair of
neddies. It was probably to advertise the livery
business,, and not the actual undertaking, that a
Sydney firm of coffin-fillers was represented at
Waitara masked ball by a party of eight—four
men, who wore black clothes and tall hats, and
carried white-beribboned whips, and four women
in black and mauve. It was a shuddersome
feature of the evening—dancing for foundlings
with the Heath mutes hovering around.
Talking of fashionable charities: The Homoeo-
pathic Hospital is one without a doubt. State-
Governor Rawson and his family are said to be
amongst those who physic themselves with a wine-
glass of water taken from a five hundred gallon
tank into which has been dropped half a speck of
something out of a small phial. (The great secret
of homoeopathy seems to lie in boiling the tail of a
germ in a train-load of water and calling the
mixture soup.) Sydney Homo Hospital is
acknowledging its subs for the past year, and I
see that Eadith Walker gave £lOO. She has a
hospital of her own to support; all the same .£lOO
is less to the millionairess than is the Rawson
subscription of £5 to the donors.
A friend sends me this letter
. Sydney Telegraph’s tale of the Jennings millions
fizzling out, and soon the news may come
that there are no millions, and possibly that there was
never any Jennings. The latest announcement on the
subject is made by a man at Nowra (N.S.W.), who seems
to be some outlying section of the Jennings clan. He
says that he tracked the estate to its lair long ago, and
found the lair empty and the property not at home.
According to him the estate is not in Chancery at all,
but was divided up among the legal heirs some 60 years
since. Also, it is alleged that owing to a rush of Jen-
ningses, or some such claimants, and the consequent
disturbance and law expenses, all further claimants were
shut out by Act of Parliament, as was done with the
lichborne estates—even if the lost Tichborne baronet
turned up now and proved his identity he not only could
l e l ov % r es tates, but he couldn’t sue for them.
What the Jennings estate amounted to when it existed
doesn’t appear.
The habit of being an unexpected heir to vast
wealth is evidently quite incurable in this country.
On top of the excitement about the “Jennings
millions,’ which Sydney Telegraph writes about
so confidingly, somebody at Parramatta has now
discovered that he is inheritor of a three-fourths
share in a 300-acre property in the heart of
.London. The whole estate is worth .£30,000 000
or thereabouts ; so the Parramatta man is to come
in for £22,500,000, on a rough estimate. Just now
f * remember that anybody died of late and
left £30,000,000 worth of Droperty in London,
but probably the news of the event was crowded
out of the papers to make room for a dog-show
item. These great fortunes appear to be mostly
managed on the irresponsible principles of the
melodrama, where the policeman carries the lost
estate around in his tail-pocket, or the boundless
wealth is left in the kitchen sink till called for.
Hoes any Sydneysider remember a Mdlle. Mag-
deleine Cauchet, a young and pretty Frenchwoman,
who was here, so she says, less than four years
ago as a lecturer and fashionable teacher of
1 rench ? Mdlle. has just enjoyed a little news-
paper boom in Paris, for she has been giving an
account of the world’s tour she undertook without
a sou in her pocket. She gave French lessons,
and lectured and exhibited her extensive Univer-
sity diplomas, and Society hugged her to its
crepe-de-chine breast. Thus she passed through
G-reat Britain, America, South Africa and Aus-
tralia. .In Australia she did not need to advertise
for pupils; they simply rushed in upon her, so
she says. What wonderful things some people
can do without a sou in their pockets ! Not
everyone knows that you can hire halls, advertise,
take long railway journeys, and live at the best
hotels, in a penniless condition.
Edith Yeo, the plaintiff in the breach of pro-
mise suit against young grazier George Fox, is a
small, quiet, somewhat anaemic-looking girl. She
was. on the verge of fainting at least a dozen times
during the course of her evidence. Those who
were in court and heard the trial are delighted
with the verdict. Newspaper reports of the
evidence hardly do the girl justice. Her
refusal to part with her infant told in her favor.
Hefendant vowed fie had no money, “ not a bean,”
and that he had to sell for £250 his interest in his
father’s will to pay the Attorney-General, who
defended him. Fox is a superior-looking sort of
hayseed, with prominent lips; he was a sailor
until lately. Having to find £lBOO will probably
make his mouth assume an extra prominence that
will last all his life.
“ It is fashionable to be dead now, so I ought to
be happy.” Extract from the letter left by a
music-teacher girl who committed suicide last
week. Be fashionable and you’ll be happy, was
perhaps the poor little woman’s creed. But she
neglected to add instructions as to fashion of her
burying robe—whether it should “flare” with a
shaped flounce and be trained or otherwise. She
also entreats “whoever comes in first” (so the
letter begins) “to look after mother.” In her
own case “ looking after mother ” meant keeping
on at music-teaching, and the teacher died in
preference. I suppose it is better to die than to
teach music but judging by Ethel Cavanagh’s full
letter she had been reading cheap romances.
State Governor Rawson has been visiting Bee-
croft to open a “village” fete. Beecroft is a
truly rural spot 12 miles from the city, and there
the bee sucks and the wild thyme grows, and the
gentle lark soars from his dew> nest, and the dan-
delions are bigger than elsewhere. It is full of
orchards, and bee - farms, and persons whose
parents and grandparents came from “villages”
and who haven’t yet outgrown the use of the
word. But it charmed Captain Share, the State
Lx. s private secretary, so much that he would
not object to a permanent lair at Beecroft.
Once upon a time,. some few years ago, a lady
with money and social aspirations but no social
sponsors came to Sydney from Possum Flat and
made several attempts to become one of the set
that has learned to say “ haw.” But the set gave
Berthe non possumus shoulder, which is corpse-
cold. So she halted awhile and watched Things
with the result that she got on to the committee of
a fashionable charity and paid liberally for doing
all the work and giving the other women all the
credit. Now she is in England, and her children
are learning to say “haw” right in the great
“ haw ” factory itself, and Australian Somebodies
visiting London are grateful if they receive a card
for one of her “ At Homes.”
Sydney Society is waiting hungrily for the
announcement of two matrimonial engagements.
At present it is not quite certain. One engage-
ment is connected with vice-regal circles ; the
other is that of a richly-left youth in the brewery
line and a mercantile heiress.
An Australian wedding at Pretoria, Transvaal,
took place last April. Bridegroom was Mr.
Francis R. Hays, of Townsville (N.Q.), and bride
Miss Rose Dean, of Coogee. G-iver-away of bride
was Mr. Hillier-Tapp, of Sydney. Amongst
those present was a Mrs. Simpsonne. My ! Isn’t
that a high holiday way to spell a very work-a-
day name ? Smith spelt “ Pjsmythee” now has a
mate.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The increase of Golfing-fever has no better
criterion than the league-long list of entries for
Sandringham this week. Some hundreds are
pledged to play champions from other States and
from all the crack links of the country.
“ A characteristic of the teeming country
throngs ‘ Showing ’ last week was defective teeth.
Scarcely a rural face broke into a laugh but there
was a shock to the onlooker’s aesthetic system, by
reason of the molar ruin presented, or the crude
dental fixtures depending like monuments from
the jaw. The townie’s teeth are a well-attended
matter, hence the thriving of the Collins-street
dentists, but our country cousins leave the matter
m the hands of a neglectful Providence or a local
stump-dealer.
“Wandering feminines found two exhibits to
grip their tired attention in the vast Flemington
show this year. One was a garden hat made from
the sheath of a Queensland pine-tree flower, and
the other a tall flower-basket amazingly wrought
out of Romsey butter by a lady resident of the
district. The hat was labelled, “ Been in use for
18 months.” It was light and satiny—rather
nicer than the Panama. The butter-basket was a
perfect structure. Its dahlias, roses and violets
were realistic in form, and the basket itself plaited
like the favorite china design.
‘[Aide Browning didn’t have to remain in
splints and plaster for very long. He made a
rapid recovery just as the sympathetic Tooraki-
ennes were devising sick-room comforts, and on
Wednesday he limped to the Show. At the same
time a very sick-looking brother of the Y.R.
staff, Private Secretary Fitzpatrick, strolled
through the kiosks and the tents of the prize
poultry and poodles on his own. Also, squeezing
a way through the general jam, were the Cliveden
dowager, Miss Hilda Cumming, massive Mrs.
Bowes Kelly, Mrs. V. Manifold, and dozens more
of ‘ the best and brightest ’ in squatteratic cirdes.
Lady Tennyson having found a navy-blue costume
and a cake-tin toque with cowslip clusters at the
brim that suits her well, she now uses no other—
like the man in the soap advertisement. At
three of the week’s crushes she has sported the
marine-hued turn-out.
“ The rural population doesn’t take any pride in
Vice-Regal display. This was particularly notice-
able at the agricultural show when the flashing
out-riders, military guard, and grand carriage
arrived on the scene. Ben and Betty gave a grin,
a jerk of the thumb that meant a heap of things,
and proceeded to inspect the prize swine or the
model Ayrshires. At the departure of the greater

“An odd pair taking a momentous interest in
the Show, one of the days, consisted of dapper
Ernest Collins and Miss Susie Vaughan—the
comedy star. Theatricals, as a rule, abhor the
noisy, smelly jostle of the yearly agricultural
event at Flemington. The man who dots take
a rollicking delight in the babel and the general
hustle is the padre. Every day you butt into the
ruddy clerics, their vests bulging, their watch
chains straining over the wide cloth area, generally
in the circle of the bhoys of 1 the parish.
“The Agricultural Show reminds me of an
idealist, who left a desk for farm-work because he
wanted to ‘get near to Nature’s heart and look
into the clear eyes of the sons of the soil.’ He
soon came back to town, and when his friends
asked, ‘ What price the’ sons of the soil ? ’ he
answered, ‘ The brutes all have indigestion.’ The
Jumpers Plate ’ was seen by the Tennysons with
preoccupied eyes straying to the Daffodil Parade,
where their Exes, were next to do a ‘ turn.’ The
competitors for the Plate acted just like humans.
Old Domino, the grey winner, behaved like a
brainy ancient from the start. They showed him
all the jfimps beforehand, and the old chap (1)
scanned, (2) smelt, (3) tasted, every fence and wall
to be negotiated. Second prize was won by a
coquettish chestnut mare, Lady Hampden by
name. Just like a woman ; a good second is the
best she can hope for !
A daughter of alleged Senator Reid figured as
the driver of one of the trimmest trotters in the
show ring last week, and won points for whipman-
ship all along the line. All the Reid daughters
are skilful with the reins. One comely member
of the family has even gone in for veterinary
practice, can shoe, plaster and physic her nags,
and is dexterous in all branches of horse business.
“ The peeping Toms of the community are being
catered for by a villainous invention of glass and
steel, so contrived as to fit any door-lock aperture,
and designed to bring to the eye the focus of the
whole room. Place your patent investigator at
the keyhole and you sight all that is going on
within. The mischievous thing was exhibited by
a public enemy at the Show last week, and the
sale for it was enormous—mostly among country
people. In the city you can hang a towel over
the keyhole and end the thing 1 . The country
house has generally large cracks in the wall.

“Nellie Stewart skipped about town a lot in
brief skirts before she left Melbourne. Her pro-
jecting hips gave the skirt too brief a look to be
suitable, but Nellie’s shoes were things of beauty
and her arched instep a joy for ever. Men in the
street forget that the foot displayed in the
Orange Nell rdle didn’t arch at all. The actress is
much more given to cheering the street with her
well-shod feet and her begemmed hands than with
her inscrutable face. Her drooping hat is regarded
nowadays as an artistic speciality. •
“ Towards the close of the Villon drama Cuy-
ler Hastings was playing with a tired air. His
make-up was careless, so that, under the illumina-
tion of the limes, his High Constableship wore a
haggard aspect. Miss Lane also died differently
after the first night or two. She took a garden
seat for the uttering of her swan notes. But the
flunkeys still dragged her corpse out with its heels
rasping along the boards.
“ The grey-black groveller in the Royal drama
had a habit of making the sign of the cross with
the left hand—a bit of bad business in the eyes of
the R.C. Even most ‘outsiders’ knew enough to
see that this was wrong. King Atholwood never fell
into this error, and his unostentatious holy water
sprinklings were wonderfully natural. The male
R.C. always dips his paw into the font in a quiet,
sheepish fashion that splashes no neighbor.
“Julius Knight will not be too much troubled
by the adulation and overtures of romantic young
things as long as he keeps gravitating on this side
of the footlights. It is a Julius of greater years,
with greying tendencies, of coarser features, with
tell-tale traces of hard work in his strong eyes and
the etchings about his lips. And always he acts.
Maud Jeffries is, on the other hand, lovelier, if
anything; calmer, splendid in her dress, and
quite the grand duchess in her manner.
“News has arrived of the engagement of Mr.
Nick Fitzgerald’s warrior son to Lady Constance
McKenzie—a lady of high degree, with Countesses
galore on the family roll-call. The big brewer’s
family has a talent for marrying blue blood. The
eldest son, a graduate of Dublin University,
is mated to Lord North’s daughter and moves In
a creamy London circle where the R.C.’s largely
predominate. The McKenzie family is also R.C.
The third son, Ned, is Melbourne’s leading
bachelor and holds his head high. More, he is
considered the ‘ killer ’ of the family, so a duchess
may be expected to fall to his matrimonial lot —if
the Fates continue smiling. And his father still
keeps the brewery.
“At the Huuane Society’s evening, Lady
Tennyson, handing over the certificates and
pinning-on the medals, did her part with smileless
stiffness. Now, the Countess of Hopetoun, social
ice-cream though she was, was wont to shine as
a hero-worshipper, and when presenting the
‘Humane awards’ greeted each blushing brave
with quiet earnestness and an appreciative flash
of her usually inscrutable eyes. J. Elder, of Port
Melbourne, having twice saved life since last
distribution, drifted forward for his awards
with the loose-jointed gait of one far
gone in abject nervousness, deprecatingly
crooking his left knee by way of salute
to Lady Tennyson. One of his achievements, the
rescue of a drunken sailor from the waters of the
Bay on a dark and dirty night, involved this man
of mettle in the double peril of drowning and being
drawn under by the steamer’s propeller. A small
boy, who came for his certificate in clothes several
sizes too large for him, had been fishing from the
pier for bits of floating wood for the family fire
when a human derelict swept past with other
flotsam and jetsam. The kid became a fisher of
men, and saved his life. Only two of the prize-
takers accepted their honors with outward
calmness. One of these, an elderly police-
sergeant, walked the platform and stood to
attention like grave discipline incarnate. On
the day he won his medal, however, he had hurled
himself down a steep bank and taken to the water,
as if he were youth and impetuosity personified,
at the cry of beauty in distress. The other com-
posed member of the ‘ valiant crew 7 ’ was a
charming heroine of 23, Miss Queenie Wood, of
Hawthorn (Melb.), who had saved a woman and
two girls from drowning. Rather cruel, by the
way, the society’s habit of publishing everyone’s
age ! Perhaps the rescuers, haloed with glory,
can laugh at old Time, but what about the rescued?
Bad enough to be half-drow 7 ned or burnt brown,
without sending a bellman round, so to speak, to
show one up as 39, say.
“On the Humane platform, the toilettes and
attitudes of the Dowager Clarke, Mrs. John For-
rest, and Bishopess Clarke, respectively, sug-
gested the Big Bear, the Middling-sized Bear,
and the Little Bear. ‘Big,’ nowadays, means
costly a,nd important, like the Cliveden widow’s
impressive black robe, coiled cunningly round
owner’s feet as she sat. Dame Forrest’s black
skirt was more utilitarian, but a glance at the
spot where the Dowager’s trilbies should have
been taught the Westralienne to veil her shoe-
leather with less or more success. Pleasant-faced
little Mrs. Clarke, in grey frock and cream lace,
showed her boot-soles to the audience as confid-
ingly as if all the world were one big, uncritical
Bishopscourt.
Yours affectionately,

A Grand Medicine.
“What a horrible thing St. Vitus’ Dance is ! ” exclaimed Miss Viola Dawson, 4 Willoughby-street, Milson’s Point, N. Sydney.
“Four years ago the weak state of my nerves kept me from going to school. I would tremble and shake all over, and at last St.
Vitus’ Dance came on. Wherever I went I thought I could see horrible black faces looking at me. The least sound made me jump,
and I couldn’t keep my hands or face still for a minute. I had to force myself to eat and fell away to a shadow. I was almost too
weak to walk lam sure I had not a pint of good, pure, warm blood in my body. I couldn’t take part in any play like the
rrirls because my nerves were always working and twitching. I tried almost everything you could think of to stop the horrible
disease but I didn’t get a bit better. A lady hearing how ill I was told me that she read how good Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills were in
all nervous troubles, and she brought me a clipping of a case in which a young girl had suffered like I was suffering, and these pills
had cured her. I tried them and the very first box made my nerves steadier. Soon I ate heartily, and a splendid healthy color came
in my cheeks. Three boxes took away all the jumping and twitching. Now my health is capital and I never get frightened or
nervous. There’s no doubt about it, but Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are really a
grand medicine.”
By this simple and direct action on the blood, and by bracing the nerves, Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills cure Anssmia, Headaches, Neuralgia, Sciatica, Lumbago, Back-
ache, Kidney and Liver Troubles and skin diseases like Pimples and Eczema.
2s. 9d. a box; six boxes, 15s. 3d.

George Turner that he has a big reputation to
lose, and that the reckless lending of his name to

Charles Ridd, dead at Molesworth (Vic.),
a colonist for 52 years, claimed to have been a
descendant of John Ridd, of Blackinore’s “ Lorna
Doone.” He was a man of great size and immense
strength, like his alleged progenitor.
G. R. Dibbs is struggling in his old age
with the manufacture of another walking-stick.
He has already presented walking-sticks of his
own making to King Ned, John See and Governor
Rawson. Speculation gives the present one to
Chamberlain.
Macdonald Paterson, M.H.R., who does
not wear pockets in his pants, returns from his
foreign trip with a cry for more imported money.
“What we want is to advertise ourselves,” he
says. A solicitor without pockets in his pants is
material for a very good advertisement.
Dowie of the Purple Wings has a father
living on the extreme edge of his halo in America.
Recently the widow of the late Colonel North
married the ancient, but hastily divorced him,
finding him merely a very dull oldest inhabitant
with neither money nor prophetic instincts.
A fraudulent Westralian postmaster lately
came to grief in a curious fashion. He threw the
incriminating documents into the sea, and the
sea washed them up on the beach, and the wrong
person found them, and then things happened.
And a match, properly applied, would have saved
all the trouble.

The Senate has wisely changed the name
of a Tasmanian electoral division from Northcote
to Bass. The Commonwealth should treasure
every atom of its old heroic tradition, and there’s
no man more worthy of being commemorated than
explorer Bass.
A Sydney rag and bottle collector carries
a sign on the front of his hand-cart which states
that a certain senator is his cousin, and other
signs on the sides of the cart give the names of
other well-known people to whom Bottle-oh says
he is closely related.
W. H. J. Slee, retired at the end of
August from the chief-inspectorship of N.S.W.
mines, had held office under 24 Ministers. Of
that 24 there were about five who knew a winze
from a haystack. The other 19 were mostly shoe-
makers, or bakers, or city attorneys.
R. E. O’Connor has been 27 years at the
Bar. Sixteen years ago (1887) Parkes appointed
him to the N.S.W. Upper House, and he stayed
there 11 years —till 1898. His only experience as
a representative of the people has been in the
Senate, and he leaves that this session.
H. C. E. Muecke, who will most likely get
into the late J. L. Parsons’ South Australia
Legislative Council seat, is German Consul in
Adelaide ; the late member was Japanese Consul.
Like most S. Australian Germans, Muecke is a
reliable Torv; also he is an Adelaide merchant,
and a director of the Broken Hill Prop. (Opposi-
tion-leader John Darling is the other Adelaide
director).
Boyd, of the Oufctrim Coal Co., is one of
the most persistent advocates of blackleg labor in
Victoria. When Boyd brought his first consign-
ment of “ free laborers ” to Gippsland they were
armed with cheap but dangerous revolvers. Boyd
played a big game of bluff in Outtrim in the early
days of the strike, but certain things happened,
and now he mostly howls and lashes his tail in
Melbourne, 70 miles away.
Seems definitely settled that Darley, C.J.,
is coming out to take up his official duties again.
Rumor alleges that this is mainly in response to
strong pressure from the local Bar, whose reason
is a desire of keeping the C.J.-ship from that
beastly democrat, Attorney-General Wise. No
man seems threatened with so many billets as
B. R. Wise, and he is now credited with aspiring
to the Chief Justice’s seat.
“ J.A.C.” on billiardists :
Mr. “Bill’’Lyons, winner of Tattersall’s billiard tour-
nament, is a left-handed player. I first knew him 25
years ago in Auckland (M.L.), and he could handle the
cue with the best of them then. He had at that time, in
company with a partner, “ Bob ” Blaikie, a boot and shoe
business. They, seemingly, found bookmaking a more
lucrative enterprise than boots, and soon devoted their
whole attention to it. Amid the bookmakers exodus
which ensued on the introduction of the tote, they botn
shifted to Australia. Blaikie subsequently went back,
hut “ Bill ” remained to gladden the Commonwealth. Re
left-handed billiardists. I knew one, in Glasgow, away
back in the ’6o‘s—a certain James Meany, who claimed to
he champion of Ireland, and who, for a left-hander, was
certainly one of the world’s best players a 200-and-over-
break man. He could not strike a hall with his right
band. I have frequently known him to get a game on
with some “ sawney ” by offerm'' in addition to points,
to “ play ye wid me lift hand !’’

The late Dean O’Hea, of Melbourne,
prided himself considerably on his good old Irish
ancestors and ancestresses, and their historical
capacity for making good old bones. His grand-
father, who died at 85, left a mother to mourn
his loss. She had been a Miss De Lacey,
descendant of the De Laceys, who figure in Irish
history among the heroic defenders of Limerick
against William of the “Glorious Memory,” and
the family pluck kept her going to the age
of 115, it is said. Monsignor the Dean didn’t
get near to his great-grandmother’s record,
but he struggled on to 89J, taking his whisky like
a warrior until he grew infirm. Much nonsense
was written and talked about the genial old
veteran —his successful speculations, his “card-
playing,” and his alleged riotous jollity. As a
matter of fact, he never played cards, and his
reputed wealth was mostly a myth.
An old Sydney press-
mam supplies this story.

ably good lie :
Probably one of the most
awful pieces of profanity that
ever sullied a N.S.W. temple
Of justice occurred in a Sydney
court, and before Justice Win-
deyer, too. Two men were
charged with robbery from a
store, and one of the articles
taken, a violin, was produced
in court. Counsel for the de-
fence endeavored to show that
the article was a paltry affair
not worth £2. The owner
swore it was worth £2O. De-
fendant’s counsel picked up
the violin and coolly said : “ I
am a violinist myself, and I’ll
soon convince the court by the
poor tone of this instrument
that it is not worth a tenth of
that sum.” And before any-
one knew what would happen
next, he tucked the thing
under his chin and played a
few bars of “Home, Sweet
Home,” or what he intended
for that ancient air. Everyone
stood aghast, waiting to hear
the awful lion on the bench
fall upon that barrister and
rend him asunder, but even
the dread Judge seemed para-
lysed. The lawyer cheerfully
observed, “That was an an-
dante movement, your Honor.
Now I’ll show how ill-suited
this instrument is for an allegro ”- ' Put
that wretched thing down!” thundered Windeyer.
“ Put it down at once, or I’ll prevent you from ever
appearing in this court again! ” And no one was
astonished when the sentence was made seven years.
Recently retired Victorian Under-Secre-
tary for Lands, T. F. Morkham, was a beautiful
instance of the Chinese system of managing the
Victorian public service. After holding for 14
years a leading position in the Public Works
Department, where he thoroughly understood the
duties, he was transferred by seniority to the
Lands, to deal with matters he knew nothing
about. And Victoria would have sent him just
as cheerfully to look after engineering matters,
geology, statistics or lighthouses if the seniority
fetish had so required,
A. N. Barnett, appointed Royal Com-
missioner to inquire into the allegations of brutal
treatment in certain N.S.W. lunatic asylums and
old-peoples’ “homes,” is a good sample of the
pushing civil servant. First in the Lands Dept.,
then in the Mines as warden, then in the Justice,
he battled all the time in the baekblocks, and
while still on the good side of 40 was sent to
Albury to replace “ Rolf Boldrewood ” when that
pleasant old yarn-spinner gave over fining the
local resident ss. for letting his cow wander.
From there Barnett got down to the city police-
court bench within the last year or two.
SOCIETY AT THE SHOW.
MR. AND MRS. TOORAK (who are looking at the
pigs): “Disgustingly fat, aren't they?"
It looks as though Sydney Town Clerk
Nesbitt would be harried into resigning. Alder-
manic nature, in spite of recent “ reform,” is still
mostly hog, and ability generally has the sort of
nerves that are not fitted to deal with hog. Also,
an enthusiast who wants to do good work wearies of
being blocked at every turn by the necessity of
employing aldermen’s incompetent friends at twice
what they are worth. For years all manner of
fools urged as the remedy for everything : “Get
a good man from England.” What is wanted is a
complete new municipal law.
Governor Le Hunte brought off a good
double in Adelaide on Sept. Ist, attending the
dedication of a church in the morning, and hurry-
ing off to witness the Derby and decorate the
winner in the afternoon. His other published
engagements for the week included Executive
Council and meeting of a Girls’ Club, a hunt
meeting, a football match, a private view of some
very moderate pictures, a lecture, an organ
recital, more racing, and other things—l 3 in all.
He is quite the most accessible Governor S. A. Ims
ever had, and now an ungrateful public is be-
ginning to suggest that he is too ready to go to
anything for which an invitation is sent.

the present time he is trying to get

“Doxo”: South Australia’s Judge
Boucaut the Grim is an omnivorous
reader, and when not on the Bench is
poring over a book—any book, from a
law treatise to a Deadwood Dick shocker.
Saw one of his lists for a month’s reading
—an order on a bookseller. No branch
of literature was forgotten ; there were
histories, biographies, novels poor, bad
and indifferent, law-books, penny-dread-
fuls, and works of science. Boucaut is
practically a reclamation job, and the
amount of mental filling in he requires
before he is fully reclaimed is enormous.
“ Melb ” : In Bourke-street, only
the other day, I was introduced to
Cabbage-Tree Ned, as it were the Last
of the Mohicans, from Cobb’s yore-time coach
drivers. Not a Bohemian in any way, but a per-
fectly quiet citizen. The romance has yet to be
written of Cobb’s coaches. Two good racehorses,
one of which was Inkerman, came out of Cobb’s
traces. For a contrast, I have seen a whilom
smart hurdle-racer in the shafts of a Melbourne
cab.
The “father” of the N.S.W. Legislative
Council is James Norton, solicitor, born in
December, 1824, in Sydney, on the spot where
Temple Court now stands, and in the house
known at one time as the Three Tuns Tavern,
and kept by Richard Driver, senr. The house
had been the private residence of Mr. Norton’s
father in the twenties. Mr. Norton was appointed
to the L.C. in October, 1879. His runner up is
Philip Gidley King, born at Parramatta in 1817,
and appointed to the Council December, 1880.

Concerning one of Phil May’s old models :
Brophy was his name, an ex-Anglican minister. Exiled
from the ministry and dreadfully down on his luck,
Brophy to the last managed to rig himself out in “ third
editions” of old-fashioned clerical clothes—a sort of
ecclesiastical scarecrow in bad circumstances. Brophy
applied to May for charity, and the artist, much to the
old man’s delight, gave him a shilling and a “retaining
fee ”of half-a-crown a week to sit as a model. This really
meant that for nearly three years the old broken-down
parson, who had something of the look and stoop of John
Henry Newman, was one of Phil May’s pensioners. When-
ever he “sat” Brophy, in addition to the “half-crown
regular,” got his dinner and tea at May’s. One day the
80-years-old model asked May to give him some lessons in
drawing. The joke-loving artist consented, on the
understanding that Brophy was to leave May his skeleton
when he died. A contract to this effect was written by
May and solemnly signed by Brophy. Brophy, who could
not be induced to draw anything save girls’ faces and
ladies of the ballet, continued to receive his lessons and
his half-crown, up to the week of May’s departure from
Sydney. The artist parted with his venerable model in
the most comical manner. “You’ve played me a dirty
trick,” said May, “ by swindling me out of that skeleton.
I could have bought one, in sound order and condition,
for half the money you’ve cost me.” The old fellow, con-
scious of his base ingratitude to his best and most patient
friend, answered, “ Don’t be angry with me, Mr. May.
It’s not my fault. I meant to keep my word. Stay in
Sydney a few months longer, and give me another chance
to show you that I am a man of honor."

admitted as solicitors in N.S.W. ; and three-
quarters of those already on the rolls are not able
to make a decent and honorable living. Many a
solicitor in N.S.W., nowadays, reckons his fortune
as made if he is given £lOO to “invest ” by some
money-lending client, and the usurers find in the
unemployed solicitor their most unscru] ulous tool.

Queensland:
Re your advice about “ staying and helping to fight for
a progressive land-tax ” (Bulletin, 30/7/’O3). What is the
worker to do in the meantime? Self and mate have
tried around most ports and many inland townships
from Cooktown to Fremantle during the past three
years. As we, who are practically teetotal, rather above
the average in bush experience and with no grey hairs to
handicap us, have not averaged 10s. a week and tucker
for the whole period, what about the others ? Fencing,
cane-cutting, driving a team—we can tackle them all
Is there any place in the world where non-boozing, com-
petent men can be reasonably sure of JBI a week and
tucker ? When we know, self and mate are off there—
and we are two out of many

Some Papuan notes :
In Papua even the natives do not escape malarial fever.
As a cure they lie on the ground exposed to the direct
rays of a fierce sun for a few hours. Coastal natives
build a small fire on the beach, and, scraping the embers
aside, curl themselves up on the hot sand. In both cases
a profuse perspiration soon results, after which con-
valescence is rapid. The forehead is gashed with a shell
in order to relieve the headache which accompanies fever.
Civilised natives generally use quinine.
To catch a python alive. The chance is seized when
the snake is suspended by only a few coils from the limb
Of a tree. Advancing cautiously with a forked stick, one
of the hunters pins the neck of the reptile to the tree-
trunk. The suddenness of the attack causes the snake
to fall writhing on the ground, where its body isinstantly
pinned down with other forked sticks by the other
members of the party. The python is then with difficulty
straightened, a long stout pole is pushed into the space
between the sticks and the captive, the whole length of
which is securely lashed to the pole with strong vines.
Then the forks are lifted, and, amidst great rejoicing, the
python, unable to move a muscle, is carried shoulder-
high to the village and slain When cooked, the white
flesh is considered a delicacy.
Amongst the Papuans, if water dashed in a swooning
person’s face has no effect, a lighted fire-stick is applied
to the big toe of the left foot. In a few seconds the
patient’s leg-muscle begins to twitch, and consciousness
quickly follows.
Matches generally accompany the presence of white
men, and the civilized Papuan seldom troubles about
making a fire in his former barbaric manner. Upon
necessity, however, a dry piece of bamboo is split in two,
and, with the aid of some creeper, one portion is formed
into a bow, while the other is placed beneath it in the
position of an arrow ready for flight. Friction is then
obtained by rubbing the bow against the “arrow,” and
sparks soon result. Another method is to place on the
ground a dry branch of the madavi-tree, and holding in
the hand, as one would a pencil, a smaller and roughly-
pointed piece of the same wood, to move it rapidly to and
fro on the surface of the stationary limb. To obtain
greater pressure one hand is pressed on top of the other,
and very soon fine charcoal appears at the end of a
narrow indentation in the wood, which gradually smokes,
and finally glows, when blown.
One Papuan method of catching fish is to spread a
small net over a mushroom-shaped growth of coral in
shallow water. A prepared root of poisonous nature,
attached to the end of a stick, is then thrust under the
shelving roof and vigorously stirred about, which causes
the water to become milky white. Fish lurking beneath
the coral, being unable to escape from the poisoned
water, succumb and float to the surface when the net is
lifted. In districts where there’s no coral, large trian-
gular nets with wooden arms and a cross piece are utilised.
Each native has one, and when a shoal of fish is sighted
from the beach a crowd of natives divides into two
parties, which, cautiously wading in neck-deep, gradually
join forces, and forming into a semi-circle, drive the prey
shoreward, where at a favorable opportunity the spoil is
excitedly scooped up. Nets of enormous size, on the float
and sinker principle, are also used, and circular cane-
traps, tapering to a point, are found very effective.
When a Papuan fop is going to visit the lady of his
choice his toilet preparations are more elaborate than
those of an up-to-date Johnnie. First he gets a friend
to shave his face with a piece of obsidian (volcanic glass);
after which, his enormous mop of lime-bleached frizzy
hair, previously deluged in cocoanut-oil, is carefully
combed and trimmed. If available, a mirror is then re-
quisitioned, and he observes with gratification that not a
single hair can be perceived on the reflected face. Even
the eyebrows have been shorn. Giving a grin, he ex-
poses to view a regular set of brown-stained teeth ; but,
with a sigh, he notes that a deal more betel-nut chewing
will be necessary before the once white ivories assume
the jet-black color of manhood. A few curves and off-
sets are designed on his face, as a representation of the
moon. Next his whole body is coated with cocoanut-oi'
untd ** ghstens. Finally, he takes unto himself a newly-
kabatn (loin-covering)—and his toilet is corn-

HE DIDN’T WANT TEACHING.
EXPERT (about to graft an orange tree): “ Look
carefully, my man, and I'll show you how to
graft-."
MY MAN: " You’ll show me how to graft, you
bit of a scallywag; do I look as if I didn’t
know how to graft?”
“ Chass ” ; Once saw two large eagles
trying to capture a hare on the open plains, and
the struggle was an interesting display of intelli-
gence. The eagles at first started to swoop down
on the running hare simultaneously, but when the
birds were within about a yard of him, the hare
would stop suddenly and dart away at right
angles, and the eagles, unable to stop, would
swoop yards ahead. Meanwhile the hare made
good use of the time by keeping on full steam for
a distant hedge. As soon as the eagles got near
enough for a second swoop the hare slackened
speed and evaded them as before. Then the
eagles divided their attentions and darted at him
separately, but the hare timed them splendidly,
and in the end he got under cover, leaving two
empty and disgusted birds speaking evil to each
other out on the plain.
* * * *
In the early days of missionaries in
Maoriland, one of the first of the chiefs
to become converted was old Te Roti. Now Te
Roti, in addition to his proper wife, had a slave-
wife that he had captured from some other tribe.
The missionary told the chief that he would have
to put away the latter, as he could not be a true
Christian and keep two. The missionary was
away at some other station for a few weeks, and
when he returned Te Roti met him, and smilingly
told him that the little difficulty about the two
wives had been fixed up. “ What have you done
with Rata ? ” said the missionary, referring to the
slave-wife. “ Oh,” said the chief, “leather last
week! ”
“Cecil P.” : Be tree-limbs growing
together (8., 20/6/’O3). There was, and probably
still is, a remarkable in-
stance of arboreal Siamese
twins to be seen in the
garden of a very old river-
side inn at Caerleon, above
Monmouth(England). Two
trqps are connected by a
bar-like bough. Impos-
sible to tell from which
tree growth began; it
grows in the usual manner
from each, and is destitute
of twigs or leaves. Forget
what trees they were.
* *
A great deal of foolish
row is made by N.S.W.
country residents in their
demand for free river fer-
ries. A ferry, they say,
is only a floating bridge,
and when the lucky dis-
trict which gets a bridge
pays nothing, the unlucky
one which has to put up
with a beastly punt should
also pay nothing—or even
less. Which is putting|the
cart of theory before the
horse of common sense.
The people who use the
bridge ought to be charged
a toll just as much as the
people who use the punt,
but it wouldn’t pay to keep
a man on the bridge solely
to collect the occasional
copper, while the man has
to be at the punt in any
case. If the bridge is a bad
debt that is no reason why the punt should be
made one also.

“ Bobrid ” : Artifical feeding lias made
sheep so cunning that they will follow the track
of a pollard-distributor and pick up the poisoned
baits. Consequently, these clever j.urnbucks have
to be removed when pollard is laid, and before
they are brought back to the paddock the uneaten
baits must be picked up—a “pet” of a job!
Fancy tramping miles of twisting trails with your
eyes glued on the ground in search of a small
pellet of phosphorised pollard ! As this duty
generally falls to the driver of the distributor, in
his “spare time,” he has sense enough to “cut
off ” the pollard supply soon after starting. Then
the picking-up job is all right, provided there’s
a shady kurrajong tree in the paddock and the
ants are not too lively. The boss sees the tracks
of the machine, so he’s satisfied.

“ Dross ” : Camped once in Thornton
(Yic.) with an old resident, and noticed a lot of
sticks sto Bft long lying around the hut. Asked
my host if he was drying them for whip-handles,
and he told me he had killed five snakes in last
two months, and so had laid a few sticks about to
be handy when his “snake-alarm-clock” called
him. Next morning early I heard a hen making
a terrific noise, and on rushing out found her
standing in middle of road with wings spread,
about five yards from big brown sna re. My host
told me she never failed to let him know if a
snake was about.
PERCY GRAINGER.
Photo, by liana, London.
“Stan. P.” : Generally understood that if
a horse breaks a limb it is a “gone coon”; but I
have found that if the break is in a place that will
permit of splints being effectively placed, the leg
will knit much quicker than a human being’s.
Had a mare that fractured a pastern, and I did
not even treat it. The beast was turned into a
grass paddock, and after a couple of months was
brought in, blistered with ointment to reduce the
swelling caused by the knitting, and now shows
no sign of the accident. Many horses are, through
ignorance, hurried into an unnecessary grave.
ON THE HILLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With tightened chains
And hot heads snatching
At slackened reins;
With quick breath catching,
The good teams climb the stubborn hills,
The grim, sardonic, scornful hills.
Ah, God ! their hearts break on the hills,
And yet my blood leaps, watching.
The strong hoofs slip
On mud and boulder,
The muscles grip
On flank and shoulder,
And proudly do they rush the hills—
The everlasting, sullen hills.
But every time they top the hills
Their hot, young blood beats colder.
The whips fall light
On sweat-stained quarter.
They wrench and fight
With breath heaved shorter.
They are the conquerors of the hills—
The finest horseflesh on the hills.
Ah ! they will die here on the hills ;
Their veins run blood, not water.
Why must they strain
With dead loads dragging,
With irksome rein
And stout hearts flagging ?
Look, for your answer, to the hills -
The cruel hills, the heavy hills.
Ah ! every time they climb the hills
I see an old horse lagging.
We shout their names ;
They toss the snaffle
And strain the hames,
And spurn the gravel.
They are the conquerors of the hills—
The finest blood on all the hills—
And they will die here on the hills,
When they’re too worn to travel.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
AT SUNDOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bush is leaning like a tired child
Her dear, dark head upon the breast of night;
One star is lone and cold above the height;
The call of cities turbulent and wild
Is with me like the sound of far-off fight.
The hot sky flames above the bush-line dim ;
Beyond the ratas where the red sun dips
Are gleaming bays and galaxies of ships.
My life is bounded by the forest rim—
The cities call me with a million lips !
The bush leans o’er me, and her tresses, long,
Sweet-scented, dark, are trailing on my breast
That craves the strife, the trial and the test,
The burning impulse and endeavor strong—
To fail or win—to stand among the rest!
The night is coming, and the shadows trail
Like wasted lives across the forest belt;
Above the sky-line is a crimson veldt
With dim, heroic forms that strive and fail,
And other faces rise and fade and melt,
That sank to greatness for a fallen name.
I seem to hear the tumult and the din
Of that wide vortex that draws all things in
About its circle towards the crest of fame ;
Some rise to failure, and some stoop to win.
The upward shadow of the broad earth meets
The sky-line, gleaming like life’s golden rim—
All dark beneath but bubbles at the brim.
My heart is throbbing for the roar of streets !
The cloister of the bush is screened and dim.
M.L.

Red Light—Danger ! We’d better look out
When we see such an ominous sign :
That peril’s ahead, there isn’t much doubt,
Perhaps it’s a slip on the line.
We feel just the same when we get a bad cough—
Our safety we haste to secure,
By buying the stuff that will soon shake it off,

“ Thaker ” :
No doubt the
cow, frequently
mentioned in
The Bulletin as
a thankless ob-
ject, does her
level best in the
way of ingrati-
tude and general
cussedness, but
nature has im-
posed limitations
which prevent
her from getting
into the same
class with the
eternally ac-
cursed ant. The
cow, for instance,
can’t crawl up
inside the leg of
your pants un-
noticed, and then
bite you sud-
denly with red-
hot jaws ; neither
can she get upon
to the bough-
shed when you
are having din-
ner, and fall—
again unnoticed
—into your pan-
nikin of tea, giv-
ing it what is
probably the
most horrible
flavor on earth.
The ant can do
both those things
and many more.
If, in a moment
of exasperat i on,
you throw a cow
down a 30ft.
shaft, she will
probably remain
there, unless a
party of fools
comes along and
yanks her out,
in which case
her ingratitude
will be their
affair. An ant
will climb cheer-
fully out of that , -
shaft and renew the attack just when you
have forgotten him. Moreover, the cow lies
under a shady tree and ruminates most of the
day but the an is a perfect dynamo of
diabolical energy all the time. Rescue a drowning
ant and he will bite your finger without waiting
to shake the water out of his hair ; feed a hungry
one, and he, with about 60 millions of his rela-
tions, will come and camp close to your tent, steal
everything m the way of tucker which they can
carry away, and die all over, and spoil anything
which they can’t steal. Then look at the rela-
five chances for retaliation. About the worst you
can do to an ant is to burn him alive, and he
dpesnt seem to mind that much ; hut sool Pin-
cher and Nettle on to that cow and see how
they will make her buck and bellow all round the
horizon—and over it. If your cow is a bullock or
a horse, you can yoke her up, and, besides the
knowledge that you are condemning her to the
worst kind of penal servitude for life, can have
the immediate satisfaction of belting the soul-case
off her. And, after all, nothing soothes a man’s
feelings like being able to belt something till he is
tired.

Trees that have died of drought in JNew
England (N.S.W.) : Iron-barks, stringy-barks,
apple trees, white box, yellow box, gums of all
sorts, wattles of all sorts, dogwood, dead-finish,
forest-oaks, and she-oaks. Animals ditto : scrub-
wallabies, rock-wallabies and kangaroo-rats. And
the kookaburra also has perished in thousands.
A Maoriland story of endurance :
A rabbiter named Clark on Mount Pisa station slipped
on ice and fell down a steep face, fracturing his hip. In
his fall a sharp rock made a gash four inches in length in
the abdomen, exposing the bowels. Clark, not having a
penknife, made holes in his flesh with the tongue of his
belt-buckle and stitched up the wound with a bootlace.
Later on a younger brother and sister found him and
brought blankets to the place, under which he had to
rest till daylight, as it was impossible to remove him in
the dark.

Exhibition of Phil May’s Drawings.
The Bulletin’s collection of Phil May’s draw-
ings, to be sold by Walter Bradley and Co., at
324 George-street, Sydney, on Friday, 18th
September, at 11 o’clock, contains upwards of 100
political cartoons, upwards of 100 portraits and
caricatures (including the famous series of
“Things We See When We Haven’t Got Our
Gun ”), and upwards of 100 humorous drawings.
The cartoons deal chiefly with N.S.W. poli-
ticians of 1880 to 1890. The Robertson Ministry
and its members—“ Jack ” Robertson, ‘ ‘ Cocky ’
Burns, and the rest ; the Jennings Ministry
Pat Jennings, Dibbs, l)r. Renwick, Jack Want,
Copeland, and others ; with the leading Opposi-
tionists —Parkes, Wise, Garrett, Neild, and others
—are shown frequently.
The portraits include leading judges and clerics,
actors and musicians, and a host of other
characters in Melbourne and Sydney. Judge Win-
deyer, Cardinal Moran, Salomons, F. W. Ward,
David Syme, “Daddy” Hallewell and George
Goodman, Lord Loch and Lord Carrington,
Purves and L. L. Smith—these and scores of
others find themselves presented by the most
brilliant portrait-draughtsman of his generation.
To many of the public the humorous drawings
will appeal. There are examples that show
May in some respects at his very best. Jhe
series will be on exhibition next week, and those
who can visit it should not miss the opportunity,
whether purchasers or not. “ There was only one
Phil May ’’—and, once dispersed, such a collection
will never again be brought together.

POLITICAL
POINTS
G. H. Reid and his two opponents did
not, among them, poll at the recent bye-election
as many votes as Harry For an did for the East
Sydney seat at the general election.
Notwithstanding Kingston’s firm adminis-
tration our Customs officials are not in the same
street with those of Freetrade Britain. The other
day a Greenock pig-breeder bought half a ton of
sweepings out of the hold of a sugar-ship, and was
heavily fined for not paying duty on the sugar in
the said sweepings. C~
A couple of months ago a produce broker’s
company in London paid the first-class passage of
one William F. Vizer to New York for the pur-
pose of opening a branch of its business. The
U.S. authorities, under the contract labor law
affecting aliens, promptly shipped William F.
back to England. Down with Barton, of course !
It is commonly reported that Brisbane
Tramway manager, Badger, goes to England to
see if he can get capital to buy up the Brisbane
suburban railways. Minister
Leahy is strongly in favor of
selling the railways, as
neither he nor anyone
else can m ake t h e m pay.
‘ ‘ Making railways
pay” in Australia
means making
them cover all
expenses and
interest on
money bor-
rowed a t
somet h i n g
like 4 per
cent., and
this with-
out the aid
of a land-
grant. No
other thin-
ly - peopled
country in
the world
asks such
wildimpos-
sibili ti es
from its lines
as Australia does.
In Canada, with all
its boom, the privately-
managed railways only pay
2 per cent, on their actual cost,
which is a lot worse than Queensland’s average,
and private management shows up no better in
most other countries.
A Workmen’s Compensation Bill is just
now occupying the Q. Assembly. M.L.A.
Macartney, of Toowong, moved that a mother-in-
law be included in the list of relative-dependants
to be benefited by the Bill. And the Q. Assembly
rose like one man and hissed derision at Macart-
ney, and cast him and his mother-in-law out into
the stilly night.
George Reid’s assurance that at next
Federal election he will fight Premier Barton in
his own constituency evidently means that Reid
confidently expects Barton to take the Chief Jus-
ticeship. . If Barton does go to the poll at next
election, it is about as certain as anything can be
that Reid won’t risk his seat by opposing him.
But, being confident that there won’t be any
Barton on deck next December, it is worth while
for a person like Reid to take up a cheap attitude
which will enable him to pose as the man whose
threatened opposition frightened Barton out of
politics.
ANOTHER FAKIR.
GEORGE REID (who has just fired his Stiger
Vortex): “Gosh! I made sure that would
have brought him down.”
The unprecedentedly small poll at the
East Sydney election fell like a wet camel on the
story about a great uprising of the people to
protest against the conduct of the Barton Govern-
ment. Reid got back, but a mere handful of
people voted, and public' interest was as flat as
the Old Man Plain. No alleged uprising of the
nation ever sat down more unanimously.
Senator Dawson has raised the question
whether the Commonwealth should not establish
a system of wireless telegraphy between Tasmania
and Victoria. The agreement with the Eastern
Extension Cable Co. would not be infringed by
an oversea scheme of telegraphic communication;
“Submarine” is the contract word in the cor-
morant company’s document.
At the recent meeting of Vic. Employers’
Federation, alleged Senator Robert Reid con-
demned the existence of “ paid agitators ” in the
country. Let’s see : What’s the name of the
Employers’ “paid agitator”? And what’s the
name of the little Freetrade paper which is main-
tained in Melbourne by subsidies from the
importers—the Robert Reid crowd—to agitate in
their interests ?
N.S.W. Government is anxious to shirk
a conflict with the Upper House. That House
knows it. The contemptuous rejection of the
Amending Arbitration Bill is to be followed by
the kicking out of the Income Tax Amendment
Bill. It’s a long time since the fossils had a
Premier whom they could kick with impunity,
and they seem bent on getting a good amount of
Rugby out of John See.
Ex-King Mahuta, the M.L. Government’s
new front-parlor boarder, is treating Seddon and
co. very cavalierly indeed. He put in an appear-
ance at the seat of Government after Parliament
opened, and then cleared back North again with
his retinue, and is at present skulking there in a
blanket and top hat. Meanwhile some very
impertinent observations are being made in both
Houses about the wild-pig potentate, and it is
very evident that if Seddon wants to secure the
regular attendance of his dusky nibs at Welling-
ton he will have to pursue measures involving loss
of dignity.
THE BOSS OF THE BULL-COHEN BREED.
Mr. Copeland to Sydney interviewer: “ Our creditors
in England would not hear of the transference of the
State debts unless the principal assets, the railways,
were taken over by the Commonwealth likewise.”
“Mr. Copeland, as you are going to Australia I
have a few instructions in matters of politics
for my mortgagor."
The Senate has agreed to a motion by
Jawbone Neild requesting the Federal Govern-
ment to negotiate with the State Governments
with a view to inaugurating a system of Federal
old age pensions. Neild carefully refrained from
suggesting a method of raising the necessary
money, and, as he strongly advocates cutting
down the revenue by the abolition of duties, it is
difficult to see where he comes in.
A Supreme Court J udge is usually choked
with indignation when a private individual
addresses him by letter concerning a case to be
decided. But, the other day, when Murray Smith
wrote to the Vic. Chief Justice on behalf of a
mercantile firm, whose clerk had embezzled, on a
salary of £2 10s. a week, while collecting thousands
per annum, that the pay was adequate, no com-
plaint of impropriety was made against the
writer.
G. H. Reid, at his fool-election meetings,
called for cheers for C. C. Kingston, because the
latter voted against Bill Lyne’s electoral boun-
daries proposals. But what did Kingston do ?
Merely voted against something which G. H.
Reid himself was guilty of in a worse form for
five whole years in N.S.W. If Kingston’s vote
was a serious reflection on Lyne, it was a more
serious reflection on Reid. Perhaps that was why
the crowd cheered so.
The Boy of the Bulldog Breed, as shown
by recent statistics of how British mercantile
Bhips are manned:

The Freetrade bulldog appears to be diminishing
pretty fast at present.
Sydney D.T. of August 31 printed an
article pleading for “private enterprise” in
water conservation:
Eighteen years of trifling and bungling over a problem
of such vast importance amply demonstrates the inca-
pacity of the Government in dealing with it, and the
necessity of invoking the powerful aid of private
enterprise.
The same issue contained nearly two columns
about the Broken Hill water famine—the typical
bit of experience that Australia has had of the
results of private enterprise in water works.
N.S.W. Public Service Board has put its
foot down on a little “perk.” Hitherto when the
estimates of a particular department were before
the House, the Under Secretary advising the
Minister used to be generally advised himself by
one or more responsible officers of the department,
and for the time they spent hanging around ready
to advise bonuses were paid. Now the Board has
decided that hanging round in this matter is not
to be regarded as an extra.
Recently a temperance deputation inter-
viewed Premier Philp to induce him to enforce
the law relating to Sunday closing in Queensland.
Philp promised he would. A week later the
Licensed Victuallers also interviewed him to
secure an amendment of the existing law so as to
allow of opening hotels during certain hours on
Sunday. Philp didn’t say he wouldn’t. Mean-
time, Sunday trading goes on just the same ; no
one is prosecuted ; and everybody gets his Sabbath
shandy.
In the Westralian Assembly, the other
night, Laborite Taylor attacked Minister for
Works Rason for having attended an election
meeting in order to back up a Ministerial candi-
date by making promises of Government expendi-
ture. Rason retorted that it was only kites that
smelt carrion, and Taylor replied, “You should
smell it pretty often, then.” Whereby Taylor
threw away a chance. A smarter man would
simply have gone over and smelt Rason in dead
silence.
Prohibition has become so violent in
Maoriland that a Christchurch wine and spirit
merchant has been fined for advertising in an
Ashburton paper that he could supply whisky by
the demijohn or case, the head and front of his
offending being that Ashburton has voted no-
license, and is supposed to remain dry until it
changes its mind. Of course, a man might
advertise his liquor in an Ashburton paper because
of its wide circulation in drinking districts out-
side, but the law is too excited now to take
account of such things.
Queensland Labor member Lesina often
steps from the bald track of Parliamentary plati-
tudes, and does unexpected things. A week back
he brought into the State Assembly some tins
and pots of alleged preserves to illustrate his text
on jam adulteration, and urged the shuddering
Philp to taste the pumpkin. Philp declined, and
the House adjourned with the dyed carrot staring
the Boodlewraith out of countenance on the table.
But when Labor-leader To.m Price took some big
potatoes into the House in S. Australia lately,
Speaker Coles promptly ruled him out of order.
So far the Gippsland coal companies, run
by men whose denunciations of State interference
with industry are terrible in their violence, have
put up £227,000 of shareholders’ capital and have
cadged ,£104,800 assistance from the State. Their
£17,000 a year aid is, if Irvine keeps his courage
up, to be cut down to £B5OO, but the frantic
demands of these old Tories for the cash benefits
of “ socialistic legislation” are loud and impudent,
and they may get back to the old £17,000. The
Bulletin believes in the socialistic principle of
State aid to local industries, but when a gang of
coarse old hypocrites denounces the principle and
yet demands the assistance, that gang deserves
the contempt of every decent person in the com-
munity.
Postmaster-General Fysh complains that
there’s too much embezzlement altogether in his
department, and that in future the offenders will
not be allowed to avoid punishment by getting
their friends to make up the stolen cash. Austra-
lasian postal departments have always been very
subject to this disease, but it would certainly be
interesting to learn what P.M.-G. Fysh regards
as a reasonable amount of misappropriation— an
amount that isn’t “ too much.” In the bad old
days of Maoriland, gambling schools used to bar
postal officials. If the P.O. gambler won, the
members of the school had to pay out; if he lost,
the school had to pay out just the same—when the
subscription-list came round to make up his deficit
in order to keep him out of gaol.
If the Victorian farmer, who is now plead-
ing that the State shall come and make his roads
for him, had the pluck and
honesty to make land-
holders pay rent for the
closed roads they now use
gratis, his prayerful hands
would look a trifle cleaner.
F’rinstans : Land along-
side Rupert Clarke’s
Green Hill run, pear
Roms e y, is now let to
dairy farmers at 10s. per
acre per annum. In that
run of about 10,000 acres
Clarke has over 100 acres
of closed roads for which
he pays no rent. That
£5O a year rent which he
should pay would go a
long way towards main-
taining the 16 miles of
road around the run.
_ An interesting question was raised by Jus-
tice Boucautin S.A. Supreme Court, whether a law
that may be passed in that (or any other) State
prohibiting the sale of spirits would be invalid
because of inconsistency with the Federal Act
allowing distillation and sale on certain conditions.
The court said candidly that it really couldn't tell,
and that the Federal High Court will have to
settle that and similar problems some day. The
point has been raised in U.S.A., where the State
right of prohibition was upheld.
The suspension of a high official in one of
the departments is the latest perturbation in
N.S.W. public service. This is only one of the
happenings arising from a squabble over a small
account received from a city firm. That small
squabble has spread to the whole question of
supply of stores to the public service, a matter
which it was fondly thought was put on a proper
basis when the Public Service Tender Board was
established some years ago. The row has led to
so many discoveries that no one knows where it
will end.
RE THAT RESIGNATION.
George Reid, the entertainer, with great diffi-
culty manages to keep in front of the footlights.
The above represents him in his latest mode.
Letter from a municipal candidate at.
Ballarat (Vic.): —
To the Editor of the Courier.
Sir,— As statements have been circulated in reference
to my association with the Citizens’ Reform League,
which originated at Kyabram, I wish to distinctly state
that I was at no time associated with same, or ever have
had any desire to become a member of that society.—
Yours, &c., Henry B. George.
Twelve months ago that wasn’t the way to any
kind of office in Victoria.
The Victorian ’Pootra has not even yet
recovered its second wind, and is still wandering
about in a dazed fashion, feeling the lumps raised
on its head by the Irvine Government’s Compul-
sory Land Purchase idea. The ’Pootra feels very
much like the man who placed the torpid serpent
in his bosom, and was rewarded with a bite ; and
what it is going to do under the circumstances
hasn’t yet transpired. Most of the prominent
’Pootras are, like the Scriptural “young ruler,”
men with great possessions, and—also like him—
the preachment of the new apostle Irvine has
made them exceeding sorrowful.
Sydney Telegraph, discussing the possible
Premiers in the event of Barton becoming Chief-
Justice of Australia, actually talked about the
“claims” of poor old Lyne. No doubt the wish
was father to the thought, and the Telegraph
was dreaming on its linotype of a bygone day
when it had a certain Premier of N.S.W. in its
pocket, and chewing over the fact that a Federal
Premier wouldn’t take up any more room in its
pocket than a State one. Kingston’s name was
never even mentioned. The Freetraders, the
sweaters, the customs fakers, and the swindlers
and robbers generally, and their press organs,
can’t bring themselves to mention Kingston’s
name. It makes them shudder.
Hickford, M.L.A., spouting at a “Free-
trade smoke-night” in Melbourne, graciously con-
ceded an admission that the tariff doesn’t need to
be “completely altered.” But it needs a lot of
adapting to the enlightened views of Hickford.
What he complains of is “the almost devilish
ingenuity exercised to make the tariff so com-
plicated and so irritating as almost to prohibit
importation.” Where do such people as Hick-
ford grow, and how is it that the keepers don’t
notice them getting away over the wall ? Austra-
lia, in proportion to its population, imports more
than almost any other country on earth. It im-
ports for its own consumption more per head than
any other country on earth. And Freetraders like
Hickford gabble of the almost entire prohibition
of imports !
A MATTER OR TWO OF BROAD AUSTRALIAN INTEREST.
According to John Douglas there are 19‘
lepers at the Queensland lazarette under his
control. The wife of one of these un-
fortunates, together with her two children,
has been allowed to follow her husband:
to the island and live there. When a re-
monstrance (on behalf of the children) was made-
to Premier Philp the other day, in the Assembly,
that inconsequent person replied that the children,
ran no danger, inasmuch as it had been shown that
leprosy was non-contagious. The logical construc-
tion of a politician who segregates lepers to pro-
tect jthe community from danger of infection, and:
then allows healthy people to live on the lazarette-
island because there is “no danger of infection,”'
must be something truly fearsome and wonderful.

A very quaint Freetrade person named W.
E. Johnson has been writing to S.M. Herald
about Protection. Johnson has four strange delu-
sions : (1) He thinks that the great wall of China
was built to keep out trade instead of barbarians.
(2) He thinks that the wall runs all round China
and talks about it “ enclosing ” the country. (3)
He holds that only countries that are Freetrade or
mainly Freetrade—like Turkey, Scandinavia,
Greece, Mexico and Persia —are prosperous, while
Protectionist lands like the United States,
Canada, Germany and France are obviously
steeped in misery. (4). And he has a wild im-
pression that China, which all its
revenue by direct taxation, laiif|||lp? being by far
the largest item, is a Protectionist country, and
rages accordingly. But W. E. Johnson’s fixed
impression that the Great Wall extends all round
China is a specially noble example of ignorance.

“H.R.H. the Prince of Wales Opening
the First Federal Parliament/'
PAINTING by CHARLES NUTTALL.
SCALE OF CHARGES FOR COPIES: Artist's Proof, £l5 155,; Proof before
Letters, £lO 10s.; Best India Prints, £3 3s.
SOME PRESS EXTRACTS.
Argus : " Mr. Nuttall has been happy with the greater number of the portraits, and with some of them has
succeeded in catching the expressions of his subjects with remarkable fidelity.
Age : “The photogravure reveals a fine and faithful impression of the scene.”
S.M. Herald: “The copy of Mr. Charles Nuttall’s historical painting of the opening of the Commonwealth
by H.R.H. the Prince of Wales was hung in the lobby of the State Parliament House this week,
and has attracted considerable attention both tio .1 members of Parliament and visitors.”
Sydney Daily Telegraph : “Mr. Nuttall’s treatment of this memorable scene, though somewhat con
ventional, is relieved by the general faithfulness of the portraits. The N.S. W. representatives
for the most part are readily recognisable.”
S.A. Register: “ The first Australian historical picture.”
Adelaide Advertiser : “ The Board (of the Art Gallery) decided to secure a photogravure of the painting
of the Opening of the First Federal Parliament, by Mr. Charles Nuttall.”
Brisbane Courier: “ A picture that is likely to attract the notice of all those who take an interest in the
public affairs of the Commonwealth was hung in the State Parliament House by permission of
the President of the Council and the Speaker of the Assembly.”
Sydney Bulletin: “Nuttall’s clever picture has been faithfully reproduced, and the likenesses of the 350
persons are, in many cases, improved by the reduction process.”
General Opinion of the Historic and Artistic Value of the Work.
Being extracts from an essay on the subject by the late Professor Sutherland, M.A., and the opinions of other
public men
“ THE PICTURE represents an event in the Southern Hemisphere that will live as long as the British
Empire lives.”
“It presentsin the most direct way some idea of the personality of the men of whom everyone has heard,
but most of whom a comparative few have ever seen, thus conveying an object lesson that no amount of
literature could possibly achieve.”
“It represents, incidentally, the advance of Australian art in the person of one of its most gifted exponents.”
“ Every year, as it passes, will enhance its value.”
“ A work of art. An historical record of undying interest to every Australian.”
“ The history of a great country, of its great men, of its greatest political event, reduced to the under-
standing of a child at school."

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Hawkesbury Spring Handicap and attendant
Gold Oup, run Saturday, fell to Bananaland crack Bride-
groom, but only on the post by a short head, and through
sheer luck, for, after Rockleigh had led all the way. his
rider was apparently caught napping just the least little
bit. Nevertheless, the winner was well handled, and
showed good stamina. Bridegroom is a six-year-old son
of King Olaf and Tooloona. He ran unplaced in Tatt’s
Spnng Handicap, a week before. Galloping previous
Thursday morning he didn’t shape much like a probable
wmner ; though, because he was used to canwfng wefgbt
and had to meet an indifferent field, thereweresome
who fancied his chance and took 6 to l. but not with
much spirit. Palmist was favorite, with Circe next in
demand, while Rockleigh went out at 10’s. Last season
Bridegroom lined up 16 times and gathered Q.T.O. Novice

Four-year-old True Blue horse, Blue Spec-
exported westward with Milano and Blenkiron-cairied
Sst. and won the Boulder City Handicap (300 sovs.) 14m
on Aug. 15, with his mouth open, in 2min. 13sec. Owned
in a cute, big betting stable, Blue Spec is now classed as
a dangerous animal in the wild West.

Handicap at; Kalgoorlie, the other day, was a big knock
te rB,whopr e f e r r e d his stable chum, Wise Step.
P?!!? 18s. for each £l—apparently even the
stable didn t fancy him over much. All the same, how-
ever, _ tis reported that the nag’s owner, R. Phillips,
accepted £5OO to £5O from a shop Mo day previous to the
lace, on the off-chance of him scoring. Bivalve, who ran
in and about Sydney for a long time, has won twice in
tour starts since becoming the property of present owner.

* * * *
Ike Foulsham’s good looking horse, Galtee
More got away with a 7 furlong race at Caulfield last
Saturday. For some time past Galtee has been numbered
among the “ likely ” lot, but seemingly his defeats were
more genuine than some people supposed. The man in
the street expected him to win when he was a strong
order. On Saturday the starting price of Foulsham’s
frequent disappointment was 15 to 1, and he led every
yard of the way. So presumably it was another instance
of a trainer not knowing when he had a good thing on
* * » *
Looks as if favorite Famous (whose track-work
for some time has been remarkable) or the better of
trainer Scully’s two mares (Faithful and Marvel Loch)
will close on Epsom, Saturday—that is, unless Purser
elects to do his best, in which event he will prove most
mi n ?^ ous ’, as the con tinent owns few better milers.
That those behind Faithful and Marvel Loch are in doubt
concerning the relative merits of the twain is evidenced
P',' the £ a ct of Scully (some weeks ago) taking £lOOO to
£5O each of the two from one “ book.” Recent form
creates the impression that Marvel Loch is the more bril-
liant and Faithful the better stayer ; then why not
Marvel Loc.h and Faithful for the Epsom and Metrop.
double? First nag backed for the A.J.C. big mile race
was Victorian Florin, who’ll probably start, but latest
display s don t encourage the belief that his prospects are
rosy, though staying powers might pull him through the
Metrop. He closed on V.R.C. Birthday Handicap (Um.)
last June, carrying Bst.—slb. over his Metrop. impost,
but the company will be a lot better this time. Another
(taking a line by his Rawsoh Stakes effort) who threatens
danger m the Epsom (especially if there’s no rain mean-
while) is fast-improving Ibex.
* * • *
Argued that if S.A.J.C. Derby winner, F.J.A.,
had come over straight to Sydney after his success in the
Jenkins State, A.J.C. blue riband would have gone to
him, and fact that he beat Duke of Grafton in Easter
Stakes, at Randwick last Autumn, is mentioned in sup-
port. The Duke wasn’t near the horse then that he is
now and ff he keeps in the same state till Saturday it
will take a better nag than has been so far revealed among
the probable starters to beat him. D. of G.’s trainer,
Tom Payten, hasn’t tasted of the sweets of an A.J.C.
Derby win since Camoola succeeded eleven years ago, and
he has been almost as unfortunate with the V.R.C. event •
three years out of four he trained the second horse!
Cocos scored the last success for him.
