Gradually the record-crank draws nearer to
riding the mile in a minute on a bicycle. But a few
years a go the feat was looked upon as next to impossible,
andContenet s record of 1.16 was regarded as phenomenal.
However it was reduced recently, and the fastest mile
ever ridden now stands to the credit of Caldwell in
2 wbo ’ °PP° se d to Walthour, created record
fioiires for nine miles, when Walthour went ahead and
won the match—ls miles having been put behind in
world’s record time of 17.46 4-5. On the same date and
track, French rider Champion flew over a mile on a motor
bicycle in 58 2-ssecs.—a hair-raising speed.

English cricket team for Australia, as far as
can be gathered from “Yes’’ one day and “No”
another, will consist of four amateurs (Warner,
captain ; R. E. Foster, Spooner and Bosanquet) and
11 professionals (Hirst, Rhodes, Tyldesley, Hav-
ward, Arnold, Braund, Lilley, Strudwick, Knight,
I lelder and Relf), who’ll leave England about end
of present month. Combination is solid though not
altogether brilliant. Foster had an aggregate of 2128
(averaging 50 runs an innings) two years ago, and same
season made two centuries in match Gentlemen v. Players
of England. Last year he only shaped occasionally, and
then 11 innings saw him put 450 together. Bosanquet is
reputed among the most versatile England owns. Rhodes
Hirst and Hayward always looked certainties, and thougn
.Strudwick is hardly a batsman, exceptional powers behind
tb fi tI . C I SS , am P ly justify his inclusion. Braund, Lilley
and Tyldesley are at the moment three of the old coun-
try s best 11. Arnold’s consistency for some time is his
endorsement, and he’ll probably improve during the trip.
Selection of Relf and Fielder surprised some. Former is
wiPtpf r h bat ’j bowler and field - On hard and true
wickets he sends in a medium to fast ball, which swings
a^ ay qmokly at the last moment, and may result in a
catch at wicket or slip. Such men as Shrewsbury, Hay-
ward and Gunn have been bowled by him on perfect
pitches. Fielder is a high-class trundler, to O P both
speedy and lasting. Combination is strong in batting
power without any really great performers, and with
LL° des . Hirst, Arnold, Braund, Relf, Fielder and Bosan-
hAh hea P s and sting in the bowling,
while the fielding should be sure and sharp. 8

By defeating Sydney Grammar School in the
final match, King’s School, Parramatta, annexes the
Great Public Schools Rugby Premiership for the year.
In school football the winners have put up a wonderful
record Since the inauguration of the competition 14
years ago, King’s School has competed 12 times, winning
9, and getting second place on the other occasions—once
being blown out in a final, and, by coming out on top
this year, they complete the third series of three
successive wins. In the last three years 25 matches were
won: 3 drawn > 1 lost). Piling up 456 points to
40 The premiers have now won three shields, and have
a leg-m for Governor Rawson’s trophy. Undoubtedly the
fast and skillful game of the schools is the best from a
spectacular point of view, and, in the final, referee
Pauling, who has filled the bill of umpire in most of the
principal fixtures in the State, found the pace so fast that
he called for a halt for “ wind.” There’s one regrettable
fact about this kind of football, however, and it is the
drastic rule disallowing the “boys” to play in matches
outside the school without a deal of bowing and scraping
for the necessary permit. The State team would be
considerably improved by the filling-up of juniors from
these ranks.
* * • *
“Mac” Smith—playing in the three-quarter
line for King’s School for the season—gave football records
a great shaking-up. In 22 matches he scored 27 tries,
kicked 47 goals from tries, and 4 other goals—altogether
putting up 188 points out of an aggregate of 428. He
gives promise of being a veritable “ sneezer” in that posi-
tion, and N.S.W. Rugby selectors for the State should
note his address.

out young Sydney footballer Wallace Millican,
who figured as three-quarter for crack Newtown team
two successive seasons. Millican, usually unlucky
sustained an injury to the back of the neck, and, blood
poisoning supervening, he succumbed after four days’
illness. While representing Newington College the
deceased had a great reputation and promised to be well
up with the State’s forefronters next year.

The gentle leather-chasing tough again. Ade-
laide-Port Adelaide football rough, Saturday, ended in a
free fight between contending teams, and, several of the
spectators taking a hand, things fairly boiled till blue-
st £-,, authorit l’ got to work witt l h« baton and quelled
the Kilkenny cats. If anything like that occurred at a
boxing match the genus pug and those who believe in
film would experience a parlous time indeed. Which
reminds that, until last few months, sanctified Adelaide
had no boxing matches for years, and what it now gets are
of the milk-and-water, half-hearted order. Does the
parson-prompted charge that boxing brutalises hold good
m the face of fact that no disturbance anything near the
magnitude of the Adelaide football melee has ever
occurred on Sydney or Melbourne grounds ?
* * * *
Concerning a dead athlete: Mons. Victor
(whose surname was Becker) arrived in Melbourne 28
years ago with a big flourish, and, as “ Mons Victor de
Bans, champion middleweight wrestler of the world”
challenged all comers at Grsaco-Roman style for £lOO
a-side. The defiance remained unanswered till Prof. Miller
came over from ’Murka, but as he couldn’t get within a
stone or two of the limit fixed they ’rastled at catch
Victor toured M.L. with Jem Mace and Miller
and there beat a huge lump of French ’rastling material
(one Delamere) who boasted he could put Victor in his
pocket with one hand. Mons. won other matches and
also engaged in boxing, fencing, and sword contests with
some success. He figured in the Franco-Prussian war
and was taken prisoner at Sedan. Breaking* away
subsequently he reached Paris, and, later, was sabred
Daaij about the head, which occurrence might have had
something to do with his suicide. Victor had exceeded
56 years by a few months.

* * * *
Said the booky: “Just before the last race
Tommy the jockey comes up to me and says, ‘Say,
Joe, lend s a bob, I’m near dead for a drink.’ I passed
hipi half-a-dollar. He turns quick-like and says ‘ What
price Mystic, Joe ? ’ (You know —the prad that looks like
a dashed bedstead.) ‘lO to I.’ ‘Right,’ says Tommy,
put me on,’ and he passes me up me own two bob.
Blast me, you know Mystic ; he got home right enough.
Tommy comes back : 1 Gimme eighteen bob, Joe, and
we re quits,’ he says, as calm as a dashed iceberg. I paid
him his eighteen bob and went round to the back of the
stand to have a fit. And the dashed place was occupied.
Mossy Isaacs was there having a fit already Tommy
had served him the same way—and Sol. Aarons was wait-
ing till Mossy would be done using the ground.”

Well-known Sydney sport, Mr. Carlyle (51
years old), for a bet of £5O, undertook, t’other day, to
run round Kensington race-track 10 times (distance, 8f
miles) in 1J hours. He had to run always ; slowing down to
a walk meant losing the wager. Odds of 2to 1 and, later,
6 to 4 were laid against the man, who eventually 7 pulled
through with 3mins. to the good, and never turned a hair
in the effort. Performance was an excellent one all
things considered.

The Dunlop Tyre Co. are issuing a half-tone
supplement (10" x 13"), giving 19 small miniature photo
prints of the winner of the recent Warrnambool to Mel-
bourne road race, and other incidents in connection with
the race. Any reader desirous of obtaining a copy
can do so gratis by forwarding address, together
with a penny stamp for postage, to the Dunlop Tyre Co.,
108 Flinders-st., Melbourne. An early application is
advisable as the issue is limited.
* * * *
“Gumsucker” : For pure sport go to the
country. At Matlock (Vic.) there were 42 entries for the
Ladies* Bracelet. A runner named Jones finished first,
but Mentiplay, the second man, protested violently on
the ground of incorrect performances. Protest upheld,
and Mentiplay to sign a declaration before receiving the
bauble. He signed solemnly and sincerely and started
for Gippsland after leaving his address with the com-
mittee.. That august body received his declaration, but
before it could despatch the bracelet, Jones, who had in
the meantime been investigating, had found out things, and
accused Mentiplay of incorrect performances. The com-
mittee then asked the third man to sign a declaration,
but he, being slightly honest, refused to sign anything,
admitting to certain irregularities and misrepresentations.
Then the committee took something to keep it up, and
called on the fourth and fifth men to sign, and they made
various excuses and admissions. Then the committee
took to drink, and after several months hard thinking
and drinking converted the bracelet into a sweep
amongst all those who entered, and the upshot was that
a man who never started in the race got the bracelet.
The committee is now suffering a severe recovery, but it
feels sort of happy that the dreadful bracelet has been
finally disposed of.

Commenting on Yes-No Reid’s challenge to
fight the coming elections on the fiscal issue,
Premier Barton gets home on the globular clown
thus:
I stated in the debate in reply and upon other
occasions that it was a pity we had to have a tariff fight
at the general elections, but, as Mr. Reid desires one, the
party to which I belong will undertake the contest with
every confidence. Mr. Reid seems to have said that this
time the fight will be final. I hope he means it. But I
cannot forget that at the first elections he told a press
interviewer at Launceston that the fight then would be
final on his part; so that finality seems to exist in his
mind as a matter with some plural significance.
Which is true enough. And what is Reid’s word
on the matter worth, anyway ? He says that if
defeated next election he will finally drop the tariff
question, but so soon as the following election
comes round the Sameolegeorge will try Bis luck
again. Such tries cost him nothing, whilst they
play into the hands of the importing crowd, inso-
far that the very possibility of Reid gaining power
and wrecking the tariff deters many from putting
their capital in some healthy local industry, and
keeps the whole community in a state of unrest.
The only thing which will make Australia feel
really easy is for some constituency to hit the Yes-
No person hard with the Dry-Dog and so free
politics of both at one fell swoop.

THE Mikado was sitting in his palace, listening to the
sweet music discoursed by the household band. As
the dulcet strains soothed his weary brain, his face lost
the worried look it had worn since the commencement of
the war. There entered an orderly, bearing news of a
great victory. “Go forth,” commanded the Oriental
potentate, “ and fill every man’s instrument with gold.”
Hard luck,” murmured the mouth organist, who used
the famous “Crackajack” ; “my instrument is so com-
pactly made that there is no waste space, and it will hold
nothing but music.” A decisive defeat, however, altered
the aspect of affairs, and the sweet, but stern, voice of
cT High Executioner was heard, commanding each
of the band to swallow his instrument. “My luck again,”
quoth the “Crackajack” playermine is the only
instrument in the band that will go down, but sooner
than lose it I will swallow it. Thank the Lord, it’s got
no sharp corners.” _ Crackajack Professional, 40 reeds,
45.; Crackajack Senior, 20 reeds, 2s. 6d ; sent post free.
Allan and Co. Prop. Ltd., 276-278 Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, Sole Agents.

George I., of glorious memory, had a fine
contempt for “Bainting and Boetry,” and the
city fathers of Brisbane evidently say ditto.
They have the gall to charge £50 a year for an ill-
lighted room in their ramshackie town hall,
wherein are hung the pictures of the National
Art Gallery. As a protest against this grocery
spirit, Queensland Parliament knocked off the
amount from this year’s estimates, so no doubt
the Art Gallery will be thrown into the street or
cast into a back room among the dog licenses.
Kingston made a vigorous speech at Gee-
long to a crowded house, the other night. An
extract from the Argus gives an idea of its
strength and the way in which it was received
As Minister of Customs he had striven to do his duty
Some had tried to do him out of duty. (Laughter) They
were caught in the act. They had had a fright which
would be to their moral edification for years He honed
it might finally land them in (A Voice- “Pent-
ridge.”) Mr. Kingston : No. In heaven. (Laughter )
Nothing had such a reforming effect as a little prosecu-
tion—brought up with the drunks. A little interview in
a back office and a pecuniary penalty were not objected
to, but exposure in courts, in company with owners of
unregistered dogs, was dreadful. (Laughter.) He had
been there himself. (Laughter.) More be token the
dog did not belong to him—(laughter)—but he was fined
just the same. (Laughter.) Yes, herded with drunks.
Men celebrating a christening or a wedding, and rolling
home, were brought before the court and lined There
was no backstairs interview with the Minist r. They
were fined 5s. for a solitary lapse from temperance, but
the man who dared, time after time, to evade his just con-
tribution to the pockets of the country through careless-
ness, or incompetence, or worse—a lie sworn to—what of
him? (Applause.) If he had added a new line of policy
a new era of Customs management, it was such as he was
delighted to be connected with. It was not his own
money he had been looking after—it was public money
(Applause.)

When the directors of Gippsland coal
companies decided many months ago to reduce
their men’s wages by 33 per cent., and thus
brought on the long-continued Outtrim strike,
they tearfully declared that their business was
not paying, and they could no longer stand the
strain. Theotherday, when they went round to see
Premier Irvine and protest against the abolition
of their subsidy, their balance-sheets came to
light, and disclosed the fact that they had been
clearing 7 per cent, dividends. If their second
bagful of protestations, that it will be impossible
to carry on unless the State railways subsidise
them by carrying their coal at a loss, are as reli-
able as their earlier excuses to the miners whose
wages were cut down, the State will be justified
in_ treating the tricky coal directors with some-
thing more than suspicion. The disadvantages
of “fiddling with socialism” must, by the way,
be very apparent to avowed individualists like
coal-director Boyd, M.L.A., and other frantic
supporters of the Irvine Government, which is
cutting down the State support of coal companies,
and is trying to screw up its courage to tell pri-
vate enterprise to grub round after the 7 per cent,
dividend entirely on its own. Some of the coal
people roust begin to realise that unrestricted
business enterprise, without any Socialistic foolerv
on the part of the Government, isn’t what they
imagined it.

The fact that, after Gregory Wade had
declared himself a candidate for Willoughby
(N.S.W.), the Yellow Pup bigots adopted him as
their candidate, suggests that there certainly
ought to be some legal process by which a
politician could prevent himself -being publicly
“adopted’’ by undesirable parties, or could
recover heavy damages from objectionable parents.
A candidate is known by the political company
he keeps, and he should be able to choose his
company. A man who was going strong for a
seat might easily find his hopes blighted through
being publicly adopted by hangmen, keepers of
two-up schools, welshers, Mormons, Yellow Pups,
Fenians, importers, dog-stealers, and the like.
Few man have a better knowledge of the
working of the Lands Acts than has Land Agent

The cheekiest thing yet evolved by the
defaulting Victorian farmer was the demand made
on Water Supply Minister Cameron during Show
week by representatives of Wangnratta borough
Council. It seems that when The great Water
Trust Repudiation Act was passed in ’99 this
blessed borough was “forgiven ’ £958 arrears of
interest it owed to the State. It would have
owed more than that, but it had paid £150, and
it was so sorry at having paid that much that it
promptly went £150 in arrears again. And what
these fine manly farmers wanted to explain to
Cameron was that they were sorry they had
paid that original £150 —about one-sixth of what
they had undertaken to pay —and that they
wanted their new £150 liability cancelled to make
up for it. Even the easy-going Cameron nearly fell
down dead.

less efficient.” This from a German co. which

VERTEX CELLULAR UNDERWEAR
■yOU need to take particular care at this time of year
against colds and chills, more especially when throw-
ing off Winter Underclothing for lighter Summer wear,
and the only material which may be relied upon as ar
absolute safeguard from the effects of sudden clime t
changes is jQERTEX CELLULAR.
When one wears underclothing of this fabric, he or he
is safe from “ taking a chill” as, owing to its p; .uliar
structure, it operates to retard a too rapid evaj ..ration
of the body’s natural heat in its insensible vapour—the
cause of the “ chill ” —allowing simply any excess of over-
heated vapour to disperse gradually and imperceptibly.
In its functions it resembles in this respect the true
epidermis and acts, in fact, as a supplementary skin, and
for this reason so many medical men order it for invalids
and delicate children, and recommend it to all their
patients for general wear all the year round.

Chancery of the County Palatine of Lan-
caster Manchester District made in an Action re
Hannah Bagnall de Jong v Chadwick 1902 D 146
the person claiming to be Henry Bagnall Park:a-
son son of one Adkm Parkinson by his marriage
with Ellen Bagnall .a niece of the above named
Hannah Bagnall is by his Solicitor to come in and
prove his claim to be the said Henry Bagnall
Parkinson at the Chambers and by the time below
mentioned. And in the event of the death of the
said Henry Bagnall Parkinson any person or
persons claiming to be his devisee or devisees or
real or personal representative or representatives
and all persons claiming through or under any
such persons are by his or their Solicitors on or
before the 7th day" of December 1003 to come in
and prove his or their claim or claims before the
Registrar of the Manchester District of the said
Court situate at Duchy Chambers No. 4 Clarence
Street Manchester England or in default thereof
the said Henry Bagnall Parkinson will be pre-
sumed to be dead and to have died intestate and
Without leaving any lawful issue him surviving.
Monday the 21st day of December 1903 at 12
.o’clock at noon at the said Chambers is appointed
for hearing and adjudicating upon the said claims.
Any person or persons having any knowledge of
the whereabouts of the said Henry Bagnall Par-
kinson if living or of the date and place of his
death if dead are requested to communicate with
the Plaintiffs’ Solicitors.
The said Henry Bagnall Parkinson went to
Australia in or about the year 1855 and was last
heard of from Mount Pleasant Crooked River
Victoria.
Dated this 21st day of July 1903
HUBERT WINSTANLEY
Registrar.

A Perfect Remedy for Constipation, Derangements of the Stomach, Liver,
and Kidneys, Influenza, Rheumatism, Cout, Lumbago, Uric Acid Troubles.
Read what “The Medical Journal,” Nov.,
1902, says concerning Kutnow’s Powder.
“ Disorders of the liver—whether arising from excessive
or deficient secretion of bile, with such well-known character-
istic symptoms as headache, nausea, pain in the abdomen,
diarrhoea, low spirits, or with the important symptoms of
dyspepsia such as constipation, a feeling of abnormal fulness
or emptiness, heartburn, an offensive taste, foul breath, a
furred tongue, flatulence, irregular heart action, an emaciated
countenance, drowsiness, and languor—these are now most
successfully treated by Kutnow’s Powder. This preparation
is a natural antiseptic aperient. It acts as a solvent, loosen-
ing all hard faecal matter which may have accumulated, and
eliminating the same without any suggestion of griping or
pain. Amongst the many eminent specialists who have com-
mended Kutnow’s Powder in the highest terms we note the
names of Sir Andrew Clark, M.D., Sir Morell Mackenzie,
M.D., and Professor Lawson Tait, M.D. We may add that
Kutnow’s Powder, although containing no sugar, is pleasant
to the taste, decidedly refreshing, and is well sustained by
the most delicate stomach. It produces an immediate sense
of relief, or to put it in the words of a patient, ‘lt seems to
at once take away a load of trouble.’”

A fatal fluency has drawn public attention
to Mr. “Monty ” Arnold, of Sydney, who retired
from N.S.W. public service 13 years ago on the
ground of ill-health with a pension of £192 a year.
The broken-down gentleman started .business in
Sydney and prospered ; and he once stood for
Parliament and got 357 votes. Lately he was
part of a deputation that waited on Premier
Barton, and he also, as chairman of Stockton Coal
Co., delivered a violent address about the socialis-
tic tendencies of the people who paid his pension.
His energy on these occasions has created an
impression that he is fully recovered, and it is
proposed to ask him £q.r,esume his old billet, and
to stop his pension if he doesn’t.

Smith’s Creek tin shares (North Queensland)
have come down with a run and have not yet touched
bottom, but,it Will not be long before they reach that
point ml. Captain Warren’s ambiguous report
on the property some months ago was not properly
interpreted by the principal Adelaide shareholders, who
preferred to exaggerate the favorable lines in the
report and to minimise or ignore the condemnatory or
doubtful portions—the doubts were most carefully ex-
pressed in what the astute captain omitted to state. Now
Warren has again reported on the mine. This time there
is no ambiguity or inferential doubt. Nothing could be
straighter than this: “At the present rate of ex-
traction the whole of the ore in reserve should be taken
out in the next three months.” No wonder that at the
general meeting the chairman had to state that “ Matters
at the mine were far from being as satisfactory as the
directors could wish.” Mr. Warren’s report will probably
mean the closing up of the mine. What will be the sense
in crushing the remainder of the ore reserve, seeing- that
no profit will result from it ?

There’s a screw loose somewhere in Bewick,
Moreing and Co.’s classification of Westralian mining
costs. Theinspireable ” London press, and the press
that cribs but never originates in Australia, are constantly
full of eulogy on this-astonishing firm. They claim to be
reducing costs all round ; but how do they do it ? Take
Oroya Brown Hill, for example—Australian costs only—
exclusive of any robbery that may be effected in London |
Yield for June—Bo4loz., worth £29,833, from 3944 tons.
. , . Costs.
Mining .. .. .. Bs. sd. per ton
Treatment .. .. 17a. 7d.
General Expenses .. 3s. 2d. „
Total .. ~£l9s. 2d. „
Total for 3944 tons, £5752.
This looks all right, and will no doubt be given full notice
in the inspired papers aforesaid. But then we come
to the following other little items : “ Total development
expenditure, £3312,” or 16s. 9Jd. per ton, and ‘‘total
other capital expenditure, £8967,” or £2 ss. sd. per ton.
Thus the total costs for the month amount to £4 11s. sd.
(=loz. ljdwt. fine gold) per ton of ore worth £7 11s. 31d!
The amount available for dividend to shareholders for
that month is £11,765, or £3 per ton treated.
No doubt it Would be claimed that “develop-
ment” and “other capital expenditure” should not be
charged against the tonnage. Development, or a part of
the development, certainly should, as in stoping a certain
amount of development work is rendered useless and
should be written off. If the ore is obtained in develop-
ment, a proportion of the cost of development should be
charged to “ ore extraction.” The same claim would be
put in for “ other capital expenditure,” but as it recurs
so regularly in the monthly reports —owing either to
replacement of defective machinery or to installation of
new—it should be treated as current expenditure and
charged against the tonnage.
Writer thinks the cute Yankees nowin charge
are making a name for themselves by manipulation of
figures. The best managed shows in W.A. are not those
managed by 8., M. and Co., but mines that pay all ex-
penses—including “development expenditure” and “all
other capital expenditure ”—on stuff worth 28s. to 30s.
per ton; like the Lancefield and the Mount Charlotte
lender Henry Nutman. The Lancefield, by the way, was
ujnder option to 8., M. and Co., but they wouldn’t con-
cjude the purchase despite the fact that they show
clearly—by press reports—that they treat the Gwalia
ore at 21s. 2jd. per ton. Bewick, Moreing, and Co. are
good managers of newspapers and jugglers with
figures. Tom Bent was not considered a great success
as manager of the Victorian railways, but no one can say
tjiat he saddled them with a lot of useless plant. Anyone
can see that 8., M. and Co. have done this with the Brown
Hill, and that at a heavy price. Therefore, Tom Bent is
better than Bewick, Moreing, and Co., which is con-
clusive.

Gulden Link’s Eclipse lease (W.A.) continues
to develop splendidly, and a large reserve of oxidised ore
is now opened up. A peculiarity of this lode is that,
PIESSE'S ORCHARD, KATANNING, WEST AUSTRALIA. [ Seepage32.
although it is very wide and strong, it seems to be com-
pletely cut. off before reaching adjoining properties.
Diamond drilling and prospecting have, so far, failed to
find the continuation of the lode in the Kalgoorlie Amal-
gamated. Possibly, if a lot of tributors were allowed to
fossick around, the missing lode would quickly be dis-
covered. The Eclipse mill is to be doubled, as well as the
cyanide plant, but the additions are not expected to be
ready till the end of the year.

Great Boulder Proprietary (W. A.) will probably
resume its place at the head of the list of W.A. gold
producers next year. Manager Hamilton will very likely
remain in London as consulting engineer. It is possible
that 100 head of stamps will be erected, and some of the
present mill and roaster plant fired on the scrap heap.
Recent developments in all parts have been very
encouraging, and the mine could easily yield a larger
output. The main lode at 1750 ft. is 30ft. wide, and worth
28dwt., so there seems to be no falling off either in size or
value as depth is attained. The September div. is only
9d., as against Is. in June, which is hardly pleasing to
optimistic holders. However it will let loose £65,625.
At Poverty Point.
Miss Romer, as she was known on the stage, mother of
Robert Brough and of Miss Brenda Gibson, died recently
on board the Orita on the way to England. Paralysis
was the cause of her decease. It is eight years since Miss
Romer left off being about the finest representative of
the stately elderly countess and the dignified drawing-
room parent that Australia ha 3 seen for many years. In
the palmy days of the Brough co. there was no lady in
Australia who could surpass her as the shocked peeress
in a castle when her daughter had made a mesalliance, or
her son had become entangled with a barmaid ; or as the
lofty moral relative whose son-in-law had been found
under circumstances that required explanation in the
company of a Greek statue which had come to life while
the family was at the theatre.
“ Melb.” : “ This tune goes merrily ” was my Shak-
spearian thought, coming out with the crowd from the
pit of Her Majesty’s, on Saturday night, after seeing “ If
I Were King.” Here was real flesh and blood. I even
fancied that Cuyler Hastings could act Hamlet, and
Atholwood’s Louis XI. was quite a resurrection of
Charles Kean, or suggestion of Irving in one of his best
roles, in a different play. To be sure “If I Were King ”
consists of shreds and patches, but it goes overwhelm-
ingly. There is Don Caesar de Bazan in it, also the
Three Musketeers, likewise Ruy Bias. What a fine hint
of the Dutch painters Rembrandt, Teniers, and Hals, in
the opening scene of the Tavern, with its vagabond
crowd of men and women. How charming the scene of
the Palace Gardens, both by sunlight and moonlight.
You are at some such place as Versailles, St. Cloud, or
Ohenonceaux. Gordon the second is all but up to his
lamented sire as a painter.
George Musgrove has formed a high opinion of Maori-
land as a field for theatrical endeavor. So enamored is
he with the prospects that his representative has made an
offer for the purchase of the site of the Wellington
Skating Rink—a fine corner section in a thickly popu-
lated part of the city— with the intention of constructing
his own theatre. The rink is owned by one Macartby, a
beer potentate. Macarthy is one of the directors of the
Opera House, and is not likely to part with the property.
Besides, he has enough to keep two or three generations
of Macarthys. Musgrove’s “ Sweet Nell ” season in Wel-
lington panned out £220 per night.
The interval between Bland Holt’s first production of
“ The Golden Ladder” in Melbourne and its first revival,
which happened last week, was rather more than 13 years.
Of the original cast three men—J. A. Rosier, W. H. Leake
and Augustus Glover—are certainly dead, and several of
the other people have left the country. Bland and Mrs.
Holt, Albert and Harry Norman, and Walter Baker, seem
to be the only members of the present co. who appeared
in the “ Ladder ”at the Alexandra (now Her Majesty’sl
13 years ago. Baker then played a minor part. Plimmer
and Harry Roberts were also numbered among the pro-
mising juveniles.
“ Adelaide ” : Please, who is Joseph Wilson ? Accord-
ing to a glowing article (with portrait) in London Sketch,
he has produced a play called “ A Test Match ” all
through Australia with immense success, and is booked
•for a return visit to Sydney, to last 10 weeks. S.A. is ex-
pressly mentioned as a scene of his operations.
“ T.D.” : Another instance of the tendency of families
to set up voice monopolies was given by Lalla Miranda’s
sister at a parade of pupils in Melbourne the other night.
This girl, with a taking stage figure, a clever manner,
and young unwearied voice, simply walked away with
Marguerite’s part in the arduous garden scene, her
associates falling far into the ruck.
Dan Barry has been confessing his sins to an Adelaide
reporter. Dan will be 44 next birthday, and says he has
been acrobat, theatrical agent, jeweller, patent medicine
vendor, and other things. He and a friend invented a
new cool drink once, and did well till one day his mate
got the jim-jams or something, and mixed the ingredients
up wrongly, and nearly slew a whole town. Dan
calmed down his advts. for the Adelaide season and billed
his show as “Dan Barry’s Famous Dramatic Organiza-
tion,” which is quite mild.
“ J.F.D.” : “ The quaint old Chow, with the fat front
and the beautiful long silken beard,” mentioned by
“ T.D.” (B. 27/6/’O3) still adorns Paddy’s Markets, but
no showman could get him. He is religious, and views
the stage as a specially baited hook on the devil’s soul
line. If Bland made overtures to him, he would have to
do a faster break than he did when coursing with the bull-
dog. That CKi>w can hold up his end of the stick, too, in
a fight, and when his white beard rages in the forefront
of tbe battle it is good to be elsewhere.
A Melbourne Correspondent writes to say that it is
“amusing to hear people say they don’t like Cuyler
Hastings as Villon on account of his American accent.”
Well, nobody wants to hear Villon talk broken French-
English; still there are limitations. Joseph of Canaan
with a strong cockney accent would also be all right in a
sense, for no one would expect him to talk Yiddish and
to carry a bookmaker’s bag and offer to lay Pharaoh 4 to
I—but again there are limitations.

The S. M. Herald’s latest explanation is
that thousands of people didn’t go to the poll to
vote for Reid in order to show their disgust at

In N.S.W. Assembly, the other night,
“Cocky” Carruthers boldly declared that he
“ would make war on those who were the para-
sites upon labor either in Parliament or out of it.”
“What do you mean?” asked a Minister,
naturally. And then it suddenly dawned upon
“ Cocky ” that, as usual, he meant nothing, and
he paused for about three weeks.

The latest row between Queensland
Justices Real and Griffith is a ridiculous affair on
Griffith’s part. A man was convicted at Gympie,
and his counsel asked the judge to reserve a
point. The judge refused to make a note of the
point. When it came up he said, lelying solely
on his memory, that it wasn’t the same point.
The counsel said it was the same, and be was
backed up by two reporters who heard the case
and took written notes. It was a case of one
man’s vague recollections against three men’s
written notes. Griffith held that because the one
man Was a judge his vague recollections must be
taken without question. The less bumptious and
more sensible Real held that a judge might forget
just like any other man. Nevertheless the
prisoner’s appeal was refused a hearing because
the point now taken was not the point in the
judge’s vague recollections. This kind of law is
calculated to bring Australian Courts into con-
tempt.

“P.M.” on “The Flames of Smithfield ”
(B. 6/8/’03) contributes these remarks :
(1) If the R.C. Church holds the most valuable sites it
has got them, like other churches, by State grant and
purchase. But has it like other denominations put on to
the market land granted for religious purposes ? (2) Re
intolerance. At the Christian Brothers’ schools attend
Jews, Protestants, and even Chinamen. They are never
troubled with religious teaching at all. My son, nominally
Church of England, attends, and I know. (3) As for
Roman Catholics accepting all help and assisting none:
their charitable institutions are open to all without
religious distinction or favor. I am a life governor of St.
Vincent’s Hospital, Melbourne, and I know. (4) Fact is,
shortly, R.C. organisation knocks all other church
organisation kite high. It is simply,its success which
constitutes its sinfulness. Pity other churches are not so
sinfuily successful or successfully sinful.
Lately the ultra-Tory Adelaide Register
announced that Victor S. Clark, who has been
touring Australia on behalf of the U.S. Depart-
ment of Labor, was shocked by the States’ indus-
trial legislation—that the evidence was “strongly
antagonistic to the socialistic movements and
State-interference tactics which are now advo-
cated.” Thereupon Clark, in reply to a letter,
said that he hadn’t come to any such conclusion,
that the Register’s yarn wasn’t true, and that in
Queensland and South Australia, the only States
he had so far investigated, “the Labor laws are
not materially different from those in the United
States. *

One good thing ha* already come of Iceberg
Irvine’s new industrial legislation proposals.
Derham, the biscuit man, who occupies so much
space in the Tory press declaiming against factory
legislation, has been led at last to state exactly
what he and his Chamber of Horrors want.
Hitherto, Derham has been so busy shouting out
what he didn’t want that he had no time to say
quietly what he did want. The other day, he
found the necessary time : “I had hoped to see
tiie whole of the present system of factories law
abolished, and in its stead an Act passed which
would have for its sole object the prevention of
sweating of boys.” This avowal is remarkably
frank for Derham, Hitherto men of his class have
solemnly sworn that they were tearing their
clothes in the rush to stop sweating of all kinds.
Now Derham comes out with a clear statement
that the demand of the Chamber of Horrors is to
be allowed to sweat men and women just as much
as it pleases. And as to protecting tlie “old
and slow worjter,” about whom Derham and co
have been so; mucih grieved, he isn’t even men-
tioned. It will hurt the kindly feelings of Derham
to suggest it, but Mt would certainly appear that,
inasmuch as you can’t sweat a boy to the same
profitable tune as. a man, the employers are
graciously pleased to say they have no objection
to the protection b.f boys.

Governor Ranfurly’s term of office in
Maoriland ends early in 1904, but he has signified
his intention of remaining till May, to see his pet
scheme—the Veterans’ Home, at Auckland—
formally opened. It is much needed, too. Some-
how, army medal-winners generally fail, in after-
life, when storming the baker’s shop for a loaf.
Bent’s day in the Victorian " Railway
Department is over, and, in most cases, the only
reminder of his misrule is the disorganised state ol
the service. But, at Sandringham, the hand of
Thomas is plainly visible. A new station, costing
about £2500, was erected, during Bent’s adminis-
tration, to adorn a fearfully unprofitable branch
line. And now all the vacant allotments of land
in the vicinity are decorated with notices, viz. :
“ Free railway pass. This land for sale on easy
terms. Money lent to build with. Apply,” etc.
Sandringham is in Bent’s electorate.
One Job Vile, who unaccountably ousted
Stevens for Manawatu at last M.L. elections,
asked Premier Seddon, in fine regal style, t’other
day, if he intended reducing the salaries of Minis-
ters and members to their old level. Seddon told
Job that he wasn’t going to do anything of the
sort; screws would be increased rather than
otherwise if. he took it into his head to deal with'
the matter. Personally he was getting £1000 a
year less than many big commercial magnates,
and his idea was that he should get more. It is
well, however, that the cheap legislatorof the Job
Vile sort should be aware of his true value.

GLEAN BLOOD.
THE KIDNEYS ARE THE CLEANSERS.
For a person to enjoy good health it is essential that the blood be kept free from all eontamina.
tion. It must be purified by the kidneys and liver. As the blood flows through the veins it becomes
laden with certain matter which has been used up by the tissues. When the blood, in the course of
its circulation, reaches the kidneys, the kidneys extract this waste matter and cause its removal from
the system. If the kidneys, from weakness or disease, are working feebly, a portion of this waste
matter remains in the blood and becomes actively poisonous. The kidneys of the average individual
filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine in twenty-four hours. In this quantity of
urine should be'dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid, together
with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. These
are all solid substances, but they pass from the body in complete solution if the kidneys are working
efficiently. It is easy to realise-the important functions which the kidneys perform. Unless the solid
substances are removed suffering must ensue. In fact, to the retention of these urinary poisons in the
system nine-tenths of all pain and sickness is due. Sometimes minute crystals of uric acid form
in the blood and are deposited in the joints and tissues of the body, thus causing the acute pain felt
when we have
RHEUMATISM, GOUT,
Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, &c. Sometimes the uric acid crystals, aggregating together
in the kidneys, form Gravel and Stones, and cause Kidney Colic, and Irritation, and
Inflammation of the Bladder. When the kidneys are working perfectly we experience none
of these pains and disorders, as the causative poisons are then duly removed in a natural manner.
WARNER’S SAFE GURE
is an unfailing specific for all disorders of the kidneys and liver. It acts directly on those vital organ
in such a manner as to always restore them to vigorous health. So intimate is the relation between
the functions of the kidneys and those of the liver that it is essential, in order to cure any disorder of
either, that an agent should be employed which possesses an equally curative action upon each. In
other words, when the kidneys are sick the liver is sick also, and the contrary is likewise true. The
supreme power possessed by Warner’s Safe Cure in the treatment of diseases of so, apparently,
diverse nature as Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, Gravel, Stone,
Bladder Troubles, Blood Disorders, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Head-
ache, Aneemia and Debility, is entirely due to its ability to restore to health and robust activity
both the kidneys and the"liver, when all urinary and biliary poisons are duly expelled from the system
as required by nature. The blood is then clean and we cannot suffer, as the cause of suffering is
removed.
The most common symptom of kidney disease is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A surer test is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and
iet it stand until the next morning. If it is then cloudy or there is a brick-dust like sediment, if
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural colour, then the kidneys are not healthy and
Warner’s Safe Cure should be instantly resorted to or Bright's Disease, Diabetes or some less ,
serious but more painful illness will surely result.
Mr. P. Elliott, of 52 Surrey Street, Darlinghurst, N.S.W., says:—
“ Having bien a sufferer for over five years from kidney and bladder complaint, I went 1
to all the principal doctors in Sydney, Melbourne and New Zealand, and they all gave my case up as
hopeless. I got thin and lost my appetite. I kept getting worse every day, and often wished that I
could die. One day I read one of your books, and decided to try the effect of Warner’s Safe Cure in
my own case. I began to feel better after taking the first bottle. My appetite can e back, and I
began to put on flesh. I took six bottles in all, and am now quite well.”

The Road to Hell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Now I had thought the road to Hell
A dread and dreary place,
With spirits fell, to grin and yell
In each man’s tortured face ;
Who fain would slip from spur and whip
To weep a little space.”
“ Nay, he who told thee of this thing
Full foully hath he lied ;
The road is bright and rose-bedight,
The way is fair and wide ;
And Wit and Grace with smiling face
Walk with thee side by side.”
“But I had thought the road to Hell
A crowded path and steep,
Where one must go in gloom and woe,
Nor pause to rest or sleep,
And though he sink have naught to drink
But tears that hot eyes weep.”
“He lied who told thee of this thing !
The road is free as air,
And soft to tread as roses spread
When forth the bride would fare ;
And sweet and fine the bread and wine
White hands shall serve thee there.”
“But I had thought the road to Hell
To be a lonesome wt.y,
Where all alone, as s< ms dull drone,
One languished right and day.
Though others wore the path before,
They could not speak nor stay.”
“ He lied wh • told thee of this thing !
On that fair road and free
With laugh and song shall dance along
A goodly company;
And, all above, thy red-lipped Love
Wall" baud in hand with thee.”
“ But 1 had thought the road to Hell
W„aid echo with men’s sighs,
And through the night would burn the light
Of woman’# tortured eyes,
And rock and plain breathe back the pain
And anguish of old cries.”
“ He lied who told thee of this thing !
On that gay road and bright
The sound of flutes and silver lutes
Shall lure thee and delight,
And suns by day shall pass away
For love-white moons by night.”
“ Though I fear not the road to Hell
Wherein ’tis joy to wend,
How shall it be with one when he
Hath reached the journey’s end,
And out leaps Fear with shriek and leer
His shrinking soul to rend ? ”
“Nay, he who walks upon that road
Doth meet no monster fell;
The clasp and kiss he would not miss
Have bound him as a spell,
And they alone nor sigli nor moan
Who walk the road to Hell.”
N.S.W.
R.B.
Of Ancient Lineage.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Good God ! ” exclaimed Murphy. “ Look at
that.”
But the colt I was riding had already seen “ it,**
and absolutely refused to go nearer. So I dis-
mounted and dragged him on, trembling in every
limb.
Pinned to the ground by the trunk of a huge
tree that had fallen across his thighs lay the body
of a powerful man. Evidently a scalper, for some
yards away, at the foot of a white gum, 1 saw his
sheath-knife and a Winchester. He was naked
down to the waist, and though he could not have
been dead many hours, the ants were at work
already ; had been, perhaps, before he But I
could not bear the sight, and turned away with a
shudder.
Poor chap ! Must have been having a spell,
when the tree came,” resumed Murphy, who had
been surveying the situation critically. “But
what did he want with this here fake at all
at all?” ’
The “ fake” proved to be a rough sort of lasso,
evidently manufactured by the trapped man out
of strips of his shirt; and from the position of
the body, and the direction the sling had fallen in,
the purpose was not difficult to discover. The
prisoner had been trying to possess himself of the
knife or the rifle to put an end to his misery But
the feeble, hampered efforts had been in vain;
and the torn ground about told its tale of lonely
agony, easily decipherable.
Well, well,’ said the imperturbable Murpliv,
after a pause ; “it’s a divil of a country, and no
mistake. But the best thing we can do is to bury
the poor fellow in a decent manner. I’ll dig a bit
of a trench, and you better have a look along that
creek yonder. Maybe you’ll strike his camp
there; it’s the only water for miles around.”
In a business-like' fashion he caught the pack-
horse, and, with his prospecting shovel and pick,
set to work, while I started off for the creek. I
did find the camp, too, but the closest search
revealed nothing more than a bit of tucker and a
few old rags. Not a scrap of writing, or a paper
of any description.
“Did you strike it ? ” asked Murphy, as I slowly
returned. I nodded. And after we had levered
the tree off the mangled remains, and carried them
into the shallow pit that was soon filled up again
with the loose sandy soil, Murphy, as a devout
Catholic, murmured some fragmentary prayers,
and then began to cut the date of burial into the
trunk of the big gum at the head of the grave. I
stood by and moodily watched him. The ghastly
humor of the thing exasperated me—this fearsome
attempt at a joke the bush had perpetrated.
Before my vision arose the scene, vividly. The
helpless victim, in his unbearable torture ; the
sun, the thirst, the loneliness, the ants —and
there, a few yards away, the unfailing panacea
the gleaming barrel that meant delivery, mock-
ingly; utterly b yond reach ! The improvised
lasso, so pathetically inadequate, thrown with
trembling fingers, to snare the black angel hover-
ing gloatingly around ! . . ,
‘‘Find out his name?” asked Murphy, finish-
ing off his work with a very ornamental cross.
“ I know his family name, at any rate, I
answered, evasively. , , .
“ Well, then, put it down underneath the date
here It’s getting late, and we must be off.”
And with a few deep cuts I chiselled into the
soft wood, “ Tantalus.”

VON KOTZE.
The Pig.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The common pig, for downright obstinacy and
contrariness, beats any animal invented up to
date. This assertion may not be original.
There is about the pig a brutal resolve to have
his own way, which stands out conspicuously
above the mild opposition given to man’s com-
mands by other animals. A pig whose wish is
opposed, works himself into a frightful state of
passion. No other animal is such a pronounced
gourmand, and as his brain is only capable of
working by stomach inspiration, he takes par-
ticular care that his meal hour will not pass
unnoticed. A pier whose meal hour has passed
over without the arrival of his slush is a fiend.
He keeps up a squeal of demoniac frenzy which
is earsplitting. If in company with other pigs,
they arrange to squeal in turn, each trying to get
a higher note than the other. Their protests
when assaulted with a fence rail for kicking up a
row are even worse.
My father had a taste for pig-farming, and
utilised his Sundays by tramping to other farms
within a fifteen-mile area to buy swine. I gener-
ally accompanied him, and we drove any pur-
chased pigs home across country. They possessed
the strategy of a De Wet, taking up positions
which were unassailable. They would get into
creeks, waterholes, or prickly scrub, climb up
tr.,es, and drop into hollow logs, and from those
points of vantage heave out sulky grunts at our
vain efforts to dislodge them.
A white boar with one ear that we acquired at
Narellan one Sunday was the most unmanageable
pig I ever met. We started for home about _ 2
p.m., but he entrenched himself on a sandbank in
Curran’s Creek and defied us for four hours. Mick
Curran came down to help us with his dogs, and
he managed to lassoo the foe with a clothes-line,
and we dragged him out. Dad told Curran about
his obstinacy, so Curran proposed that we
should drive him in the opposite direction
to the one which we wanted to take. We did
so, and got him- home at 10 p.m. Only
that he was white we would have lost him in the
darkness. He got even with ua for the trick.
When he found that he had been got home he
refused to eat, and lay down on one side till he
expired. His hide was so thick on the side he
lay on that Dad cut a cricket ball out of it for little
Paddy. Curran’s idea was a brilliant one, but it
cost us £3 15s. If we had got that boar home by
fair means I think he might have forgiven us.
The Germans near our place would catch all
pig’s blood at killing-time, and use it in manu-
facturing black puddings. Mother Bruckhaiser
had a big milk-dish full at our killing one night
—the life-blood of seven pigs. She was fill-
ing the dish from the blood flowing from the
throat of the eighth pig, when Peter Mahoney,
who was holding the pig in his last moments,
released his grip. That pig was on his feet in a
moment, and picking Mother Bruckhaiser out of
the group of spectators, he charged. She wasn’t
a sprinter, and the dish of blood handicapped her,
so he caught her halfway across the paddock,
knocked her down, upset the blood, and then fell
dead on top of her.
I always think the rebellious character of the
pig is the bond that endears him to the Irish
nation. No one ever saw a satisfied pig ; he is
always in a chronic state of rebellion, and will
argue with anything from a rooster to a tramcar.
In fact, the pig is a low, vulgar animal, who takes
an easy first for sheer “cussedness” and obstinacy,
leaving the cow, ram and gander far behind.
JAMES F. DWYER.
Broken Hill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bei oiie you reach the place you are depressed.
You travel by train from Adelaide —a night jour-
ney, always a depressing thing in itself. After
vain efforts to snatch a few minutes’ sleep in an
unccm for table, crowded second-class carriage,
you sit up and watch the ghostly dawn creep over
a ghostly landscape. Treeless, waterless, grass-
less —brown, bare, monotonously level. Unin-
habited by man or beast, so far as you can see.
You look round at your depressed, dishevelled
fellow-passengers, and the insistent suggestion is
that you are all lost souls travelling together to
Gehenna.
On leaving the train the first inhabitant I saw
had his arm in a sling. The next hobbled along
on crutches. Then I met the ambulance on its
way to the hospital with an accident case from
amine. In Argent-street I passed a funeral pro-
cession. The town clock was stopped (its chronic
state, by th 6 way). The scavenger carts always
contrive to be in the main street about 8 a.m.—
sweeping up the gutters and stirring up the smells
just when the train arrives and lands strangers in
the town.
Broken Hill is a great town to do business in—
of a sort. If you want to go speedily to ruin,
start a grocery store. You will do a grand busi-
ness. All the bad marks who can’t get tick else-
where will cheerfully run up bills with you.
Broken Hill people are the most shifty and shift-
ing population in Australia. It is cheaper to shift
than pay the rent. About 10 per cent, have several
names, answering indifferently to either, and run
up bills in each. The people don’t buy firewood
—they steal it from each other. Very few people
fence their garden plots—the neighbors use the
fences for firewood. Standing at your back-door
you can see the back doors of all your neighbors.
You can’t hide your goings-out and comings-in.
The drainage system is perfect. All slops are
emptied on the sandy soil of the back yard. It
soon dries up, and you can’t tell that anything had
been emntied there—except by the smell.
Saturday night is a perfect saturnalia of beer.
It is flowing in ever}' hotel bar. The popular
pubs, are jammed with men hustling to get to the
bars for drink. There are fights going on in
eiery corner, and there is a constant procession
of policemen from the pubs to the lock-up with
drunks. On Sunday morning, the first duty of
every free and enlightened miner is to go to the
police-station and bail out his mate The hotel-
keepers bail out the rest. I know one who bailed
out 17 of his own customers one Sunday morning.

An English Morning.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This morn, in close-drawn winter dress,
I sought a far suburban way
And watched the toilers onward press
To where the smoke-wrapped city lay.
A distant clock with throbbing tongue
Across the icy atmosphere
A chime of six abruptly flung,
And yet no sign of dawn was near.
The sleeping East lay dead and still
Deep cradled in the halls of night ;
And westward, calm, serene, and chill,
A full moon flooded all with light.
No clouds were in the steely sky ;
The stars stood out like lights at sea.
No skirl of wind went wandering by
The mist-enshrouded house or tree.
A silver-featured frost was there,
Mocking numbed feet With grating sound ;
It smote strong plants with white despair
And trailed their limp leaves on the ground.
It touched the roadways, and it laid
Quaint tracings where the stones did meet;
It touched the outer slush, and made
. ,■ . . *
Fantastic figures in the street.
And, of the crowds that westward went,
It chilled the weaker through and through,
And keen with biting discontent
Painted their lips a palsied blue.
And, oh, that sweeping surging throng !
Women whose sex seemed almost dead, ■ ,
Qld men and young, the weak, the strong,
Child ren'Scant-clad and poorly fed :
I watched them, as I walked beside,
Pass westward to the daily yoke,
Till, 'Us the 'moon-lit minutes died,
The city swallowed them in smoke.
Then, homeward turning, lo ! the sun
Up from the East in splendor rolled,
And roofs snow-laden, one by one,
It decked in fancies red and gold.
And well-fed citizens and sleek
Came forth to breathe the bracing air,
And down the radiant roadways seek
The bright sumglories beaming there.

MILKY WHITE.
The Big Tank.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thb Big Tank was an engine which Dan Jefferson
drove on the 1 Short Hill section of the Maoriland
railways. At 6.30 every morning the Elephant
pulled out with the North Express, and the Big
Tank moved from her stall in the engine-shed,
coughing and gasping in the morning mist, and
put her broad chest against the tail of the train.
Then they went roaring up the grade.
The Tank went with the Elephant co the top of
the hill at Thomsontown, where she ran on to a
siding ; and, after the passenger-train had lurched
out of sight, they set the Tank kicking trucks about
the yard. These were for the heavy goods-train,
which left the city an hour later than the express,
in charge of Jane, a low-geared, low-funnelled,
low-looking engine, with a reputation for holding
her load on a down-grade and being the dirtiest
engine on the section. Beside the line, about
halfway up the hill, was a farmhouse, and in it
lived, besides other people, a girl— the girl, so far
as Jefferson was concerned.
When the Elephant blew for the last tunnel,
the Big Tank did so too, though there was no
necessity for it, since she was not in charge of the
train. But the whistle had a peculiar intonation,
which Jefferson’s girl knew.
She usually tried to get a glimpse of Dan
Jefferson through the heavy smoke from the
engines. This was also unnecessary, for when the
Big Tank had finished shunting at the hill-top, she
came crawling backwards, down the hill, and
usually stopped so that her driver might get a
note, or a button-hole of flowers, or something,
from his girl. The fireman was a discreet man,
and always found something to oil on the blind
side of the engine. Probably he had done the
same sort of thing himself before he married.
One morning, after pushing the North Express
•up the hill, the Big Tank proceeded, with the
assistance of a much smaller namesake, to arrange
a string of trucks, for the up-goods. She was
B' ngone towards the main line when Jefferson
a shout, and, looking back, saw the station-
miaster signalling him. The smaller engine had
Ikicked a truck along the main line, and a shunter
Hiad jumped off the foot-brake, and had turned to
meet another truck, but it was plain the first
waggon would not stop before it reached the
down-giade.
Jefferson saw all this, and, giving two short
whistles, he backed the Big Tank and sent her
whirling past the station. Two more shunters
were running vainly after the truck, and the
stationmaster was calling up the station at the
foot of the hill to stop Jane. But Jane was up-
to-date, and some distance up the hill. The last
the panting shunters saw was flie truck, which
was half-full of sand, swerving violently round
the curve on its way to destroy Jane when they
met on the grade, and the Big Tank racing after it.
“Was the coupling-hook up or down ?” the
stationmaster asked.
“Down,” one of the shunters answered. “It
would shake down anyhow,” he added.
“Then he’s got a chance, if he can hit her hard
enough.” . ,
The couplings were of the American single-
buffer, automatic-hook pattern.
Just above the farmhouse there is a place called
the Semicircle—the line sweeps round the head
of a wide gully—and when the Big Tank, with the
truck still racing free in front of her, swung into
the Semicircle, Jefferson blew a long blast on the
whistle. He had bumped the truck twice without
succeeding in coupling on, and, as the grade was
becoming steeper, he began to despair of doing so.
His fireman had volunteered to crawl round on to
the cow catcher and do it by hand, but it was a
dangerous thing to attempt at such a speed. _ A
tank engine has no tender, and it meant climbing
over the tail of the engine. So Jefferson whistled
wildly, hoping that Lavers, the driver of Jane,
.would hear, and reverse that approaching female,
and set her flying downhill to a place of safety.
His girl heard the whistle, and looked up the
line in surprise. She had just heard Jane call,
away down the hill-side. She saw the truck, and,
close behind, the engine. As she looked, Jefferson
sent the Big Tank at the truck in another attempt
to get hold of it, but he only succeeded in making
the truck travel faster.
She was a clever girl. She left her work and
flew down the precipitous hill to the lower side of
the Semicircle. There were fallen trees on the
hill, and she selected one and tried to roll it
towai-ds the line. At first it refused to budge.
Far below she heard Jane blow for a tunnel, and
suddenly the roar of the flying truck and engine
reached her ears. With a final effort she sent the
log on to the line and it fell across the metals.
Then she ran swiftly up the line.
When she came in full view of the Semicircle
the sight made her heart stop beating. The big,
double-bogie waggon was flying madly down hill,
and, swaying and lurching dangerously, the Big
Tank raced after it.
But her coolness returned instantly, and she
stepped into the middle of the line, waving her
arms.
The Big Tank screamed, and the girl leaped
against the bank as the heavy truck swept past.
Jefferson had decided to try and knock the
truck off the line at the bend where the girl had
thrown the log, but at sight of her danger he put
his brakes down, and, as the engine screeched past
her, he leaned out and heard his girl scream :
“ Stop ! stop ! the log ! ”
And the Big Tank came to a stand-still, with a
great groaning of brakes.
_ Jefferson wiped his forehead and moistened his
lips with his tongue.
** What’s up, Loo ?” he asked. His voice shook
a little.
“I rolled a log on the line. I put it there to
stop the truck. Listen ! ”
They heard a bumping sound. It was the
truck floundering about like a blind megatharium
down in the valley hundreds of feet below them,
and disintegrating as it floundered.
“ I’ll go down and see if the track’s all right,”
said the judicious fireman. “ Maybe she ripped
it up ”
The fireman found the line unhurt. He heard
Jane struggling-at a walking pace up the grade,
so he sat down and waited for her. When she
came up he stepped calmly on to the footplate to
tlie surprise of her crew.
“ We’ve come down to pull you up to the
yards, ” he said to Lavers.
Lavers became profane.
“ Where’s your engine ? ”
“ Round that bend.”
“Well, I’m damn glad to hear it,” said Lavers.
“For this old force-pump can’t get any gait on a -
all. Time she was put to pushing perambulators.”
As Jane approached, the Big Tank moved
slowly up the hill till the couplings clashed, and
the fireman, swinging himself into the cab, said :
“Let her go !”
Then she threw her great strength on to t' e
load, and blew proudly because she was in the
lead.
But Jefferson’s girl shut her ears to the sound.
She hated the Big Tank.

WILL LAWSON.
The Pension.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jenkyn Jessop (cetat 70), having battled about in
the Bakblox until he could battle no more, made
application, in a moment of senile temerity, to
the Baratarian authorities for the customary
indigent dole of some few shillings per week, and
then waited three months, during which time the
police ascertained the following facts :
(1) That he had never been in gaol.
(2) That he had never at any time been addicted to
strong drink.
(3) That he hadn’t a solitary relative, from a mother-
in-law to a forty second cousin, on to whom the State
could shelve the responsibility of his maintenance.
After all these matters had been satisfactorily
settled the allowance was finally granted.
The first two instalments arrived duly at
Jessop’s address, Greenvale station. Then they
suddenly failed altogether, and the old man had
to live on credit and casual charity. Some
months of inquiry resulted in the discovery that
the aforesaid allowance had been sent, by official
error, to Green Hills station —200 miles away. As
no one called for it, it was assumed that Jessop
was non-existent and the pension lapsed.
After a wearisome and dreadful amount of
further correspondence, officialdom finally acknow-
ledged its error and, by way of expiation, pro-
pounded to Jenkyn Jessop, by formal epistle, the
following poser —Hoiv had he lived during the
months in which he had received no pension.
Jessop, a methodical old fellow, replied promptly
by sending in his unpaid bread bill to show that
he had lived on the credit and kindness of the
local storekeeper.
Hereupon officialdom scratched its fat Bara-
tarian skull, and, in the genuine spirit of honest
reform and Kyabram economy, wrote back in tlii,
wise:—
Sir, —As you have evidently been able to live for the
last three months, without the assistance of the State
Indigent allowance, the Minister can see no sufficient
reason why you should again be placed on the list of
persons receiving that allowance.
And the Baratarian deficit is getting gradually
lightened by the amount of Jenkyn Jessop’s bread
bill.
P.L.
“ Tom ” Hassall, now rusticating in South
Africa, says “ N.S.W. is a very good place to live
out of,” because it is dominated by the Labor
party. And N.S.W. is certainly a very good
country for Hassall to live out of; also, among all
its politicians, it doesn’t know a better man to
live out of N.S.W. than Hassall. The political
standard of N.S.W. isn’t high, but it rose percep-
tibly when Hassall left, and the increasing
strength of the Labor party has had the blessed
result of making Hassall’s departure out of poli-
tics a permanent affair.
It is practically certain that the system of
aldermanic patronage —the system by which no-
body gets a corporation job unless he is an alder-
man’s friend—doesn’t work well in Sydney. When
a ganger dismisses a man for inefficiency and finds
him coming back to work and taking no notice of
the dismissal, it is awkward; and when a boy,
being urged to greater celerity in his work, tries
to drag the alleged boss away by the coat so that
they may immediately interview an alderman, who
will deal with the boss for being cross with the
alderman’s pet boy, it is ridiculous. About the
most interesting story, so far, however, is that of
the man who was so confident that his alderman
would see him through that he proposed to hit the
ganger with a spade for ordering him about. It
really looks as if every public functionary in
N.S.W., except the Judges and the hangman and
the official botanist, existed chiefly for the pur-
pose of getting some friend or other a pick and
shovel job, and enabling him to tell his overseer
to go to Sheol after he has got it.

TUB BROWN=SEQUARD TREATMENT A SPECIALTY.
Melodramatic justice happened up north
some time ago. A private detective, who was
put on to the labor unions’ tracks in troublous
times to worm out their secrets; and who figured
as a red-hot propagandist, had to be away from
home so much that, he says, his wife got tired, and
disgusted, and found out that he wasn’t the only
pebble on the beach. His real character was
discovered some years ago, and he was expelled
from the union, and wasn’t any more use as a
detective; but the -shameful admissions of the
part he played now come from the man himself in
the Divorce Court.
“L.W.” : Every day the trail of the
blessed Yankee grows wider and wider across the
face of this planet. The latest development of the
disease is a Vegetarian Mission, yrhieh, true to
Yankee instincts and methods, runs in the shape
of a syndicate, and calls itself the “Sanitarium
Health Food Co.” It has a vegetarian cafe in
Pitt-st., Sydney, and has branches in Melbourne,
Wellington (M.L.), Queensland, Perth, Tasmania
and South Australia. It also has a sanatorium at
Wahroonga(N.S. W.). But the co.’s most valuable
possessions are a number of sweet-faced women
who distribute literature and talk pleasantly, in a
dignified manner, of the utter ruin that bull’s
meat and Dago-sold fish bring upon a household.
Their literature is not so dull, either; in fact, for
a religious commercial enterprise it is extremely
bright. It tells you things like this: “ Says
Seneca: ‘Many dishes, many diseases; be not star-
tled by the multitude of diseases —count the cooks.’
Among birds, these that sing are grain-feeders,
while those that eat meat croak. . . . Worry is the
most popular form of suicide.” The new religion
maintains that the kitchen, where the food is
cooked, should be a clean, bright, cheerful apart-
ment ; and lays it down as the first commandment
that the dining-room should be the most cheerful
apartment in the house. It falls heavily on the
pipe, and points out that when the good house-
wife desires to rid her pot plants of insect pests
she merely calls the old man and bids him blow
a few puffs of cigar smoke upon them, and the
pests are dead. Apparently the co. wants to
encourage insects, for it reckons that this handy
insect-destroyer should be abolished. Don’t know
why it is so fond of insects. I recently marched
into the cafe and tried to go through the whole
course of healthy, life-giving foods. It seemed to
me that the taste of the peanut was in everything.
It was like the p.n. that is ever being renewed.
For people who like peanuts it would be heavenly.
I ran through six courses, and finished up with
unfermented wine, and came away with an un-
satisfied feeling, but so puffed out that my feet
were invisible. Talk about “ fulness after meals: ”
It was a perfect revelation of what poor Dry-Dog
Reid feels like. A man that feels blown out like
that can’t be anything else but a Freetrader. It is
the only creed that can make him feel thin again.
